Refuting the Argument that Non-Existence Cannot Be an Effect and Affirming Persistence
Fourth refutation and final affirmation of persistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument (non-existence as an effect) and the author's affirmation that some accidents persist, especially mental ones.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be an effect of an agent, and some accidents are known to persist like bodies.

The opponent's fourth argument is that non-existence cannot be the effect of an agent. The author counters by arguing that non-existence is an event requiring an originator, or alternatively, the agent simply ceases to produce the accident. Then the author states the truth: some accidents (especially mental states) are known to persist, just as bodies persist.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument and refutation
The opponent claims non-existence cannot be an effect. The author gives two responses: first, non-existence is an event that needs a cause; second, if not, the agent merely stops acting.
2
Read the truth statement
The author affirms that the persistence of accidents is generally analogous to bodies, especially accidents of the soul. He cites examples like colors, shapes, sciences, and perceptions.
This directly counters the Ash'ari position that no accident persists.
3
Understand the evidential basis
The author says reliance is not on mere observation, but knowledge of persistence is akin to knowledge of body persistence. This suggests a rational or intuitive certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العرض لو لم يبق ففناؤه أي عدمه عقيب الوجود، إما بنفسه أو غيره من زوال شرط، أو طريان ضد، أو وجود مؤثر، والكل باطل. وأما الحل فيمنع بعض مقدمات بيان إبطال أجزاء المنفصلة، وذلك من وجوه.

الأول: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بنفسه لكان ممتنع الوجود، وإنما يلزم لو اقتضى ذاته العدم مطلقاً، وأما إذا اقتضاه في بعض الأحوال كحال ما بعد البقاء فلا، وذلك كالحركة تقتضي العدم عقيب الوجود، غاية الأمر أن ترجح بعض الأوقات للزوال يفتقر إلى شرط لئلا يلزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام العلة.

والثاني: لا نسلم أنه لو كان زواله بزوال شرط لزم الدور أو التسلسل لجواز أن يكون وجود العرض مشروطاً بوجود أعراض تتجدد في محالها على سبيل التبادل بأن يصير لاحقاً بدلاً عن سابق في الشرطية إلى أن ينتهي تلاحقهما إلى عرض لا يوجد الفاعل له بدلاً، فحينئذ يزول العرض المشروط بهذا الشرط بزوال شرط.

والثالث: لا نسلم أنه لو زال بطريان الضد لزم الدور المحال أو الترجح بلا مرجح.

أما الأول: فلأنه إن أريد بتوقف طريان الضد على زوال الآخر واشتراطه أن تحققه يحتاج إلى تحقق الزوال، والزوال مقدم عليه ولو بالذات ليكون تقدم الطريان عليه بالعلية دورانا لازمو ممنوع، وإن أريد أنه لا يفارقه أن يتحقق بدونه فالاستحالة ممنوعة، وذلك كدخول كل جزء من أجزاء في حيز الآخر وخروج الآخر عنه فإنه لا يتحقق أحدهما بدون الآخر من غير استحالة. نعم يكون للطريان سبق علته، وهو لا ينافي المعية الزمانية على أنه يجوز أن تكون العلة طريان الضد على المجاور، ويكون طريانه على المحل وزوال الباقي عنه معاً بحسب الذات، لا تقدم أحدهما على الآخر أصلاً.

وأما الثاني: فلجواز أن يكون الطارئ أقوى بحسب السبب فيرفع الباقي، ولا يندفع به، وإن تساويا في التضاد.

الرابع: لا نسلم أن العدم لا يصلح أثراً للفاعل، كيف وهو حادث يفتقر إلى محدث، والفاعل مقدم يلزم أن يكون أثره العدم، ولو سلم فنختار أنه ليس بفاعل، بمعنى أنه لا يفعل العرض أي يترك فعله لا بمعنى أن يفعل عدمه.

قال: والحق أن بقاء العرض في الجملة كبقاء الجسم لا سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس وليس التعويل على مجرد المشاهدة إذ الأمثال المتواردة قد تشاهد أمراً مستمراً كالماء المصبوب من الأنبوب. والحق يريد أن امتناع بقاء الأعراض على الإطلاق وإن كان مذهباً للأشاعرة وعليه يبتنى كثير من مطالبهم إلا أن الحق أن العلم ببقاء بعض الأعراض من الألوان والأشكال سيما الأعراض القائمة بالنفس كالعلوم والإدراكات وكثير من الملكات بمنزلة العلم ببقاء بعض الأجسام من غير فرق.
If the accident does not persist, then its annihilation, i.e., its non-existence after existence, is either by itself or by something else: by removal of a condition, or by the advent of an opposite, or by an efficient cause. But all are false. As for the solution, it prevents some premises of the demonstration of the invalidity of the disjunctive parts, and that is from several aspects.

First: We do not grant that if its passing away were by itself, then its existence would be impossible. That would only follow if its essence necessitated non-existence absolutely, but if it necessitated it in some states, like the state after persistence, then not. Such is motion: it necessitates non-existence after existence, except that the preponderance of a certain time for passing away requires a condition so that the effect does not lag behind the complete cause.

Second: We do not grant that if its passing away were by the removal of a condition, then circularity or infinite regress would follow, because it is possible that the existence of the accident is conditioned by the existence of accidents that are renewed in their places on a basis of exchange, such that a later accident becomes a substitute for an earlier one in conditionality until their alternation ends at an accident for which the agent does not create a substitute. Then the accident conditioned by this condition ceases due to the removal of the condition.

Third: We do not grant that if it ceased by the advent of an opposite, then impossible circularity or preponderance without a preponderant would follow.

As for the first [aspect]: If by the dependence of the advent of the opposite on the passing away of the other and its being conditioned that its actualization requires the actualization of the passing away, and the passing away is prior to it even in essence, so that the advent's priority over it by causality is a circularity whose necessary consequence is impossible, it is not granted. And if it is meant that it is inseparable that it occurs without it, then the impossibility is not granted. Such is the entering of each part into the space of the other and the exiting of the other from it, for neither occurs without the other, without impossibility. Indeed, the advent has the priority of its cause, which does not contradict temporal simultaneity, on the basis that it is possible that the cause is the advent of the opposite upon the adjacent [thing], and its advent upon the subject and the passing away of the remaining [accident] from it are simultaneous in essence, with no priority of one over the other at all.

As for the second [aspect]: Because it is possible that the adventitious [opposite] is stronger in terms of the cause, so it removes the remaining [accident] and it is not repelled by it, even if they are equal in opposition.

Fourth: We do not grant that non-existence is not suitable as an effect of the agent. How so? For it is an event and requires an originator, and the agent is a cause; it would necessarily follow that its effect is non-existence. And if it is granted, then we choose that it is not an agent, meaning that it does not produce the accident, i.e., it leaves off producing it, not that it produces its non-existence.

He said: The truth is that persistence of the accident in general is like persistence of the body, especially the accidents that subsist in the soul. And reliance is not on mere observation, for successive examples may be observed as a continuous thing like water poured from a spout. The truth intends that the impossibility of persistence of accidents absolutely, although it is a school of the Ash'aris and many of their claims are built upon it, yet the truth is that knowledge of the persistence of some accidents—colors and shapes, especially accidents subsisting in the soul like sciences, perceptions, and many dispositions—is akin to knowledge of the persistence of some bodies with no difference.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of four arguments that accidents cannot persist (baqa') and an affirmation of the truth that some accidents do persist. The author first presents the possible ways an accident might cease to exist (by itself, by removal of a condition, by an opposite, or by an efficient cause), then denies all of them.

First Objection: If the accident perished by itself, its existence would be impossible. The author replies: Not necessarily; it is like motion, which necessitates non-existence after existence but requires a condition for the preponderance of the time of cessation.

Second Objection: If it perished by removal of a condition, circularity or infinite regress would follow. The author replies: No, because the condition could be a series of alternating accidents that ends at one for which the agent creates no substitute.

Third Objection: If it perished by an opposite, circularity or preponderance without a preponderant would follow. The author gives two answers: (1) The dependence of the opposite's advent on the prior passing away is not a vicious circle—it is like two things that enter each other's space simultaneously. (2) The adventitious opposite might be stronger and thus remove the other, without equality requiring a preponderant.

Fourth Objection: Non-existence cannot be the effect of an agent. The author replies: Non-existence is an event and needs an originator; if the agent causes it, that is fine. If not, the agent simply ceases to produce the accident.

Conclusion (from 'He said'): The truth is that persistence of accidents in general is like persistence of bodies, especially those subsisting in the soul (mental states). One can know the persistence of some accidents (colors, shapes, knowledge, perceptions) just as one knows the persistence of bodies. The Ash'ari position that all accidents are non-persistent is not absolute truth; some are known to persist.

Key Terms
عدم
فاعل
بقاء العرض
أعراض قائمة بالنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that non-existence cannot be an effect of an agent at all.
Correction: The author shows that non-existence can be an effect, either because it is an event needing a cause, or the agent is not acting.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is an example of an accident that persists?
A) Sound
B) Color
C) Motion
D) Time

Key Takeaway
Non-existence can be an effect of an agent, and some accidents (especially mental ones) are known to persist just as bodies persist, contrary to the absolute Ash'ari denial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage asserts that some accidents like knowledge and perceptions persist in the soul as surely as bodies persist. Consider your own knowledge of Allah—does it persist in you as a living, constant reality, or is it fleeting and conditional? If it fades, what does that reveal about your relationship with the One who grants persistence? Will you take a concrete step today to reinforce that knowledge, such as reciting and reflecting on His names?
?
The author argues that non-existence can be caused by an agent ceasing to produce an accident. Think of a spiritual habit you once maintained but have now stopped—did you 'cease to produce' it? What does that decision say about your role as an agent of your own faith? Are you willing to restart that habit today, knowing that your inaction actively brings about its absence?
?
The passage distinguishes between knowledge based on mere observation and knowledge based on rational insight. What do you truly 'know' about your own soul that you cannot see or measure—your intentions, your love for Allah, your sincerity? Are you living in accordance with that inner knowledge, or are you neglecting it? What would change if you treated that knowledge as foundational as the persistence of your own body?

The Universal Properties of Quantity
Taftazani enumerates the three universal properties of quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three universal properties of quantity as defined by Taftazani, especially the acceptance of division in itself.

Core Idea
Quantity has three inherent properties: accepting division, equality/inequality, and containing number.

Taftazani opens the chapter on the universal properties of quantity. He lists three key characteristics that define quantity in itself, beginning with the property of accepting division. This passage sets the foundation for understanding how quantity is conceptualized in Islamic logic and natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the text lists three properties but focuses on the first two.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the full explanation of the first property continues on the next page.
2
Identify the three properties
Find the three properties: acceptance of division, acceptance of equality/inequality, and inclusion of number.
3
Understand the first property
Focus on 'qabul al-qisma li-dhatihi'. Taftazani explains that this division can be imaginary (by supposition) or actual (infikak).
The majority defined quantity by this property.
4
Consider the dispute with al-Imam
Notice that al-Imam (al-Razi) reverses the order of the first two properties and argues that the third is most suitable for definition.
This shows scholarly disagreement about the foundational property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول في الأحكام الكلية (قال : المبحث الأول في أحكامه الكلية منها، أي من خواصه قبول القسمة لذاته، وهما بأن يفرض فيه شيء غير شيء وبه عرفه الجمهور، أو فعلاً بأن ينفك ومنها قبول المساواة واللامساواة، وهي فرع الأولى، وعند الإمام بالعكس، ومنها الاشتمال على العدد. وزعم الإمام أنه الصالح للتعريف إذ المساواة اتفاق في الكم فيدور قبول القسمة مختص بالمتصل فلا ينعكس، وكأنه أخذ القبول منافياً للحصول، ولذا قال إلا إذا أخذ القبول باشتراك الاسم، وأما حمله على أنه أحد القسمة الانفكاكية فغلط بامتناعها في المقدار. بتصريحه والمنفصل من الكم ما لا يكون لأجزائه حد مشترك وهو العدد لا غير، إذ قبول الانقسام للقبول [؟] عرضي. للأحكام الكلية، وللزمان وللمكان فمن الأحكام الكلية بيان خواصها وهي ثلاث: الأولى: قبول القسمة لذاته حتى إن غيره من الأجسام والأعراض إنما تقبل القسمة بواسطته والقسمة تطلق على الوهمية وذلك بأن يفرض فيه شيء
The first chapter on the universal properties. (He said: The first chapter on its universal properties includes, i.e., from its properties: the acceptance of division in itself, and it is by supposing in it a thing other than a thing, and by it the majority defined it; or actually by being separable. And from them: the acceptance of equality and inequality, and it is a branch of the first, and according to al-Imam the opposite. And from them: inclusion of number. Al-Imam claimed that it is suitable for definition because equality is agreement in quantity, so the acceptance of division revolves, specifically for the continuous, so it does not reflect. And it is as if he took acceptance as opposed to attainment, so he said: unless acceptance is taken by homonymy. And as for interpreting it as one of the separable divisions, it is a mistake because of its impossibility in magnitude. By his explicit statement: the discrete quantity is that whose parts have no common boundary, and it is number only, since the acceptance of division for the [؟] is accidental. For the universal properties, and for time and place, so from the universal properties is the explanation of their characteristics, and they are three: The first: acceptance of division in itself, so that other bodies and accidents only accept division through it, and division is applied to the imaginary, and that by supposing in it a thing.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines the universal properties of quantity (al-kamm). It begins by stating that one of its properties is the ability to be divided in itself (qabul al-qisma li-dhatihi). This division can be understood either by supposing one thing within another (a conceptual division) or by actual separation (infikak). The majority of scholars define quantity by this property. The second property is the ability to have equality and inequality (musawa wa la-musawa), which is derived from the first, though al-Imam (likely al-Razi) reverses this order. The third property is the inclusion of number (ishtimal 'ala al-‘adad). Al-Imam claims that this third property is the most suitable for defining quantity, because equality is an agreement in quantity that depends on the first property, which is specific to continuous quantity and does not apply to discrete. He argues that if acceptance of division is taken in its usual sense, it would be opposite to actual existence (husul), so he says it must be taken by homonymy. Interpreting it as one of the separable divisions is a mistake because such division is impossible in magnitude (miqdar). The passage then distinguishes discrete quantity (al-munfasil min al-kamm) as that whose parts share no common boundary, which is number alone, and its acceptance of division is accidental. It then states that the universal properties are three, and begins explaining the first: acceptance of division in itself, through which all bodies and accidents accept division, and division is applied to the imaginary by supposing a thing within another.

Key Terms
قبول القسمة
المساواة
اللامساواة
الاشتمال على العدد
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل
القسمة الوهمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Acceptance of division means only actual physical separation.
Correction: It includes imaginary or conceptual division, not just actual separation. The passage explicitly mentions 'by supposing a thing other than a thing' and 'imaginary division'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which property of quantity does al-Imam consider the most suitable for defining it?
A) Acceptance of division in itself
B) Acceptance of equality and inequality
C) Inclusion of number
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The first and most fundamental property of quantity is its inherent ability to be divided, either conceptually or actually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a precise technical definition like 'acceptance of division in itself', do you test it by thinking of counterexamples, or do you accept it passively? What does your answer reveal about your intellectual commitment to truth?
?
This passage carefully distinguishes between imaginary and actual division. In your own understanding of Islamic theology, is there a concept you hold vaguely where mental abstraction and physical reality blur? Will you seek clarity like the scholars did?

Defining Quantity and Its Second Property
Majority definition of quantity and its second property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the majority defined quantity (al-kamm) and why its second property—accepting equality or inequality—follows from its first property of accepting division.

Core Idea
Quantity is defined by its acceptance of division, which logically leads to the property of equality or inequality.

Before exploring the properties of quantity, the author first establishes its definition according to the majority of scholars. They define quantity as an accident that inherently accepts division, with the understanding that this division is imaginary. From this definition arises its second property: that any quantity can be compared to another quantity and be equal, greater, or lesser. In this passage, the logical connection between these two properties is emphasized, and Imam al-Razi provides further clarification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the majority definition
Read the definition: quantity is an accident that accepts division per se. Note that the division is not physical but imaginary (wahmiyya).
The term 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) means it is not self-subsistent.
2
Identify the second property
Find the statement: 'the second is accepting equality or inequality.' Understand that when one quantity is related to another, it can be equal, more, or less.
3
See the branch relationship
The text says this property is a branch of the first (division). Explain why: having parts allows comparing numbers of parts, leading to equality or inequality.
This shows the logical dependence of the second property on the first.
4
Reflect on Imam al-Razi's comment
Imam al-Razi argues that division actually follows from the first property (accepting equality/inequality). Consider how measurement between bodies requires a magnitude that can be equal or unequal, which implies division.
This is a subtle reversal: the second property implies the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجمهور عرفوا الكم بقبول القسمة فقالوا : هو عرض يقبل القسمة لذاته والمراد الوهمية لما سيجيء الثانية : قبول المساواة أو اللامساواة بمعنى أنه إذا نسب إلى كم آخر فإما ا أن يكون مساوياً له ، أو أزيد أو أنقص، وهذه الخاصة. فرع الأولى لأنه لما اشتمل على أجزاء وهمية أو فعلية لزم عند نسبته إلى كم آخر أن يكون عدد أجزائهما على التساوي، أو على التفاوت. وقال الإمام : إن قبول الانقسام إنما يلزم الكم بسبب الخاصة الأولى، لأنه لما كانت الأجسام يتقدر بعضها بالبعض من غير لزوم المساواة وجب أن يكون فيها ما يقبل المساواة، أو اللامساواة لذاته وهو المقدار، ولا يتصور اللامساواة إلا بأن يشتمل أحدهما على مثل الآخر مع زيادة ، فلزم أن يقبل القسمة ، أي فرض شيء غير شيء.
The majority defined quantity (al-kamm) by its acceptance of division, saying: It is an accident that accepts division per se, and by division is meant imaginary division as will come. The second [property] is the acceptance of equality or inequality, meaning that when it is related to another quantity, it is either equal to it, or more or less, and this property is a branch of the first because when it comprises imaginary or actual parts, it follows upon its relation to another quantity that the number of their parts are either equal or unequal. And the Imam said: Indeed, the acceptance of division necessarily accompanies quantity due to the first property, because since bodies are measured by each other without necessarily being equal, there must be in them something that accepts equality or inequality per se, and that is magnitude (al-miqdar), and inequality cannot be conceived except by one of them comprising the like of the other with an increase, so it necessarily follows that it accepts division, i.e., the positing of one thing distinct from another.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage defines quantity and begins enumerating its properties. The author first gives the majority definition: quantity is an accident that inherently accepts division. He clarifies that the division meant is imaginary, not actual. Then he states the second property: quantity accepts equality or inequality when compared to another quantity. This is a branch of the first property because having parts allows comparison of number of parts. Then he cites Imam al-Razi's comment that the acceptance of division arises from the first property, because measurement between bodies requires something that can be equal or unequal, and that thing is magnitude. Inequality implies one has the same as the other plus an excess, which necessitates division. So the argument is that division is the basis for equality/inequality.

Key Terms
الكم
القسمة
المساواة
اللامساواة
العرض
المقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division that quantity accepts is actual physical division of bodies.
Correction: The text clarifies that the division meant is imaginary (wahmiyya), not actual physical division.

Checkpoint
According to the majority definition, what is quantity (al-kamm)?
A) A substance that accepts division.
B) An accident that accepts division per se.
C) A body that accepts equality.
D) A concept that cannot be defined.

Key Takeaway
Quantity is an accident that inherently accepts (imaginary) division, and this property of division logically entails that quantities can be compared as equal or unequal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you compare yourself to others, you are engaging in the property of equality or inequality. But this lesson reveals that such comparisons depend on a prior division of yourself into parts. What 'parts' have you divided yourself into (e.g., achievements, status, wealth) that fuel these comparisons? Are those divisions real or imaginary?
?
The passage distinguishes between actual and imaginary division. Think of a recent moment when you felt inferior or superior to someone. Did you imagine a division between you and them? How would your feelings change if you recognized that this division is imaginary?
?
Imam al-Razi argues that the acceptance of division follows from the acceptance of equality/inequality. In your own life, do you first perceive yourself as unequal, and then divide your identity into parts to justify that perception? What is one specific comparison you will stop making today, and what imaginary division will you dissolve?

The Third Property of Quantity: Containing a Counter
The author states the third property of quantity: containing a counter, with examples for discrete and continuous quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third property of quantity: that it contains a unit that can count it, and how this applies to discrete and continuous quantities.

Core Idea
Every quantity contains a unit that measures it, actually or potentially.

After discussing the properties of quantity being divisible and having a position, the author now turns to a third property: every quantity contains something that counts it. This property distinguishes quantity from other categories and explains how we measure both discrete numbers and continuous magnitudes. The passage gives clear examples from arithmetic and geometry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice how the author presents the property and then splits it into two cases.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the definition
The third property is that quantity contains something that counts it. Count means determining the quantity by repeatedly subtracting that unit.
The phrase 'بالإسقاط عنه مراراً' means by subtracting from it repeatedly.
3
Distinguish actual vs potential counting
For discrete quantity (like numbers), counting is actual because the units are separate. For continuous quantity (like time or length), counting is potential because the units are only potentially separable.
Examples: four counted by one actually; year counted by months potentially.
4
Reflect on the examples
The author gives a chain: year→months→days→hours, and cubit→handspans→fingers→hairs. Notice the indefinite progression.
5
Check your understanding with the quiz
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you understood the distinction between actual and potential counting.

الثالثة : اشتماله على أمر يعده أي يعينه بالإسقاط عنه مراراً إما بالفعل كما في الكم المنفصل، فإن الأربعة تعد بالواحد أربع مرات وإما بالقوة كما في المتصل، فإن السنة تعد بالشهور، والشهور بالأيام، واليوم بالساعات وكذلك الذراع يعد بالقبضات والقبضة بالأصابع، والأصبع بالشعيرات، والشعيرة بالشعرات.
The third [property]: Its comprising something that counts it, i.e., determines it by repeatedly subtracting from it, either actually as in discrete quantity, for four is counted by one four times, or potentially as in continuous quantity, for the year is counted by months, and months by days, and the day by hours, and likewise the cubit is counted by handspans, and the handspan by fingers, and the finger by hairs, and the hair by hairs.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage enumerates the third property of quantity: it contains a unit that can count it. Counting is done by repeatedly subtracting that unit. For discrete quantities like numbers, counting is actual. For continuous quantities like time or length, counting is potential because the units are only potentially separable. The examples illustrate how larger units are composed of smaller units indefinitely.

The Division of Quantity into Continuous and Discrete
Al-Taftazani divides quantity into continuous and discrete and defines discrete as number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how quantity is divided into continuous and discrete based on whether its supposed parts have a common boundary, and that discrete quantity is essentially number.

Core Idea
Discrete quantity is nothing but number—a collection of units.

Al-Taftazani begins by establishing a fundamental distinction within the category of quantity. He explains that the criterion for separating continuous from discrete quantity is the presence or absence of a common boundary between its parts. This passage clarifies that discrete quantity is strictly number, while other things described as discrete are only so metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion
Read the passage and find the criterion that distinguishes continuous from discrete quantity. Notice the phrase 'حد مشترك' (common boundary).
2
Define discrete quantity
See how the author defines discrete quantity as number alone. Understand his reasoning: number is composed of units essentially.
Other things like speech are not essentially discrete.
3
Contrast with continuous
Contrast the definition of discrete with what continuous will be in the next lesson. Note that this division sets up further subdivisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والمتصل من أحكام الكم انقسامه إلى المتصل والمنفصل، ثم المتصل إلى أقسامه فالكم إما أن يكون لأجزائه المفروضه حد مشترك، أو لا. الثاني : المنفصل وهو العدد لا غير، لأن حقيقته ما يجتمع من الوحدات بالذات ولا معنى للعدد سوى ذلك، وغيره إنما يتصف بذلك لكونه معروضاً للعدد لكون أجزائه معروضاً للوحدة كالقول الذي يوهم أنه كم منفصل على ما يستحق في بحث الحروف والأول المتصل وهو إما أن يكون قار الذات أي مجتمع الأجزاء في الوجود، أو لا، الثاني الزمان والأول المقدار
He said: Among the rulings of quantity is its division into continuous and discrete, then the continuous into its subdivisions. So quantity either has a common boundary for its supposed parts, or it does not. The second (i.e., discrete) is number, nothing else, because its reality is what is composed of units essentially, and there is no meaning for number other than that. Other things are only described as such because they are subjects of number, due to their parts being subjects of unity, like speech which gives the impression that it is discrete quantity, as will be discussed in the section on letters. The first (i.e., continuous) is either self-subsistent, i.e., having its parts coexisting in existence, or not; the second is time, and the first is magnitude.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage defines the primary division of quantity (الكم) into continuous (متصل) and discrete (منفصل). The author states that quantity is either such that its supposed parts share a common boundary (حد مشترك) — which makes it continuous — or they do not — which makes it discrete. He then explains that discrete quantity is essentially number (العدد), whose reality is a collection of units. Other things like speech are called discrete only metaphorically because they are subjects of number. Next, he subdivides continuous quantity: it either has its parts coexisting simultaneously (قار الذات) or not. The non-coexisting is time (الزمان). The coexisting is magnitude (المقدار). This sets the foundation for further subdivision into line, surface, and body.

Key Terms
الكم
متصل
منفصل
عدد
حد مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Discrete quantity includes all countable things like days or words.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that discrete quantity is essentially number, and other things are only metaphorically discrete because they are subjects of number.

Checkpoint
What is the criterion for distinguishing continuous quantity from discrete quantity?
A) Whether the quantity has dimensions.
B) Whether the supposed parts share a common boundary.
C) Whether the quantity is self-subsistent.
D) Whether the quantity is a number.

Key Takeaway
Discrete quantity is essentially number, and the division of quantity hinges on whether its parts share a common boundary.

Subdivisions of Continuous Quantity: Magnitude and Time
Al-Taftazani subdivides continuous quantity into magnitude and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how continuous quantity is subdivided into magnitude (مقدار) and time (زمان) based on whether its parts coexist or not.

Core Idea
Continuous quantity is either static magnitude or flowing time.

Having established the division of quantity into continuous and discrete, al-Taftazani now turns to the subdivisions of continuous quantity. He explains that continuous quantity is either self-subsistent (قار الذات), meaning its parts coexist simultaneously, or it is not. The former is magnitude (line, surface, body), and the latter is time. This lays the groundwork for further discussions in geometry and physics.

Guided Path

2

Understand that magnitude is the self-subsistent continuous quantity, like a line that has all its parts existing together.
3
Define time
See that time is the non-self-subsistent continuous quantity, where parts do not coexist (past, present, future).

Subdivisions of Magnitude: Line, Surface, and Body
Al-Taftazani defines line, surface, and body as the three types of magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three subdivisions of magnitude—line, surface, and body—based on the number of dimensions in which division is possible, and the distinction between the mathematical body (جسم تعليمي) and the natural body (جسم طبيعي).

Core Idea
Magnitude is classified by the number of dimensions it extends: one for line, two for surface, three for body.

Al-Taftazani now moves from the general definition of magnitude to its specific subdivisions. He explains that magnitude can be divided into line, surface, and body based on how many directions it can be divided. This classification is foundational for understanding continuous quantities in geometry and physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the definitions of line, surface, and body. Notice the key terms like 'امتداد' (extension) and 'جهة' (direction).
The passage uses technical language; don't worry if some terms are unfamiliar at first.
2
Identify the three types and their dimensions
The passage defines line as admitting division in one direction, surface in two, and body in three. Note how the author describes the possibility of perpendicular divisions for surface and body.
This is the core classification based on dimensionality.
3
Distinguish between جسم تعليمي and جسم طبيعي
The passage distinguishes between the mathematical body (جسم تعليمي) which is the quantity permeating a substance, and the natural body (جسم طبيعي) which is the substance itself. Understand this key distinction.
This distinction is often misunderstood; the common_misunderstanding field addresses it.
4
Understand continuous quantity
The passage concludes that line, surface, and body are all continuous quantities. Reflect on why they are continuous: they have parts that share a common boundary.
Continuity is a key property of magnitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو إن فقط قبل القسمة في وجهة واحدة فقط فخط، وإن قبلها في جهتين فسطح، وإن قبلها في جهات فجسم تعليمي. فالخط امتداد واحد لا يحتمل إلا تجزئة في جهة والسطح امتداد يحتمل التجزئة في جهة وأمكن أن يعارضها تجزئة أخرى قائمة عليها حتى يمكن فيها فرض بعدين على قوائم، ولا يمكن غير ذلك، والجسم يحتمل التجزئة في ثلاث جهات، وحقيقته كمية ممتدة في الجهات المتناهية بالسطح الواحد المحيط، أو بالسطوح لها باعتبار كل جهة امتداد لازم كما في الفلك، أو غير لازم، بل متغير كما في الشمعة مثلاً بين السطوح الستة للمربع جوهر متحيز هو الجسم الطبيعي وكمية قائمة به سارية فيه هو الجسم التعليمي ويسمى باعتبار كونه حشواً بين السطوح أو جوانب السطوح الواحد المحيط تحتا باعتبار كونه نازلاً من فوق عمقاً، وباعتبار كونه صاعداً من تحت سمكاً. والثلاثة كم متصل.
And it is such that if it admits division in only one direction, it is a line; if it admits division in two directions, it is a surface; if it admits division in three directions, it is a body (جسم تعليمي). The line is a single extension that only admits division in one dimension. The surface is an extension that admits division in one dimension and can be intersected by another division upon it so that two dimensions can be posited perpendicularly, and no more is possible. The body admits division in three dimensions. Its reality is a quantity extended in finite dimensions, either by a single surrounding surface, or by surfaces having a necessary extension in each direction, as in the celestial sphere, or non-necessary but mutable, as in a candle, for example, between the six surfaces of a square—a substance occupying space is natural body (جسم طبيعي), and a quantity subsisting in it and permeating it is the body of instruction (جسم تعليمي), also called filling between surfaces, or sides of a single surrounding surface underneath, considered as descending from above as depth, and ascending from below as thickness. And these three are continuous quantity.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage details the subdivision of magnitude (المقدار) into three types based on the number of dimensions in which division is possible. A line (خط) admits division in only one direction, making it one-dimensional. A surface (سطح) admits division in two perpendicular dimensions, so you can posit length and width. A body (جسم تعليمي) admits division in three dimensions: length, width, and depth. The author then explains the reality of body: it is a quantity extended in finite dimensions, either surrounded by a single surface (like a sphere) or by multiple surfaces. He distinguishes between natural body (جسم طبيعي), which is the substance itself, and the body of instruction (جسم تعليمي), which is the quantity that permeates the natural body. He also uses the example of a candle between the six surfaces of a cube. These three — line, surface, body — are all continuous quantities because their parts share a common boundary: a point for line, a line for surface, a surface for body, as will be explained.

جسم تعليمي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing the mathematical body (جسم تعليمي) with the natural body (جسم طبيعي).
Correction: The mathematical body is the quantity that subsists in the natural body and permeates it. The natural body is the substance itself that occupies space.

Key Takeaway
Magnitude is subdivided into line (1D), surface (2D), and body (3D), with body further distinguished as mathematical quantity vs. natural substance; all are continuous quantities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson distinguishes between the natural body (your physical substance) and the mathematical body (the quantity filling it). When you look in the mirror, do you see only the dimensions and measurements, or do you see the breath of Allah inside? Are you living as if your body is merely a measurable object, or as an entrusted vessel for a purpose beyond measurement? Today, before you move your limbs through the three dimensions, ask: 'Are these extensions serving my Creator?'
?
Consider the celestial sphere: its body has necessary extension in every direction, fixed by Allah's command. Your body, on the other hand, changes—it grows, shrinks, decays. How does reflecting on the permanence of the heavens versus the mutability of your own body humble you? This lesson defines body as quantity. But your existence is not just quantity; it is quality of faith. Are you measuring your deeds as carefully as the dimensions of the earth? Commit now to spend your limited time in a way that extends your good deeds into the next life.

The Common Boundary in Continuous Quantities
Al-Taftazani explains the concept of common boundary (حد مشترك) as the feature that makes quantities continuous, using examples from geometry and time, and contrasting with discrete number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what a common boundary is and how it distinguishes continuous quantities (line, surface, body, time) from discrete quantities like number.

Core Idea
Continuous quantities unite through common boundaries; discrete quantities lack them.

Al-Taftazani begins by explaining how continuous magnitudes are made continuous. In lines, surfaces, bodies, and even time, adjacent parts share a boundary that unifies them. This contrasts sharply with numbers, where division leaves no shared element.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on common boundaries
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author lists each continuous quantity and its shared boundary.
The passage uses parallel structure for line, surface, body, and time.
2
Identify the common boundary for each
For each continuous quantity mentioned, identify what serves as the common boundary: point for line, line for surface, surface for body, and 'now' for time.
These boundaries are dimensionless or of lower dimension.
3
Contrast with discrete number five
See how the number five, when divided into two and three, has no common part. Attempts to force one change the number. This clarifies the distinction.
Discrete quantities do not have overlapping boundaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن الأجزاء المفروضة للخط تتلاقى على نقطة مشتركة، وللسطح على خط مشترك، وللجسم على سطح مشترك، وكذا الزمان إذا اعتبر انقسامه يتوهم فيه شيء هو الآن يكون نهاية للماضي، وبداية للمستقبل بخلاف الخمسة فإنها إذا انقسمت إلى اثنين وثلاثة لم يكن هناك حد مشترك، وإن عين واحد من الخمسة للاشتراك كان الباقي أربعة لا خمسة وإن أخذ واحد خارج صارت الخمسة ستة. قال : ويختص يعني أن الجسم التعليمي يمكن أن يتخيل بشرط أن لا يكون معه غيره حتى إن أصحاب الخلاء جوزوا وجود ذلك في الخارج أيضاً، وأما السطح، والخط فلا يمكن أخذهما كذلك، وإلا لأمكن تخيل السطح بشرط عدم الجسم والخط بشرط عدم السطح، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون للسطح حد من جهة العمق كما له حدان من جهة الطول والعرض، وأن يكون للخط حدان من جهة العرض والعمق، كما له حد من جهة الطول.
Because the supposed parts of a line meet at a common point, and of a surface at a common line, and of a body at a common surface. Similarly, time, if its division is considered, something is imagined in it called the 'now' which is the end of the past and the beginning of the future. Unlike the number five, for if it is divided into two and three, there is no common boundary. If one of the five is specified for sharing, the remaining becomes four, not five. And if one is taken externally, the five become six. He said: 'It is specific,' meaning that the body of instruction (جسم تعليمي) can be imagined on condition that there is nothing else with it, to the extent that the supporters of the void allowed its existence externally as well. But the surface and line cannot be taken like that; otherwise, it would be possible to imagine the surface on condition of the absence of body, and the line on condition of the absence of surface. Then it would follow that the surface has a limit in depth as it has two limits in length and width, and the line has two limits in width and depth as it has one limit in length.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage addresses two important aspects of continuous quantities. First, it explains the common boundary (حد مشترك) that makes a quantity continuous. For a line, the boundary is a point where its supposed parts meet; for a surface, a line; for a body, a surface. Even time has the 'now' (الآن) serving this role between past and future. This contrasts with discrete quantity: for example, the number five divided into two and three has no shared boundary. If you try to share one unit, you change the number. Second, the author discusses a special property (اختصاص) of the body of instruction: it can be imagined in isolation (بشرط لا شيء), i.e., without any other entity accompanying it. This is so plausible that some philosophers (أصحاب الخلاء) posited the existence of a void. However, a surface cannot be imagined without body, nor a line without surface, because they are boundaries. If you tried, you would end up attributing depth to the surface or width and depth to the line, which would change their definitions.

Key Terms
حد مشترك
نقطة
الآن
عدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that time's 'now' is a duration or segment.
Correction: The passage defines 'now' as the end of the past and beginning of the future, which is a dimensionless instant, not a duration.

Checkpoint

A) A point
B) A line
C) A surface
D) A body

Key Takeaway
Continuous quantities are unified by a common boundary shared by adjacent parts, while discrete quantities lack such a boundary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The 'now' is the common boundary between your past and future. In what specific ways are you living as if the past is completely cut off from the present, ignoring how your past choices shape this very moment? What will you do today to acknowledge that continuity?
?
The number five cannot share a part with its division without changing its identity. What is one non-negotiable value or commitment in your life that, if compromised, would change who you are? How will you protect that boundary today?
?
You are a continuous being across time, not a disconnected series of moments. Is there a decision from your past that you are treating as if it has no connection to your present self, thereby losing the common boundary? How can you restore that connection today?

Why Body Can Be Conceived Alone But Line and Surface Cannot
Al-Taftazani explains that body of instruction (جسم تعليمي) can be imagined in isolation, while surface and line cannot, due to their nature as boundaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the 'body of instruction' (جسم تعليمي) is special in being conceivable in isolation, while surface and line depend on other magnitudes.

Core Idea
Line and surface are boundaries of bodies; body alone can stand alone.

After explaining common boundaries, al-Taftazani turns to a special property of the body of instruction: it can be imagined by itself, without any other entity. This leads to the concept of the void. In contrast, surface and line cannot be abstracted from body and surface respectively because they are limits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on body's special property
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the claim that body can be imagined alone (بشرط لا شيء) and the mention of the void.
The passage begins with 'قال : ويختص' referring back to the body.
2
Understand the argument for surface and line
See the reductio ad absurdum: if a surface could exist without a body, it would need a limit in depth, altering its definition. Similarly for line without surface.
This shows surface and line are essentially boundaries.
3
Reflect on the void concept
The passage notes that some philosophers (أصحاب الخلاء) accepted the existence of a void because they considered body conceivable alone.
Al-Taftazani does not endorse the void here; he simply reports the view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن الأجزاء المفروضة للخط تتلاقى على نقطة مشتركة، وللسطح على خط مشترك، وللجسم على سطح مشترك، وكذا الزمان إذا اعتبر انقسامه يتوهم فيه شيء هو الآن يكون نهاية للماضي، وبداية للمستقبل بخلاف الخمسة فإنها إذا انقسمت إلى اثنين وثلاثة لم يكن هناك حد مشترك، وإن عين واحد من الخمسة للاشتراك كان الباقي أربعة لا خمسة وإن أخذ واحد خارج صارت الخمسة ستة. قال : ويختص يعني أن الجسم التعليمي يمكن أن يتخيل بشرط أن لا يكون معه غيره حتى إن أصحاب الخلاء جوزوا وجود ذلك في الخارج أيضاً، وأما السطح، والخط فلا يمكن أخذهما كذلك، وإلا لأمكن تخيل السطح بشرط عدم الجسم والخط بشرط عدم السطح، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون للسطح حد من جهة العمق كما له حدان من جهة الطول والعرض، وأن يكون للخط حدان من جهة العرض والعمق، كما له حد من جهة الطول.
Because the supposed parts of a line meet at a common point, and of a surface at a common line, and of a body at a common surface. Similarly, time, if its division is considered, something is imagined in it called the 'now' which is the end of the past and the beginning of the future. Unlike the number five, for if it is divided into two and three, there is no common boundary. If one of the five is specified for sharing, the remaining becomes four, not five. And if one is taken externally, the five become six. He said: 'It is specific,' meaning that the body of instruction (جسم تعليمي) can be imagined on condition that there is nothing else with it, to the extent that the supporters of the void allowed its existence externally as well. But the surface and line cannot be taken like that; otherwise, it would be possible to imagine the surface on condition of the absence of body, and the line on condition of the absence of surface. Then it would follow that the surface has a limit in depth as it has two limits in length and width, and the line has two limits in width and depth as it has one limit in length.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage addresses two important aspects of continuous quantities. First, it explains the common boundary (حد مشترك) that makes a quantity continuous. For a line, the boundary is a point where its supposed parts meet; for a surface, a line; for a body, a surface. Even time has the 'now' (الآن) serving this role between past and future. This contrasts with discrete quantity: for example, the number five divided into two and three has no shared boundary. If you try to share one unit, you change the number. Second, the author discusses a special property (اختصاص) of the body of instruction: it can be imagined in isolation (بشرط لا شيء), i.e., without any other entity accompanying it. This is so plausible that some philosophers (أصحاب الخلاء) posited the existence of a void. However, a surface cannot be imagined without body, nor a line without surface, because they are boundaries. If you tried, you would end up attributing depth to the surface or width and depth to the line, which would change their definitions.

Key Terms
جسم تعليمي
خلاء
بشرط لا شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a line not be imagined alone (بشرط لا شيء)?
A) It would then have no limits.
B) It would then have limits in width and depth, altering its definition.
C) It would become a point.
D) It would require a void.

Key Takeaway
Body can be conceived in isolation, allowing the concept of void, but surface and line are inherently dependent boundaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever treat a principle or rule (a 'boundary') as if it existed on its own, forgetting that it depends on a deeper reality? How can this lesson remind you to trace boundaries back to their source?

Al-Taftazani shows that line and surface cannot be imagined without body. When you imagine yourself, do you see yourself as an independent 'body' or as a boundary defined by your relationships to others? What might change if you saw your very existence as dependent on another?

Mathematical Body, Surface, and Line as Abstract Quantities
Passage defines mathematical body, surface, line as imagined without condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mathematical body, surface, and line are conceived as abstract quantities without condition, and how they relate to each other in the kalam discourse.

Core Idea
Mathematical abstractions are taken 'without condition' – free from matter.

In this lesson, we delve into the philosophical conception of abstract geometrical entities. The passage explains how mathematicians (and kalam scholars) think of bodies, surfaces, and lines as pure quantities, independent of material existence. This lays the groundwork for understanding discussions about indivisible parts and atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'la bi shart shay' (without condition) which is key.
2
Understand 'without condition'
The phrase 'لا بشرط شيء' means taking something as abstract, without any condition of material existence. All three geometric entities share this property.
This is a standard term in logic and kalam for abstraction.
3
Follow the progression: body → surface → line
The passage shows how imagining three dimensions (body) leads to surface (two dimensions) and then to line (one dimension) by successively ignoring dimensions. Notice the phrase 'ينتهي' (ends with) indicating a conceptual reduction.
This progression is not physical but conceptual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتخيل جسماً لا سطحاً، أو خطاً، هذا خلف (1)، ويشترك الثلاثة في إمكان أخذها لا بشرط شيء، كما إذا تخيلنا مجموع الأبعاد الثلاثة، من غير التفات إلى شيء آخر من المادة وعوارضها كان ذلك المتخيل جسماً تعليمياً وينتهي بالسطح، فإذا تخيلناه من غير التفات إلى غيره كان سطحاً تعليمياً، وينتهي بالخط، فإذا تخيلناه من غير التفات إلى شيء من السطوح وغيرها كان خطا تعليمياً.
What is imagined as a body not a surface, or a line, that is a circle? And the three share in the possibility of taking them without condition (la bi shart shay'), such as if we imagine the sum of the three dimensions, without attending to anything else of matter and its accidents, that imagined thing is a mathematical body; and it ends with the surface, so if we imagine it without attending to anything else, it is a mathematical surface; and it ends with the line, so if we imagine it without attending to anything of surfaces and others, it is a mathematical line.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage defines how mathematical quantities (body, surface, line) are abstractly conceived. It first states that imagining a body as something other than a surface or line is a different category. Then it explains that all three share the characteristic of being taken without any condition (la bi shart shay'), meaning they are abstracted from matter. The example: if we imagine the three dimensions without considering matter, we get a mathematical body. If we imagine only two dimensions (surface) without the third, we get a mathematical surface. If we imagine only one dimension (line) without surfaces, we get a mathematical line. This introduces the notion of abstract geometrical objects in kalam discussions of quantity.

Key Terms

Correction: The passage explicitly states these are conceived 'without condition' and abstracted from matter and its accidents.

Checkpoint

B) It must be taken without condition (la bi shart shay').
C) It must be a surface.
D) It must be a line.

Key Takeaway
Mathematical body, surface, and line are conceived by ignoring matter and focusing on dimensions alone: three dimensions give body, two give surface, one gives line.

Division of Quantity into Intrinsic and Accidental
Classifies quantity into intrinsic and accidental with definitions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main divisions of quantity (intrinsic and accidental) and the criterion of essential divisibility, including the four subcategories of accidental quantity.

Core Idea
Quantity divides into intrinsic (essential divisibility) and accidental (dependent divisibility).

This passage from the Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a fundamental classification in kalam logic: the division of quantity. The author distinguishes between quantity that is divisible by its own essence and quantity that is divisible only through something else, providing clear examples for each. Understanding this classification helps in analyzing attributes and their relation to substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Focus on the author's definitions.
Notice the repeating Arabic terms: الكم, الذاتي, العرضي.
2
Identify the two main categories
Find the two types of quantity: intrinsic (ذاتي) and accidental (عرضي). Note the definitions given for each.
Intrinsic does not accept contrariety or intensity.
3
Understand the criterion
The passage explains that the division is based on whether the acceptance of division is essential (لذاته). If yes, it is intrinsic; otherwise, accidental.
This criterion is key for distinguishing.
4
Examine the subcategories of accidental
Accidental quantity has four subcategories: being a locus, a state, in a locus, or related. Note the examples for each.
Examples: counted object (locus), shape (state), whiteness (in locus), powers (related).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انقسام الكم إلى الذاتي والعرضي (قال) والكم منه ذاتي ولا يقبل التضاد ولا الاشتداد ومنه عرضي وهو المحل للذاتي أو الحال فيه أو في محله أو المتعلق به، كما في اتصاف القوى بالتناهي واللاتناهي، باعتبار آثارها). قال : ومن الكم قد يقال الكم لما يقبل القسمة فيقسم إلى الذاتي والعرضي لأن قبول القسمة إن كان لذاته فذاتي كالعدد، والزمان، والمقدار، وإلا فعرضي بأن يكون محلا للذاتي كالمعدود، والحركة، والجسم، أو حالاً فيه كالشكل أو في محله كبياض الجسم، أو متعلقاً بمحله كالقوى التي تتصف بتناهي الآثار، ولا تناهيها.
Division of quantity into intrinsic and accidental. (He said:) Quantity is of two types: intrinsic (dhati), which does not accept contrariety nor intensity; and accidental ('aradi), which is the locus for the intrinsic, or the state in it, or in its locus, or that which is related to it, as in the description of powers with finitude and infinitude, with respect to their effects. He said: Of quantity it is said that quantity is that which accepts division, so it divides into intrinsic and accidental, because if the acceptance of division is for its essence, then it is intrinsic, like number, time, and magnitude; otherwise it is accidental, either being a locus for the intrinsic like the counted object, motion, and body, or being a state in it like shape, or in its locus like whiteness of body, or related to its locus like powers that are described with finitude or infinitude of effects.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage classifies quantity into two main types: intrinsic (dhati) and accidental ('aradi). Intrinsic quantity does not accept contrariety or intensity. Accidental quantity is defined as something that is the locus for intrinsic quantity, or a state in it, or in its locus, or related to its locus. Examples are given for each subcategory. The criterion for the division is whether the acceptance of division is essential: if yes, it is intrinsic (e.g., number, time, magnitude); if not, it is accidental, with four subcategories: being a locus (like counted object, motion, body), being a state in it (like shape), being in its locus (like whiteness), or being related to its locus (like powers finite/infinite in effects). This is a standard logical classification in kalam discussions of quantity.

الذاتي
العرضي
القسمة
العدد
الزمان
المقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidental quantity is always a property.
Correction: The passage shows accidental quantity can be a locus, a state, or related to a locus.

Checkpoint
What is the criterion for distinguishing intrinsic quantity from accidental quantity?
A) Whether it accepts contrariety.
B) Whether it accepts division essentially.
C) Whether it is a locus.
D) Whether it is perceived.

Key Takeaway
Quantity is classified based on whether division is essential to its nature; intrinsic quantity is divisible by itself, while accidental quantity is divisible through something else.

?
The passage says intrinsic quantity does not accept contrariety or intensity. Consider your faith: do you see it as an intrinsic quality that cannot be mixed with hypocrisy or intensified by mere actions, or do you treat it as accidental, fluctuating with circumstances? What would change if you truly believed your iman is an unchangeable essence?
?
Accidental quantity includes being a locus, state, or relation. Reflect on your role in your family or community: are you the locus (the body), the state (the shape), or the relation (the effect)? Which status do you most identify with, and does that alignment bring you closer to recognizing your true, inextricable relationship with your Creator?

Intrinsic Quantity Does Not Accept Intensity or Contrariety
Explains why intrinsic quantity lacks intensity/weakness and contrariety.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intrinsic quantity (al-kamm bi al-dhat) does not accept intensity or weakness, nor does it admit contrariety, and how this differs from qualitative change.

Core Idea
Quantity grows by addition, not by intensification.

In this passage, the author clarifies a key distinction between quantitative and qualitative change. While qualities like colour admit intensity, numbers and magnitudes only increase by adding parts. Similarly, contrariety applies only where opposites can coexist in the same subject, which is impossible for numbers and magnitudes. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Pay attention to the terms shidda and du'f.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage first argues that quantity does not accept intensity/weakness, then that it does not accept contrariety. Find the reasoning for each.
Notice the examples: line increase vs. blackness intensification.
3
Reflect on the key distinctions
Consider why increase is not intensity, and why numbers and magnitudes cannot be contraries. Use the commentary to help.
Think about the difference between additive change and qualitative change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكم بالذات لا يقبل الشدة والضعف، إذ لا يعقل عدد أو مقدار أشد في العددية أو المقدارية، وإنما يقبل الزيادة والنقصان والكثرة، والقلة والفرق بينهما أن تعقل كل من الزيادة والنقصان لا يكون إلا بالقياس إلى تعقل الآخر بخلاف الكثرة والقلة، والفرق بينهما الاشتداد أن العدد إذا كثر والخط إذا زاد أمكن أن يشار فيه إلى مثل ما الزيادة، بأن يقال: هذا هو الأصل، وهذا هو الزائد بخلاف ما إذا اشتد السواد، وأيضاً الكم بالذات لا يقبل التضاد، أما العدد فلأن بعضه داخل في البعض ولا يتصور بين عددين غاية الخلاف ولا اتحاد الموضوع، وأما المقدار فلأنه لا يعقل بين مقدارين غاية الخلاف ولا اتحاد الموضوع، ولأن كلا منهما قابل للآخر أو مقبول له.
Intrinsic quantity does not accept intensity and weakness, for it is not conceivable that a number or magnitude be more intense in numberhood or magnitudehood. Rather, it accepts increase and decrease, and abundance and scarcity. The difference between them is that the conception of each of increase and decrease is only by comparison to the conception of the other, unlike abundance and scarcity. And the difference between them (increase/decrease) and intensity is that when number increases and line increases, it is possible to point to something like the increase, by saying: this is the original, and this is the increased, unlike when blackness becomes more intense. Also, intrinsic quantity does not accept contrariety. As for number, because part of it enters into part, and it is not conceivable between two numbers extreme opposition nor unity of subject. As for magnitude, because it is not conceivable between two magnitudes extreme opposition nor unity of subject, and because each of them is receptive of the other or received by it.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage demonstrates two properties of intrinsic quantity: (i) it does not accept intensity (shidda) or weakness (du'f), and (ii) it does not accept contrariety (tadadd). First, numbers and magnitudes cannot be 'more intense' in their essence; they only increase or decrease by addition or subtraction of parts. The difference between increase and intensity is illustrated: when a line increases, we can compare the original and the added part; when blackness intensifies, there are no discrete parts. Second, intrinsic quantity does not admit contrary opposites because numbers are inclusive (e.g., 3 contains 2) and magnitudes can be superimposed; thus no extreme opposition or unity of subject is possible between two numbers or two magnitudes. This guards against thinking quantity behaves like qualities.

Key Terms
الشدة
الضعف
الزيادة
النقصان
الكثرة
القلة
التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Increase and intensity are the same.
Correction: Increase is additive in quantity; intensity is qualitative and does not involve discrete parts.

Checkpoint
Why does intrinsic quantity not accept intensity?
A) Because it is not measurable.
B) Because it only increases by addition of parts, not by qualitative intensification.
C) Because intensity requires matter.
D) Because it is abstract.

Key Takeaway
Intrinsic quantity changes by addition or subtraction of parts, never by internal intensification, and it cannot have contraries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Can you identify one area of your life where you have been seeking 'more' (increase) but neglecting 'better' (intensity)? What specific steps will you take this week to develop intensity in that area?

No Contradiction Between Intrinsic and Accidental Quantity
Demonstrates compatibility of intrinsic and accidental quantity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how one and the same thing can be both intrinsic and accidental quantity, and why there is no contradiction between these categories.

Core Idea
Intrinsic and accidental classifications can coexist in one entity without contradiction.

This passage addresses a potential confusion in the classification of quantities: can something be both intrinsic and accidental? The author demonstrates with time and motion that these categories are not mutually exclusive but apply from different aspects. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this compatibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a general claim followed by examples.
The passage ends abruptly; this is a transmission artifact.
2
Understand the claim 'no contradiction'
The author states there is no contradiction (la tanāfī) between intrinsic and accidental quantity. Ask yourself: how can something be both?
The key is that these categories apply from different aspects.
3
Analyze the example of time
Time is intrinsic quantity as continuous non-permanent (muttasil ghayr qar), but accidental as discontinuous permanent (munfaṣil qār) when superimposed on motion and distance. See how one entity takes two classifications.
4
Analyze the example of motion
Motion is accidental quantity in multiple ways: as a state in a body (hence divisible), as superimposed on distance (varying in abundance), and as superimposed on time (varying in speed). This shows multiple accidental aspects.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this principle applies to other quantities. The key is recognizing that 'intrinsic' and 'accidental' are not exclusive but complementary perspectives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ولا تنافي بين الذاتي والعرضي، فإن الزمان غير قار بالذات، ومقدار للحركة المنطبقة على المسافة ولا بين كل قسمين من العرضي فأن الحركة يعرضها التجزي والتفاوت لقيامها بالمتجزي، والتفاوت قلة وكثرة لانطباقها على المسافة وسرعة وبطء، لانطباقها على الزمان وقد يعرض المنفصل للمتصل كساعات النهار، وقبضات الذارع). قال : ولا تنافي، يعني أن الشيء الواحد قد يكون كماً بالذات وكماً بالعرض كالزمان فإنه بالذات كم متصل غير قار، وبالعرض كم منفصل، قار، لانطباقه على الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة، التي هي مقدار وأيضاً قد يكون الشيء الواحد كما بالعرض على وجهين، أو أكثر من وجوه العرضية كالحركة فإنها كم بالعرض من جهة كونها حالة في محل للكم أعني الجسم المتحرك ولهذا يقبل التجزيء، فإن الحركة القائمة بنصف المتحرك نصف الحركة القائمة بالكل ومن جهة كونها منطبقة على الكم المتصل المسافة ولهذا تتفاوت قلة وكثرة فإن الحركة إلى نصف المسافة أقل من الحركة إلى منتهاها ومن جهة كونها منطبقة.
He said: There is no contradiction between intrinsic and accidental; for time is non-permanent (ghayr qar) by essence, and is a measure of motion superimposed on distance; and not between each two divisions of accidental, for motion is subject to partitioning and variation due to its being subsistent in what is subject to partitioning, and variation in scarcity and abundance due to its superimposition on distance, and speed and slowness due to its superimposition on time. And accidental discontinuity (munfasil) may be superimposed on continuous (muttasil), like hours of the day and fist-measures of the cubit. He said: There is no contradiction, meaning that one and the same thing may be intrinsic quantity and accidental quantity, like time: by essence it is continuous non-permanent quantity, and by accident it is discontinuous permanent quantity, due to its superimposition on motion superimposed on distance, which is magnitude. Also, one and the same thing may be accidental quantity in two or more aspects, like motion: it is accidental quantity from the aspect that it is a state in a locus of quantity, namely the moving body, hence it accepts partitioning, for the motion subsisting in half of the moving object is half the motion subsisting in the whole; and from the aspect that it is superimposed on continuous quantity, the distance, hence it varies in scarcity and abundance, for motion to half the distance is less than motion to its end; and from the aspect that it is superimposed on time...
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage addresses the compatibility between intrinsic and accidental quantity. It begins by stating there is no contradiction (la tanafi) between them, using time as an example: time is intrinsically continuous non-permanent quantity (muttasil ghayr qar) but accidentally it can be considered discontinuous permanent quantity (munfasil qar) when superimposed on motion and distance. Then it explains that accidental quantity itself can have multiple aspects, using motion as an example: motion is accidental quantity because it is a state in a body (a locus of quantity), hence it is divisible; it is also superimposed on distance, causing variation in scarcity/abundance; and it is superimposed on time, causing speed/slowness. This illustrates that one thing can be accidental quantity in multiple ways. The passage emphasizes that there is no logical conflict between these categorizations.

Key Terms
تنافي
ذاتي
عرضي
الزمان
متصل
منفصل
قار
الحركة
الانطباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intrinsic and accidental are mutually exclusive; something cannot be both.
Correction: The passage shows that one and the same thing can be intrinsic quantity from one aspect and accidental from another, as exemplified by time and motion.

Checkpoint
How does the passage show that time is both intrinsic and accidental quantity?
A) Time is permanent by essence.
B) By essence it is continuous non-permanent, by accident it is discontinuous permanent.
C) Time is neither intrinsic nor accidental.
D) Time is only accidental.

Key Takeaway
A single entity can be both intrinsic and accidental quantity when considered from different aspects; there is no contradiction between these classifications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Where in your life have you been too rigid in labeling something (a person, a situation, even yourself) as only one thing, missing the nuance? What would it take for you to see that other aspect today and soften your judgment?
?
The author says 'there is no contradiction between intrinsic and accidental.' Consider your own faith: are there beliefs you hold that seem to conflict (like Allah's mercy and justice, or divine predestination and human responsibility)? How might these be like time — the same reality seen from different essential and accidental perspectives? What is one contradictory-feeling truth you will approach with more openness this week, trusting that there is no real contradiction?
?
Time, by essence, is non-permanent and continuous, but by accident it is permanent and discontinuous. Which aspects of your character do you consider 'by essence' — who you 'really are' — and which do you consider accidental (circumstantial)? Have you mistaken an accidental habit for an essential trait, or vice versa? Write down one quality you thought was fixed but may actually be accidental, and one quality you dismissed as accidental that may be essential to your purpose. Then take one action today to align with the essential.

Distinction between intensity and multitude in essential quantity
Distinguishes intensity from multitude and proves quantity does not admit contrariety.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between intensity and multitude within the category of essential quantity and why quantity does not admit contrariety.

Core Idea
Intensity is relative; multitude is additive; quantity has no opposites.

This passage from the Maqasid clarifies a subtle distinction between two types of change in quantity—intensity (like becoming blacker) and multitude (like adding numbers). It then establishes a key principle: essential quantity does not admit contrariety, unlike qualities. Understanding this distinction is foundational for metaphysics and natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the examples given.
The passage uses a compressed style; take time to parse each clause.
2
Distinguish intensity from multitude
Note the key contrast: intensity (ishtidad) is relative to another degree, while multitude/fewness (kathra/qilla) are not. The example of blackness vs. number illustrates this.
Intensity cannot be isolated into an 'original' and 'increase' like a number line.
3
Understand the proof for no contrariety
The passage argues that essential quantity does not admit contrariety. For number: parts are inclusive and no extreme opposition or unity of subject. For magnitude: similarly, no extreme opposition and each is receptive to the other.
Contrariety requires a common subject and maximal difference—quantity lacks both.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: it shows that quantity is a distinct category from quality, with its own rules. This distinction prevents confusion in metaphysical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبين كان لا يكون إلا بالقياس إلى تعقل الآخر بخلاف الكثرة والقلة، والفرق بينهما الاشتداد أن العدد إذا كثر والخط إذا زاد أمكن أن يشار فيه إلى مثل ما الزيادة، بأن يقال: هذا هو الأصل، وهذا هو الزائد بخلاف ما إذا اشتد السواد، وأيضاً الكم بالذات لا يقبل التضاد، أما العدد فلأن بعضه داخل في البعض ولا يتصور بين عددين غاية الخلاف ولا اتحاد الموضوع، وأما المقدار فلأنه لا يعقل بين مقدارين غاية الخلاف ولا اتحاد الموضوع، ولأن كلا منهما قابل للآخر أو مقبول له
And between [something] is only with reference to the intellect of the other, contrary to multitude and fewness. The difference between them is intensity: when number increases and the line increases, it is possible to point to the increase, saying 'this is the original, this is the increased,' contrary to when blackness intensifies. Also, essential quantity does not accept contrariety. As for number, because part of it enters into part, and between two numbers neither extreme opposition nor unity of subject is conceivable. As for magnitude, because between two magnitudes neither extreme opposition nor unity of subject is conceivable, and because each of them is receptive to the other or recipient of it.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage begins by distinguishing between intensity (ishtidad) and multitude/ fewness (kathra/qilla). It states that intensity is only conceivable in relation to another degree, whereas multitude and fewness do not require such a relation. The difference is illustrated: if a number increases or a line extends, one can point to the original and the added part, but if blackness intensifies, one cannot isolate the increase. Then it argues that essential quantity does not admit contrariety (taddad), unlike qualities (e.g., black and white). The proof: for number, each number contains parts within parts, and two numbers cannot exhibit extreme opposition (like heat and cold) nor share the same subject. Similarly for magnitude. Thus, quantity per se is not subject to opposition.

Key Terms
الكم بالذات
التضاد
العدد
المقدار
الاشتداد
الكثرة والقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That quantity can have opposites like qualities do (e.g., large vs. small as opposites).
Correction: Quantity does not admit contrariety because neither numbers nor magnitudes can be extreme opposites nor share a common subject as opposites do.

Checkpoint
Why does essential quantity not admit contrariety according to this passage?
A) Because quantity is always continuous
B) Because between two numbers there is no extreme opposition or unity of subject
C) Because quantity is accidental
D) Because quantity is divisible

Key Takeaway
Essential quantity does not admit contrariety because neither numbers nor magnitudes can exhibit extreme opposition or share a subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate your faith (īmān), do you treat it as a quantity to be accumulated (more prayers, more charity) or as a quality that intensifies internally? Which approach does this passage suggest is more appropriate for qualities like belief, and why might it matter for your relationship with Allah?
?

?
Consider a time you felt your faith 'increase'—was it because you added more actions or because your belief itself deepened and intensified? Based on the distinction in this lesson, what concrete measure can you take today to seek authentic intensity (ishtidad) rather than mere multitude (kathra) in your devotion?
