One Thing as Essential and Accidental Quantity
Sanusi demonstrates that time is both essential and accidental quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single entity—like time—can be both essential and accidental quantity without contradiction.

Core Idea
One thing can be quantity in two ways at once.

Sanusi begins by asserting there is no conflict between essential and accidental quantity. He then illustrates with time: essentially it is continuous and non-stable, but accidentally it can be discrete and stable when measured against motion. This lesson unpacks that principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author presents the principle and then the example.
The passage includes both the matn and its commentary.
2
Identify the two respects for time
Find where the passage describes time as essential quantity and as accidental quantity. Note the terms 'متصل غير قار' and 'منفصل قار'.
Essential: continuous non-stable. Accidental: discrete stable.
3
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself why there is no contradiction. Because being essential and accidental are different respects of consideration, not mutually exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ولا تنافي بين الذاتي والعرضي، فإن الزمان غير قار بالذات، ومقدار للحركة المنطبقة على المسافة ولا بين كل قسمين من العرضي فأن الحركة يعرضها التجزي والتفاوت لقيامها بالمتجزي، والتفاوت قلة وكثرة لانطباقها على المسافة وسرعة وبطء، لانطباقها على الزمان وقد يعرض المنفصل للمتصل كساعات النهار، وقبضات الذارع). قال : ولا تنافي، يعني أن الشيء الواحد قد يكون كماً بالذات وكماً بالعرض كالزمان فإنه بالذات كم متصل غير قار، وبالعرض كم منفصل قار، لانطباقه على الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة، التي هي مقدار وأيضاً قد يكون الشيء الواحد كماً بالعرض على وجهين، أو أكثر من وجوه العرضية كالحركة فإنها كم بالعرض من جهة كونها حالة في محل للكم أعني الجسم المتحرك ولهذا تقبل التجزيء، فإن الحركة القائمة بنصف المتحرك نصف الحركة القائمة بالكل ومن جهة كونها منطبقة على الكم المتصل المسافة ولهذا تتفاوت قلة وكثرة فإن الحركة إلى نصف المسافة أقل من الحركة إلى منتهاها ومن جهة كونها منطبقة على الزمان الذي هو كم متصل غير قار ولهذا يتفاوت بالسرعة والبطء، فإن قطع المسافة المعينة في زمان أسرع منه في زمانين وقد يعرض الكم المنفصل للكم المتصل الغير القار، أو القار كما يقال هذا اليوم عشر ساعات، وهذا الذراع ست قبضات.
He said: And there is no contradiction between essential and accidental quantity. For time is non-stable in essence, and a measure of motion superimposed on distance. And between each of the two divisions of accidental quantity, motion is subject to division and variation because it inheres in a divisible subject. Variation is in fewness and multitude due to superimposition on distance, and in speed and slowness due to superimposition on time. And discrete may accidentally accrue to continuous, like hours of the day and hands-breadths of the cubit. He said: And there is no contradiction, meaning that one thing may be quantity essentially and quantity accidentally, like time: it is essentially continuous non-stable quantity, and accidentally discrete stable quantity, because it superimposes on motion superimposed on distance, which is magnitude. Also, one thing may be quantity accidentally in two ways, or more, like motion: it is accidentally quantity because it inheres in a locus of quantity, i.e., the moving body, and hence accepts division, for the motion in half the moving object is half the motion in the whole; and because it superimposes on continuous quantity, i.e., distance, and hence varies in fewness and multitude, for motion to half the distance is less than motion to its end; and because it superimposes on time, which is continuous non-stable quantity, and hence varies in speed and slowness, for traversing a given distance in one time is faster than in two times. And discrete may accidentally occur to continuous non-stable or stable, as is said 'this day is ten hours' and 'this cubit is six hands-breadths.'
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage consists of a citation from the matn and its commentary. The matn asserts no contradiction between essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi) quantity, illustrating with time as non-stable in essence and a measure of motion. It then notes that motion is subject to division (tajazzi) and variation (tafawut) due to its divisible subject, and variation occurs in multitude/fewness (from distance) and speed/slowness (from time). Discrete accidentally attaches to continuous, like hours to a day. The commentary explains that one thing can be both essential and accidental quantity: time is essentially continuous non-stable, accidentally discrete stable. Moreover, one thing can be accidental quantity in multiple ways: motion is accidental quantity by inhering in a locus of quantity (the moving body) – hence divisible – and by superimposing on distance (hence varying in fewness/multitude) and on time (hence varying in speed/slowness). Finally, discrete quantity may accidentally attach to continuous, as when we speak of 'ten hours' or 'six hands-breadths'.

الزمان
الكم المتصل
الكم المنفصل

Misconception: A thing cannot be both essential and accidental quantity.
Correction: The passage shows that time is both: essentially continuous non-stable, accidentally discrete stable.

Key Takeaway
Time exemplifies how one entity can be both essential and accidental quantity because these are different respects of consideration.

Motion as Accidental Quantity in Three Ways
Sanusi explains the multiple accidental aspects of motion and the relationship between discrete and continuous quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motion is accidental quantity in three respects (inherence, distance, time) and how discrete quantity attaches to continuous.

Core Idea
Motion is accidental quantity by inherence, distance, and time.

Having established that time is both essential and accidental, Sanusi now examines motion. He explains that motion is accidental quantity in multiple respects: because it inheres in a divisible body, because it superimposes on distance, and because it superimposes on time. Finally, he notes how discrete quantity can be applied to continuous, like hours to a day.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the enumeration of three ways.
The passage includes both the matn and commentary on motion.
2
Identify the three ways
Find where the passage lists the three respects in which motion is accidental quantity: inherence in a locus of quantity, superimposition on distance, superimposition on time.
Each leads to a different kind of variation.
3
Understand the variations
For each respect, note the resulting property: division (tajazzi), fewness/multitude (qilla wa kathra), speed/slowness (sur'a wa but').
4
Note the final point
The passage concludes with the application of discrete quantity to continuous, e.g., 'this day is ten hours'.
This shows another aspect of accidental quantity.

Variation is in fewness and multitude due to superimposition on distance, and in speed and slowness due to superimposition on time. Magnitude with Addition: Length, Width, and Depth
Sanusi distinguishes pure quantity from relational magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two senses of magnitude (pure quantity vs. relational) and how the terms length, width, and depth can refer to either.

Core Idea
Length, width, and depth can be pure extensions or relational measures.

Sanusi explains that magnitude (miqdar) can be considered either as pure extension or as a quantity taken with additional relations. The terms length, width, and depth have two meanings: one absolute and one relative. This distinction is crucial for understanding how these terms are used in geometry and physics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on magnitude with addition
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the distinction between pure quantities and relational magnitudes.
2
Identify the two meanings
Notice that length, width, and depth can mean pure extensions (كميات محضة) or specific relational measures (e.g., first dimension, longest extension, head-to-toe).
3
Understand the consequence
When relational, these terms can be denied of extension, as in 'this line is long, that is not long'. Reflect on why this shows they are not pure quantities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المقدار قد يوجد مع إضافة ويسمى الطول والعرض والعمق. قال : والمقدار قد يؤخذ يعني أنه قد يراد بالطول والعرض والعمق نفس الامتدادات على ما مر فتكون كميات محضة، وقد يراد بالطول البعد المفروض أولاً أو أطول الامتدادات، أو البعد المأخوذ من رأس الإنسان إلى قدميه، أو الحيوان إلى ذنبه أو من مركز الكرة إلى محيطها، وبالعرض البعد المفروض ثانياً، أو أقصر البعدين أو البعد الآخذ من يمين الحيوان إلى شماله، وبالعمق البعد المفروض ثالثاً والمتحيز المعتبر من أعلى الشيء إلى أسفله أو فيما بين ظهر الحيوان وبطنه وحينئذ لا تكون كميات محضة بل مأخوذة مع إضافات، ولهذا يصح سلبها عن الامتداد كما يقال هذا الخط طويل، وذاك ليس بطويل، وذلك السطح عريض، وذاك ليس بعريض . المقادير جواهر مجتمعة عند المتكلمين قال : وأنكر المتكلمون وجود العدد لما مر وجعلوا المقادير جواهر مجتمعة، على أنحاء مختلفة، أو أموراً عدمية لكونها نهايات وانقطاعات، ورد الأول بتبدلها مع بقاء الجسم بعينه ويتوقف السطح على التناهي المفتقر إلى البرهان، والخط في الكرة على الحركة أو القطع.
He said: Magnitude may exist with addition and is called length, width, and depth. He said: And magnitude is taken, meaning that by length, width, and depth may be meant the extensions themselves as previously, so they are pure quantities. And by length may be meant the first dimension or the longest extension, or the distance taken from the head of a man to his feet, or of an animal to its tail, or from the center of a sphere to its circumference. And by width, the second dimension, or the shorter of the two dimensions, or the distance taken from the right of an animal to its left. And by depth, the third dimension and the extension considered from the top of a thing to its bottom, or between the back and belly of an animal. Then they are not pure quantities but taken with additions, and hence it is valid to deny them of extension, as is said 'this line is long' and 'that is not long', 'this surface is wide' and 'that is not wide'. Magnitudes are composite atoms according to the theologians. He said: And the theologians denied the existence of number for the reason previously mentioned, and they made magnitudes composite atoms, in different arrangements, or non-existent things because they are ends and cut-offs. The first is refuted by their changing while the body remains the same, and the surface depends on termination which requires demonstration, and the line on a sphere depends on motion or cutting.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage covers two topics. First, it discusses magnitude (miqdar) taken with addition, i.e., length (tul), width (ard), and depth (umq). The commentary explains that these terms can refer either to the pure extensions themselves (as pure quantities) or to specific relational measures (like head-to-toe length, or the shorter of two dimensions). When relational, they are not pure quantities and can be predicated or negated, e.g., 'this line is long' but 'that is not long'. Second, it presents the view of the mutakallimun (theologians) that magnitudes are composite atoms (jawahir mujtami'a) or non-existent things (umur `adamiyya) because they are just boundaries. The text refutes the atomistic view: atoms change while the body remains, and surface presupposes termination which itself requires proof, while a line on a sphere depends on motion or cutting (implying geometrical construction rather than atomism).

الطول
العرض
العمق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a pure quantity without relation
B) It is considered relationally, not as pure extension
C) It is added to other magnitudes
D) It is a mathematical point

Key Takeaway
Magnitude taken with addition refers to relational dimensions, not pure quantities.

Theologians on Magnitudes as Atoms and the Refutation
Sanusi refutes the atomistic view of magnitudes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological view that magnitudes are composite atoms or non-existent things, and the refutation based on change and geometrical reasoning.

Core Idea
Theologians reduce magnitudes to atoms; this is refuted by change and geometrical reasoning.

Sanusi presents the mutakallimun's view that magnitudes are nothing but composite atoms (jawahir mujtami'a) or non-existent boundaries. He then refutes this by pointing out that atoms change while the body remains, and that surface and line depend on termination and motion which require proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the theologians' view
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the two claims made by the theologians: magnitudes are composite atoms or non-existent things.
2
Understand the first refutation
Sanusi argues that atoms change while the body itself remains unchanged. This shows magnitudes cannot be atoms, as the body's magnitude persists despite atomic changes.
3
Understand the second refutation
The surface depends on termination, which itself requires demonstration. The line on a sphere depends on motion or cutting, implying that lines are not mere atoms but require construction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: المقدار قد يوجد مع إضافة ويسمى الطول والعرض والعمق. قال : والمقدار قد يؤخذ يعني أنه قد يراد بالطول والعرض والعمق نفس الامتدادات على ما مر فتكون كميات محضة، وقد يراد بالطول البعد المفروض أولاً أو أطول الامتدادات، أو البعد المأخوذ من رأس الإنسان إلى قدميه، أو الحيوان إلى ذنبه أو من مركز الكرة إلى محيطها، وبالعرض البعد المفروض ثانياً، أو أقصر البعدين أو البعد الآخذ من يمين الحيوان إلى شماله، وبالعمق البعد المفروض ثالثاً والمتحيز المعتبر من أعلى الشيء إلى أسفله أو فيما بين ظهر الحيوان وبطنه وحينئذ لا تكون كميات محضة بل مأخوذة مع إضافات، ولهذا يصح سلبها عن الامتداد كما يقال هذا الخط طويل، وذاك ليس بطويل، وذلك السطح عريض، وذاك ليس بعريض . المقادير جواهر مجتمعة عند المتكلمين قال : وأنكر المتكلمون وجود العدد لما مر وجعلوا المقادير جواهر مجتمعة، على أنحاء مختلفة، أو أموراً عدمية لكونها نهايات وانقطاعات، ورد الأول بتبدلها مع بقاء الجسم بعينه ويتوقف السطح على التناهي المفتقر إلى البرهان، والخط في الكرة على الحركة أو القطع.
He said: Magnitude may exist with addition and is called length, width, and depth. He said: And magnitude is taken, meaning that by length, width, and depth may be meant the extensions themselves as previously, so they are pure quantities. And by length may be meant the first dimension or the longest extension, or the distance taken from the head of a man to his feet, or of an animal to its tail, or from the center of a sphere to its circumference. And by width, the second dimension, or the shorter of the two dimensions, or the distance taken from the right of an animal to its left. And by depth, the third dimension and the extension considered from the top of a thing to its bottom, or between the back and belly of an animal. Then they are not pure quantities but taken with additions, and hence it is valid to deny them of extension, as is said 'this line is long' and 'that is not long', 'this surface is wide' and 'that is not wide'. Magnitudes are composite atoms according to the theologians. He said: And the theologians denied the existence of number for the reason previously mentioned, and they made magnitudes composite atoms, in different arrangements, or non-existent things because they are ends and cut-offs. The first is refuted by their changing while the body remains the same, and the surface depends on termination which requires demonstration, and the line on a sphere depends on motion or cutting.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage covers two topics. First, it discusses magnitude (miqdar) taken with addition, i.e., length (tul), width (ard), and depth (umq). The commentary explains that these terms can refer either to the pure extensions themselves (as pure quantities) or to specific relational measures (like head-to-toe length, or the shorter of two dimensions). When relational, they are not pure quantities and can be predicated or negated, e.g., 'this line is long' but 'that is not long'. Second, it presents the view of the mutakallimun (theologians) that magnitudes are composite atoms (jawahir mujtami'a) or non-existent things (umur `adamiyya) because they are just boundaries. The text refutes the atomistic view: atoms change while the body remains, and surface presupposes termination which itself requires proof, while a line on a sphere depends on motion or cutting (implying geometrical construction rather than atomism).

الأمور العدمية

Misconception: Magnitudes are merely composite atoms or non-existent ends.
Correction: Magnitudes are pure quantities that can change while the body remains, and geometrical concepts depend on demonstration and motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The theologians reduced magnitudes to atoms to avoid infinite regress. In your own thinking, do you ever reduce complex realities—like relationships, faith, or identity—to simpler components? What might you be missing, and how can you cultivate a more integrated view?
?
If surfaces and lines depend on demonstration and motion, how does this affect your understanding of the physical world as something constructed by God? Are you content to accept conventional explanations without seeking deeper proof, or does this passage compel you to examine the foundations of your own beliefs?

Mutakallimūn on Magnitudes: Composite Substances or Non-Existents
Mutakallimūn's view on magnitudes with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimūn's position that magnitudes are either composite substances (arrangements of atoms) or non-existential endpoints, and the arguments they gave in response to objections.

Core Idea
Magnitudes are not separate entities but arrangements of atoms or mere endpoints.

The mutakallimūn (Islamic theologians) had a distinctive view of physical magnitudes. They denied that number exists independently and instead argued that magnitudes are either composite substances—atoms arranged in different configurations—or merely non-existential endpoints. In this passage, the author presents this view and then addresses two objections that might be raised against it.

Guided Path
1
Read the source text
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the compact style and the two distinct claims about magnitudes.
The passage contains a statement of the view followed by two objections and answers.
2
Identify the two positions
The mutakallimūn held that magnitudes are either 'composite substances' (jawāhir mujtami‘a) or 'non-existents' (umūr ‘adamiyya). Understand what each means: composite substances are atoms arranged together; non-existents are mere endpoints like surfaces and lines.
These are two alternative explanations, not contradictory.
3
Examine the first objection and answer
The first objection: magnitudes change while the body remains the same. The answer: what changes are the positions (awḍā‘) of the atoms, not the atoms themselves. The surface depends on finitude, which requires proof.
The mutakallimūn preserve the permanence of atoms.
4
Examine the second objection and answer
The second objection: magnitudes are non-existents because they are endpoints. The answer: they have positions (awḍā‘) and are indicated as themselves. Depending on something else for a specific state does not negate their own existence.
This defends the reality of surfaces/lines as having position.
5
Reflect on the overall view
Consider how this view fits into the atomistic metaphysics of the mutakallimūn. Magnitudes are not separate substances but relational properties of atoms. This contrasts with other philosophical schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأنكر المتكلمون وجود العدد لما مر وجعلوا المقادير جواهر مجتمعة، على أنحاء مختلفة، أو أموراً عدمية لكونها نهايات وانقطاعات، ورد الأول بتبدلها مع بقاء الجسم بعينه ويتوقف السطح على التناهي المفتقر إلى البرهان، والخط في الكرة على الحركة أو القطع. والثاني: بكونها ذوات أوضاع وأجيب بأن المتبدل أوضاع الجواهر والمتوقف على الغير كونها على حالة مخصوصة والإشارة إليها أنفسها.
He said: And the mutakallimūn denied the existence of number for what has passed, and they made magnitudes to be composite substances (jawāhir mujtami‘a) in different ways, or to be non-existents (umūr ‘adamiyya) because they are endpoints and cuts. And the first [objection] was refuted by their changing with the persistence of the body itself, and the surface depends on finitude which requires proof, and the line in the sphere depends on motion or cut. And the second [objection] was refuted by their having positions, and it was answered that what changes are the positions of the substances, and what depends on something else is their being in a specific state, and the indication is to them themselves.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimūn's view on magnitudes (al-maqādīr). They denied the existence of number based on a previous argument, and they considered magnitudes either as composite substances (jawāhir mujtami‘a) – referring to atoms (al-ajzā' allatī lā tatajazza') arranged in different configurations – or as non-existents because they are merely endpoints (nihāyāt) and cuts (inqiṭā‘āt). Two objections are then addressed: First, magnitudes change while the body remains the same. The mutakallimūn respond that what changes are the positions (awḍā‘) of the atoms, not the atoms themselves. Second, the existence of a surface depends on the finitude of the body, which requires a proof. The answer is that depending on something for being in a specific state does not negate its own existence; the surface itself is indicated.

Key Terms

أمور عدمية
أوضاع
تناهي
السطح
الخط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mutakallimūn believed magnitudes are separate substances independent of atoms.
Correction: They held that magnitudes are either arrangements of atoms (composite substances) or mere endpoints (non-existents) – not independent entities.

Checkpoint
How did the mutakallimūn respond to the objection that magnitudes change while the body remains the same?
A) They denied that magnitudes exist at all.
B) They said that what changes are the positions of the atoms, not the atoms themselves.
C) They argued that the body also changes.
D) They said that magnitudes are non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimūn's view on magnitudes illustrates their atomistic metaphysics where quantities are not real entities but either relational properties of atoms or non-existential endpoints.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimūn taught that magnitudes change while the substance of the body remains. What 'magnitudes'—status, possessions, relationships—do you cling to as if they are your core identity? Are you willing to see them as mere arrangements, and to invest in the substance of your soul before Allah? What concrete action will you take today to shift your focus from what changes to what endures?
?
The objection that non-existents cannot be indicated was answered: they have positions and are indicated as themselves. Your very existence is not self-sufficient; it depends entirely on Allah. Do you live as though you are an independent substance, or do you constantly acknowledge your dependence? How can your next prayer be a moment of recognizing that you are merely a 'positioned' being sustained by Him?

Mutakallimūn on Magnitudes as Non-Existents
Explains the mutakallimūn's view that magnitudes are non-existent based on atomist composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimūn considered magnitudes (length, width, depth) to be non-existents based on their atomist theory of body composition.

Core Idea
Magnitudes are just aggregates of atoms — real only as endpoints, not things.

The mutakallimūn had a distinctive view on quantities like number, time, and magnitude. While they deferred discussion of number and time, they directly addressed magnitude, grounding their analysis in their atomist conception of body. According to them, a body is composed of indivisible atoms that contact but do not truly connect. From this, they concluded that magnitudes like length, surface, and depth are not independently existing substances but rather non-existents (umūr 'adamiyya) — mere endpoints or cuts of the body. This passage presents that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The mutakallimūn famously disagreed about the existence of quantities. Note that number and time are set aside: number involves mental existence (previously discussed) and time will come later. Focus on magnitudes.
This sets the scope: only magnitudes are analyzed in detail.
2
Identify the atomist foundation
The view on magnitudes is built on the idea that body is composed of indivisible atoms (ajzā' lā tatajazza') that are in contact (tamās) but not truly connected. This means there is no continuous extension; only discrete atoms touching.
This is key: continuity is denied, so magnitudes reduce to arrangements of atoms.
3
Trace how magnitudes arise
From the ordering of atoms: if they line up in one direction, we get length; in two directions (a surface), we get width; differences in length/width are just differences in the number of atoms.
No new entities are needed; magnitude is just a relational property.
4
Grasp the conclusion: magnitudes as non-existents
Even if we grant that magnitudes are not substances (not atoms), they must be non-existents. Why? Because a surface is just an endpoint of a body, a line is an endpoint of a surface. The mathematical body (continuous extension) is not real; even if it were, it would be composed of non-existents and thus be non-existent.
The argument leans on the idea that endpoints are not independently existing things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأنكر المتكلمون قد اشتهر خلاف من المتكلمين في وجود الكميات على الإطلاق، أما العدد فلما مر في باب الوحدة والكثرة وكأنه مبني على نفي الوجود الذهني، وإلا فالفلاسفة لا يجعلونه من الموجودات العينية، بل من الاعتبارات الذهنية، وأما الزمان فلما سيأتي، وأما المقادير فبناء على أن الجسم متألف من أجزاء لا يتجزء مجتمعه على وجه التماس دون الاتصال الرافع للمفاصل والمقاطع، والمجتمع من ترتبها على سمت الخط، وباعتباره يتصف بالطول، وعلى سمتين هو السطح، وباعتباره يتصف بالعرض والتفاوت راجع إلى قلة الأجزاء، أو كثرتها، ولو سلم أن المقادير ليست جواهر فهي أمور عدمية إذ السطح نهاية وانقطاع الجسم والخط للسطح كالنقطة للخط، ولا يثبت للجسم التعليمي، ولو ثبت فالمتألف من العدمي عدمي.
He said: And the mutakallimūn denied – the disagreement of the mutakallimūn became famous regarding the existence of quantities absolutely. As for number, it is due to what has passed in the chapter on unity and multiplicity, and it is apparently built on the denial of mental existence; otherwise the philosophers do not make it among external existents, but rather among mental considerations. And as for time, it will come later. And as for magnitudes, it is based on the fact that the body is composed of indivisible parts (ajzā' lā tatajazza'), their aggregate being by contact without the connection that removes joints and sections. And the aggregate from their ordering in the direction of a line is by it qualified with length, and in two directions is the surface, and by it qualified with width, and the difference goes back to the fewness or multitude of parts. And if it is granted that the magnitudes are not substances, then they are non-existents, because the surface is an end and cut of the body, and the line for the surface is like the point for the line, and it is not established for the mathematical body, and if it were established, then the composite of the non-existent is non-existent.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage further elaborates the mutakallimūn's position on quantities. They famously disagreed about the existence of quantities in general. Regarding number, they denied it based on a previous topic (unity and multiplicity) and its connection to the denial of mental existence. Time will be discussed later. For magnitudes, their view is based on the atomist theory: the body is composed of indivisible parts that are in contact (tamās) but not truly continuous. The arrangement of these parts in a line gives length, in two directions gives surface, and in three directions gives depth. Differences in length and width are due to the number of parts. If one grants that magnitudes are not substances (i.e., not the atoms themselves), then they are non-existents (umūr ‘adamiyya) because a surface is merely the endpoint of a body, and a line is the endpoint of a surface. The mathematical body (al-jism al-ta‘līmī) is not established; even if it were, a composite of non-existents would itself be non-existent.

Key Terms
أجزاء لا تتجزأ
التماس
الاتصال
الجسم التعليمي
أمور عدمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mutakallimūn considered magnitudes to be completely non-existent in every sense.
Correction: Their claim is more nuanced: magnitudes are not independently existing substances; if they were accidents, they could exist, but here they argue they are not even accidents but non-existents because they are endpoints of atomic arrangements.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimūn in this passage, if magnitudes are not substances, what are they?
A) Accidents inhering in atoms.
B) Non-existents, because they are endpoints and cuts.
C) Mental concepts only.
D) The atoms themselves.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimūn's atomism led them to view magnitudes as non-existent properties arising from atomic contact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimūn argue that magnitudes like the line, surface, and volume are not truly existent—they are just aggregates of atoms and endpoints. If the physical dimensions you rely on daily (your property, your body's boundaries, your goals) are ultimately composed of non-existent endpoints, where is your real substance? What are the 'atoms' of your faith—the indivisible deeds and intentions—that actually exist? How can you invest more in those enduring realities rather than in the mere 'magnitudes' of worldly success?
?
The passage contrasts 'contact' (tamās) without 'connection' (ittiṣāl) in the composition of bodies. In your relationship with the Qur'an and prayer, are you merely 'in contact'—going through motions—or is there a genuine 'connection' that transforms your inner state? What one concrete change can you make today to move from tamās to ittiṣāl?

Philosophers' Proof that Magnitudes are Accidents
Philosophers prove magnitudes are accidents via change and finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers argue that magnitudes (measurements) are accidents rather than substances, using both a simple example and a deeper logical proof.

Core Idea
Magnitudes are accidents because they can change while the body remains the same.

This passage presents the philosophers' case that the quantities we observe in objects—like length, width, surface area—are not part of the object's essence but rather accidental features. They offer two complementary approaches: a vivid example of a candle changing shape, and a logical argument from the dependence of surfaces on finitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two parts: 'ijmālī' (summary) and 'tafsīlī' (detailed).
The philosophers use both a concrete example and a logical deduction.
2
Understand the summary evidence
The candle example shows that the same body (the candle) can have different magnitudes (round, cubic, elongated) while remaining the same substance. This proves magnitudes change accidentally.
3
Understand the detailed evidence
The detailed argument points out that a surface only exists because the body is finite; an infinite body cannot have a surface. But finitude itself requires a proof, which will come later.
This establishes magnitudes as dependent on something else, hence accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الحكماء على كون المقادير أعراضاً لا جواهر هي أجزاء الجسم، إما إجمالاً فبأنها تتبدل مع بقاء الجسم بعينه، كالشمعة المعينة تجعل تارة مدوراً له سطح واحد لا خط فيه، وتارة مكعباً لها سطوح، وفيها خطوط، والمكعب يجعل تارة مستطيلاً يزداد طوله وينقص عرضه، وتارة بالعكس، وإما تفصيلا فبأن ثبوت السطح للجسم يتوقف على تناهيه ضرورة أن غير المتناهي لا يحيط به سطح، وثبوت التناهي يفتقر إلى برهان يدل عليه، كما سيجيء في بيان تناهي الأبعاد.
And the philosophers argued that magnitudes are accidents (a‘rāḍ), not substances that are parts of the body. Either in summary: because they change while the body itself remains, like a particular candle: sometimes it is made round, having one surface with no line in it, and sometimes it is made cubic, having surfaces and lines in it, and the cube is sometimes made elongated, its length increasing and its width decreasing, and sometimes the opposite. Or in detail: because the existence of a surface for a body depends on its finitude, necessarily because the infinite cannot be surrounded by a surface, and the existence of finitude requires a proof that indicates it, as will come in the explanation of the finitude of dimensions.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (ḥukamā') proof that magnitudes (al-maqādīr) are accidents (a‘rāḍ) and not substances (jawāhir) that are parts of the body. They provide two types of evidence: (1) overall (ijmālī): magnitudes change while the body itself remains the same. For example, a candle can be shaped round, then cubic, then elongated—its surfaces, lines, and dimensions change, yet the candle (the body) persists. (2) detailed (tafsīlī): the existence of a surface for a body depends on the finitude of that body, because an infinite body cannot be bounded by a surface. The finitude itself requires a proof, which will be given later in the discussion on the finitude of dimensions. This shows that magnitudes are not essential parts of the body but rather accidental features.

Key Terms
أعراض
جواهر
الشمعة
تناهي
الأبعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers' argument disproves the atomist view completely.
Correction: The argument only shows magnitudes are accidents, not necessarily refuting atomic composition; it is a preliminary step in a larger discussion.

Checkpoint
What is the overall (ijmālī) evidence that the philosophers give to prove magnitudes are accidents?
A) Magnitudes are always perceivable.
B) Magnitudes change while the body itself remains, as with a candle reshaped.
C) Magnitudes are divisible.
D) Magnitudes are mental constructs.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use the example of a reshaped candle and a logical argument about finitude to show that magnitudes are accidental, not essential, to a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body changes dramatically over a lifetime—weight, shape, even cells renew entirely. If magnitudes are accidental, what part of 'you' remains constant? How does that affect your attachment to your physical appearance?
?
The philosophers demand a proof for finitude; they do not accept it as obvious. Is there an assumption in your life—about your identity, your purpose, or your beliefs—that you have never questioned? What would it take to examine its foundation?
?
If the same body can take on completely different shapes while remaining essentially the same, does that change how you view others who look different from you? Will you actually practice seeing their essence beyond appearance today?

The Mutakallimun's Denial of Quantities: Number and Time
Sanusi introduces the mutakallimun's denial of quantities, focusing on number and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun denied the real existence of number and time in the external world.

Core Idea
Number and time are mental constructs according to the mutakallimun.

The passage opens with a broad claim: the mutakallimun denied quantities altogether. The author then briefly explains the denial of number, referring to an earlier discussion and the rejection of mental existence. Time is deferred to a later discussion. This lesson focuses on the mutakallimun's stance on number and time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a general claim, then specific examples.
2
Understand the general denial
The mutakallimun denied the existence of quantities (al-kammiyyāt) in an absolute sense. This is a strong claim that differentiates them from philosophers.
Quantities include number, time, and magnitude.
3
Examine the denial of number
The passage states that the denial of number is based on the denial of mental existence (al-wujūd al-dhihnī). The philosophers consider number a mental consideration, not a concrete existent.
Refer to earlier discussion on unity and multiplicity.
4
Note the treatment of time
The denial of time is promised for a later discussion. For now, recognize that the mutakallimun also deny time as a real quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنكر المتكلمون قد اشتهر خلاف من المتكلمين في وجود الكميات على الإطلاق، أما العدد فلما مر في باب الوحدة والكثرة وكأنه مبني على نفي الوجود الذهني، وإلا فالفلاسفة لا يجعلونه من الموجودات العينية، بل من الاعتبارات الذهنية، وأما الزمان فلما سيأتي، وأما المقادير فبناء على أن الجسم متألف من أجزاء لا يتجزء مجتمعه على وجه التماس دون الاتصال الرافع للمفاصل والمقاطع، والمجتمع من ترتبها على سمت الخط، وباعتباره يتصف بالطول، وعلى سمتين هو السطح، وباعتباره يتصف بالعرض والتفاوت راجع إلى قلة الأجزاء، أو كثرتها، ولو سلم أن المقادير ليست جواهر فهي أمور عدمية إذ السطح نهاية وانقطاع الجسم والخط للسطح كالنقطة للخط، ولا يثبت للجسم التعليمي، ولو ثبت فالمتألف من العدمي عدمي
And the mutakallimun denied—the disagreement of the theologians regarding the existence of quantities in an absolute sense has become well-known: as for number, due to what has passed in the chapter on unity and multiplicity, and it is apparently based on the denial of mental existence; otherwise, the philosophers do not make it among concrete existents, but rather among mental considerations. As for time, it will come later. As for magnitudes, it is based on the fact that the body is composed of indivisible parts, its aggregate being by way of contiguity without the continuity that removes joints and sections, and the aggregate from their arrangement along the direction of the line, and by virtue of that it is described as length; and along two directions it is the surface, and by virtue of that it is described as width; and variation reduces to the fewness or multitude of parts. And even if it is granted that magnitudes are not substances, they are non-existential things, since the surface is an end and termination of the body, and the line is to the surface as the point is to the line, and it is not established for the mathematical body, and if it were established, then what is composed of the non-existent is non-existent.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the mutakallimun (theologians) who deny the real existence of quantities (al-kammiyyāt) in the external world. The author notes that there is a well-known disagreement among theologians on this issue. The denial is explained for each type of quantity: number, time, and magnitude. For number, it is referenced to the earlier discussion of unity and multiplicity, and it is based on the denial of mental existence; otherwise, philosophers treat number as a mental consideration, not a concrete existent. Time is deferred to a later discussion. For magnitudes (al-maqādīr), the mutakallimun rely on their doctrine of the indivisible atom (juzʾ lā yatajazzaʾ). They explain that a body is composed of atoms that are contiguous (tamās) but not continuous (ittiṣāl), which would remove joints and sections. Length, width, and depth are merely configurations resulting from the arrangement of these atoms along one or two dimensions. Furthermore, they argue that even if magnitudes were not substances, they would be non-existential (ʿadamiyya) because a surface is an end and termination of the body, and a line is an end of the surface. Since the mathematical body does not exist, a composition of non-existents cannot be existent. This establishes the mutakallimun's view that quantities are not real entities but conceptual constructs based on atomic configurations.

The Atomistic Explanation of Magnitudes
Sanusi presents the mutakallimun's atomistic explanation of magnitudes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun's doctrine of indivisible atoms leads to the denial of magnitudes as real continuous quantities.

Core Idea
Magnitudes are merely configurations of contiguous atoms.

After dismissing number and time, the passage turns to magnitudes (al-maqādīr). The mutakallimun's denial of magnitudes is based on their atomistic theory: the body is composed of indivisible atoms (juzʾ lā yatajazzaʾ) that are in contact but not continuous. This lesson unpacks that explanation, showing how length, width, and surface are just arrangements of atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage. Notice the key terms: أجزاء لا يتجزء (indivisible parts), التماس (contiguity), الاتصال (continuity).
2
Identify the atomistic foundation
The mutakallimun hold that a body is made of indivisible atoms. These atoms aggregate by contiguity (tamās), not by continuity (ittiṣāl), which would merge them into a single continuous substance.
Continuity would remove 'joints and sections'.
3
Understand the generation of dimensions
Length is produced when atoms are arranged along a single line (samt al-khaṭṭ). Width is produced when atoms are arranged along two lines (surface). Depth is not mentioned here but can be inferred.
4
Understand variation
Differences in size (length, width) are due to the number of atoms: more atoms give greater magnitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنكر المتكلمون قد اشتهر خلاف من المتكلمين في وجود الكميات على الإطلاق، أما العدد فلما مر في باب الوحدة والكثرة وكأنه مبني على نفي الوجود الذهني، وإلا فالفلاسفة لا يجعلونه من الموجودات العينية، بل من الاعتبارات الذهنية، وأما الزمان فلما سيأتي، وأما المقادير فبناء على أن الجسم متألف من أجزاء لا يتجزء مجتمعه على وجه التماس دون الاتصال الرافع للمفاصل والمقاطع، والمجتمع من ترتبها على سمت الخط، وباعتباره يتصف بالطول، وعلى سمتين هو السطح، وباعتباره يتصف بالعرض والتفاوت راجع إلى قلة الأجزاء، أو كثرتها، ولو سلم أن المقادير ليست جواهر فهي أمور عدمية إذ السطح نهاية وانقطاع الجسم والخط للسطح كالنقطة للخط، ولا يثبت للجسم التعليمي، ولو ثبت فالمتألف من العدمي عدمي
And the mutakallimun denied—the disagreement of the theologians regarding the existence of quantities in an absolute sense has become well-known: as for number, due to what has passed in the chapter on unity and multiplicity, and it is apparently based on the denial of mental existence; otherwise, the philosophers do not make it among concrete existents, but rather among mental considerations. As for time, it will come later. As for magnitudes, it is based on the fact that the body is composed of indivisible parts, its aggregate being by way of contiguity without the continuity that removes joints and sections, and the aggregate from their arrangement along the direction of the line, and by virtue of that it is described as length; and along two directions it is the surface, and by virtue of that it is described as width; and variation reduces to the fewness or multitude of parts. The Non-Existential Status of Magnitudes
Sanusi argues that magnitudes are non-existential entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun considered magnitudes to be non-existential (ʿadamiyyāt) rather than substances.

Core Idea
Even if not substances, magnitudes are non-existential because they are boundaries.

Having established that magnitudes are atomic configurations, the passage now addresses a hypothetical: even if magnitudes are not substances, what are they? The mutakallimun argue they are non-existential things (umūr ʿadamiyya). This lesson examines the reasoning: a surface is the end of a body, a line is the end of a surface, and so on. Since the mathematical body does not exist, any composition of non-existents is itself non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic. Notice the logical flow: a conditional statement, then definitions, then a conclusion.
2
Analyze the conditional premise
The passage begins: 'Even if it is granted that magnitudes are not substances.' This shows the mutakallimun are addressing an objection or a hypothetical concession.
They accept that magnitudes might not be substances, but they still deny their existence.
3
Examine the definitions
A surface is defined as the end and termination of a body. A line is to a surface as a point is to a line—so a line is the boundary of a surface. These are not positive entities but boundaries.
4
Understand the conclusion
The mathematical body (al-jism al-taʿlīmī) is not established. If it were, it would be composed of non-existents, and a composition of non-existents is non-existent. Thus magnitudes are non-existential.

Philosophers' Proofs on Magnitudes
Philosophers argue magnitudes are accidents and existent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' arguments that magnitudes (lines, surfaces, volumes) are accidents and existent entities distinct from the body.

Core Idea
Magnitudes are accidents because they change while the body remains, and existent because they can be pointed to.

The philosophers present a two-pronged argument: first, that magnitudes like surfaces and lines are accidents because they can change while the body stays the same; second, that they are existent because they can be sensorily indicated. This lesson unpacks their reasoning using examples like a candle and a thread forming a sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two main proofs
The philosophers give two sets of arguments: one for magnitudes being accidents, and another for them being existent. Find where each starts.
3
Understand the candle example
The candle can be round or cubical, changing its surface and lines while remaining the same wax. Notice how this is used to show magnitudes are not parts of the body.
4
Examine the sphere/thread example
A thread forms a sphere through motion, producing points and lines that are not parts of the thread. This is a detailed proof that magnitudes are accidents.
5
Reflect on the existence argument
Magnitudes can be pointed to sensorily, so they are existent. Consider the objection about finitude and how the philosophers respond to it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الحكماء على كون المقادير أعراضاً لا جواهر هي أجزاء الجسم، إما إجمالاً فبأنها تتبدل مع بقاء الجسم بعينه، كالشمعة المعينة تجعل تارة مدوراً له سطح واحد لا خط فيه، وتارة مكعباً لها سطوح، وفيها خطوط، والمكعب يجعل تارة مستطيلاً يزداد طوله وينقص عرضه، وتارة بالعكس، وإما تفصيلا فبأن ثبوت السطح للجسم يتوقف على تناهيه ضرورة أن غير المتناهي لا يحيط به سطح، وثبوت التناهي يفتقر إلى برهان يدل عليه، كما سيجيء في بيان تناهي الأبعاد. فلو كان السطح من أجزاء الجسم لما كان كذلك وثبوت الكرة للخيط يتوقف على حركتها الوضعية المستديرة، لتحدث نقطتان لا يتحركان هما قطباها، وبينهما خط المحور على محيطهما منطقة أعظم الدوائر، وتتوقف على قطعهما لتحدث سطحاً مستديراً هو دائرة محيط بها خط مستدير وما يتوقف ثبوته للشيء على الغير لا يكون نفسه، ولا جزءاً منه، واحتجوا على كون المقادير وجودية بأنها ذوات أوضاع يشار إليها إشارة حسية بأنهاهنا، ولا إشارة إلى العدم غاية ما في الباب أن عروض السطح للجسم التعليمي، وعروض الخط للسطح، وعروض النقطة للخط إنما يكون باعتبار التناهي وهو عدم الامتداد الأخذ في جهة ما بمعنى نفاد ذلك الامتداد وانقطاعه، وهذا القدر لا يقتضي عدمية هذه الأمور لجواز أن يكون الوجودي مشروطاً بالعدمي ومتصفاً به وأجيب بأن الذي يتغير ويتبدل مع بقاء الجسم هو وضع الجواهر المفردة بعضها مع بعض فقد تجتمع، وقد تفترق ولكل من الاجتماع والافتراق هيئات مخصوصة، فإن أريد بثبوت المقادير هذا فلا نزاع، وإن أريد أعراض قائمة بالجسم، غير أجزائه وهيئات ترتبها فممنوع، ولا دلالة لما ذكرتم عليه، وإنما يتم لو ثبت نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وما ذكر من توقف السطح والخط على أمر خارج عن الجسم وعما يتوقف عليه الجسم ليلزم كونهما عرضين فراجع إلى ما ذكرناه إذ حقيقتهما عندنا الجواهر الفردة لكن على وضع وترتيب مخصوص بأن يترتب على الطول من غير عرض، أو على الطول والعرض من غير عمق والمتوقف على الغير هو تلك الحالة والترتيب المخصوص، وما ذكر من كونها ذوات أوضاع، فعندنا الإشارة إنما هي إلى نفس الجواهر الفردة المترتبة ترتباً مخصوصاً، والنهايات إعدام وانقطاعات بمعنى أنه ليست بعد تلك الجواهر جوهر آخر
And the philosophers argued that magnitudes are accidents, not substances that are parts of the body: either summarily—because they change while the body itself remains, like a specific candle that is made sometimes round, having one surface with no line in it, and sometimes cubical, having surfaces and lines in it, and the cube is made sometimes rectangular, its length increasing and its width decreasing, and sometimes the opposite; and in detail—because the existence of a surface for the body depends on its finitude, necessarily because the infinite is not encompassed by a surface, and the establishment of finitude requires a proof indicating it, as will come in the explanation of the finitude of dimensions. For if the surface were among the parts of the body, it would not be thus. And the establishment of the sphere for the thread depends on its rotational motion, so that two points arise that do not move, which are its poles, and between them the axis line, and on their circumference the zone of the greatest circles; and it depends on their cutting to produce a round surface which is a circle surrounded by a round line; and what depends for its existence in a thing on something else is not itself, nor a part of it. And they argued that magnitudes are existent because they possess positions that can be pointed to by sensory indication as 'here,' and there is no indication to non-existence. The most that can be said is that the occurrence of the surface to the mathematical body, and the occurrence of the line to the surface, and the occurrence of the point to the line, is only by way of finitude, which is the absence of extension taken in some direction, meaning the exhaustion and termination of that extension; but this amount does not entail the non-existence of these things, because it is possible for an existent to be conditioned by the non-existent and to be described by it. And it was answered: that which changes and alters while the body remains is the position of the individual atoms with respect to each other; they may come together and may separate, and for each of togetherness and separation there are specific configurations. So if by the establishment of magnitudes this is intended, there is no dispute; but if what is intended is accidents subsisting in the body, other than its parts and the configurations of their arrangement, it is forbidden, and there is no indication for what you have mentioned. And it would only be established if the denial of the indivisible part were established. And what was mentioned—that the surface and line depend on something external to the body and to what the body depends on, so that they would be accidents—refers to what we have mentioned, for their reality according to us is the individual atoms, but in a specific position and arrangement: that they be arranged along the length without width, or along length and width without depth; and what depends on something else is that state and specific arrangement. And what was mentioned about their having positions, according to us the indication is only to the individual atoms themselves arranged in a specific arrangement; and the endpoints are non-existences and terminations, meaning that there is no other atom after those atoms.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' arguments and the mutakallimun's response regarding the nature of magnitudes (al-maqādīr: lines, surfaces, volumes). The philosophers argue that magnitudes are accidents (aʿrāḍ), not substances (jawāhir), through two forms of proof. The first is an inductive argument: magnitudes change while the body remains the same, as in the example of a candle that can be shaped round or cubical, demonstrating that the surface and lines are not parts of the body but accidents. The second is a detailed argument: a surface depends on the finitude of the body, and finitude requires proof; if the surface were an actual part, it would not depend on finitude. They also give a geometric example involving a thread forming a sphere, which depends on motion and produces points and lines, none of which are parts of the thread. The philosophers further argue that magnitudes are existent (wujūdiyya) because they can be pointed to sensorily. They acknowledge that surface, line, and point are related to finitude (which is non-existence), but this does not prove they are non-existent, as an existent can be conditioned by a non-existent. The mutakallimun respond by asserting that what changes is merely the arrangement (waḍʿ) of individual atoms (jawāhir farda). They agree that if by 'magnitudes' one means these arrangements, there is no dispute; but if one means accidents distinct from atoms and their configurations, it is denied. They argue that the philosophers' proofs only work if atomism is rejected. The mutakallimun explain that the reality of surfaces and lines is the individual atoms arranged in a specific way—along length, or length and width—and what depends on something else is that specific arrangement, not a separate accident. Furthermore, when pointing, one points to the atoms themselves, and endpoints are non-existences (iʿdām) indicating the absence of further atoms.

Key Terms
المقادير
السطح
الخط
العرض
الوجودي
العدمي
النهاية

A) The sun setting
B) A candle shaped round and cubical
C) A growing tree
D) A moving animal

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that magnitudes are accidents because they change while the body remains, and existent because they can be pointed to, using examples like a candle and a thread forming a sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The philosophers argued that what depends on something else is not a part of that thing. Your very existence depends entirely on Allah every moment. Do you live with that awareness, or do you often feel self-sufficient? What would change today if you truly believed that your breath is a loan?
?
The passage says an existent can be conditioned by non-existence. Our existence is bordered by non-existence before birth and after death. Does this reality make you anxious, or does it drive you to seek the One who alone is Necessary Existence? What is one concrete step you can take today to shift your focus from the changing magnitudes of this world to the Eternal?

Mutakallimun's Response: Magnitudes as Atomic Arrangements
Mutakallimun reduce magnitudes to positions of indivisible atoms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's response that magnitudes are not separate accidents but merely the arrangements (positions) of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Magnitudes are not accidents; they are the specific arrangements of indivisible atoms.

The mutakallimun reply to the philosophers by asserting that what changes is the position of individual atoms, not a separate accident. They argue that magnitudes like surfaces and lines are just the atoms arranged in certain ways (length without width, etc.), and endpoints are simply non-existences. This lesson explains their response and how it refutes the philosophers' claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the mutakallimun's answer. Notice how they directly address the philosophers' examples.
2
Understand the atomic interpretation
The mutakallimun say that change in magnitudes is actually change in the positions of indivisible atoms. They do not deny that magnitudes change, but they reinterpret the change.
3
The concept of arrangement (waḍʿ)
The specific arrangement of atoms—along length, or length and width—is what we call surface and line. This arrangement depends on something else, but it is not a separate accident.
4
Endpoints as non-existences
The philosophers brought up finitude; the mutakallimun reply that endpoints (nihaayaat) are simply non-existences—the absence of further atoms. This removes the need for a separate entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الحكماء على كون المقادير أعراضاً لا جواهر هي أجزاء الجسم، إما إجمالاً فبأنها تتبدل مع بقاء الجسم بعينه، كالشمعة المعينة تجعل تارة مدوراً له سطح واحد لا خط فيه، وتارة مكعباً لها سطوح، وفيها خطوط، والمكعب يجعل تارة مستطيلاً يزداد طوله وينقص عرضه، وتارة بالعكس، وإما تفصيلا فبأن ثبوت السطح للجسم يتوقف على تناهيه ضرورة أن غير المتناهي لا يحيط به سطح، وثبوت التناهي يفتقر إلى برهان يدل عليه، كما سيجيء في بيان تناهي الأبعاد. فلو كان السطح من أجزاء الجسم لما كان كذلك وثبوت الكرة للخيط يتوقف على حركتها الوضعية المستديرة، لتحدث نقطتان لا يتحركان هما قطباها، وبينهما خط المحور على محيطهما منطقة أعظم الدوائر، وتتوقف على قطعهما لتحدث سطحاً مستديراً هو دائرة محيط بها خط مستدير وما يتوقف ثبوته للشيء على الغير لا يكون نفسه، ولا جزءاً منه، واحتجوا على كون المقادير وجودية بأنها ذوات أوضاع يشار إليها إشارة حسية بأنهاهنا، ولا إشارة إلى العدم غاية ما في الباب أن عروض السطح للجسم التعليمي، وعروض الخط للسطح، وعروض النقطة للخط إنما يكون باعتبار التناهي وهو عدم الامتداد الأخذ في جهة ما بمعنى نفاد ذلك الامتداد وانقطاعه، وهذا القدر لا يقتضي عدمية هذه الأمور لجواز أن يكون الوجودي مشروطاً بالعدمي ومتصفاً به وأجيب بأن الذي يتغير ويتبدل مع بقاء الجسم هو وضع الجواهر المفردة بعضها مع بعض فقد تجتمع، وقد تفترق ولكل من الاجتماع والافتراق هيئات مخصوصة، فإن أريد بثبوت المقادير هذا فلا نزاع، وإن أريد أعراض قائمة بالجسم، غير أجزائه وهيئات ترتبها فممنوع، ولا دلالة لما ذكرتم عليه، وإنما يتم لو ثبت نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وما ذكر من توقف السطح والخط على أمر خارج عن الجسم وعما يتوقف عليه الجسم ليلزم كونهما عرضين فراجع إلى ما ذكرناه إذ حقيقتهما عندنا الجواهر الفردة لكن على وضع وترتيب مخصوص بأن يترتب على الطول من غير عرض، أو على الطول والعرض من غير عمق والمتوقف على الغير هو تلك الحالة والترتيب المخصوص، وما ذكر من كونها ذوات أوضاع، فعندنا الإشارة إنما هي إلى نفس الجواهر الفردة المترتبة ترتباً مخصوصاً، والنهايات إعدام وانقطاعات بمعنى أنه ليست بعد تلك الجواهر جوهر آخر
And the philosophers argued that magnitudes are accidents, not substances that are parts of the body: either summarily—because they change while the body itself remains, like a specific candle that is made sometimes round, having one surface with no line in it, and sometimes cubical, having surfaces and lines in it, and the cube is made sometimes rectangular, its length increasing and its width decreasing, and sometimes the opposite; and in detail—because the existence of a surface for the body depends on its finitude, necessarily because the infinite is not encompassed by a surface, and the establishment of finitude requires a proof indicating it, as will come in the explanation of the finitude of dimensions. For if the surface were among the parts of the body, it would not be thus. And the establishment of the sphere for the thread depends on its rotational motion, so that two points arise that do not move, which are its poles, and between them the axis line, and on their circumference the zone of the greatest circles; and it depends on their cutting to produce a round surface which is a circle surrounded by a round line; and what depends for its existence in a thing on something else is not itself, nor a part of it. And they argued that magnitudes are existent because they possess positions that can be pointed to by sensory indication as 'here,' and there is no indication to non-existence. The most that can be said is that the occurrence of the surface to the mathematical body, and the occurrence of the line to the surface, and the occurrence of the point to the line, is only by way of finitude, which is the absence of extension taken in some direction, meaning the exhaustion and termination of that extension; but this amount does not entail the non-existence of these things, because it is possible for an existent to be conditioned by the non-existent and to be described by it. And it was answered: that which changes and alters while the body remains is the position of the individual atoms with respect to each other; they may come together and may separate, and for each of togetherness and separation there are specific configurations. So if by the establishment of magnitudes this is intended, there is no dispute; but if what is intended is accidents subsisting in the body, other than its parts and the configurations of their arrangement, it is forbidden, and there is no indication for what you have mentioned. And it would only be established if the denial of the indivisible part were established. And what was mentioned—that the surface and line depend on something external to the body and to what the body depends on, so that they would be accidents—refers to what we have mentioned, for their reality according to us is the individual atoms, but in a specific position and arrangement: that they be arranged along the length without width, or along length and width without depth; and what depends on something else is that state and specific arrangement. And what was mentioned about their having positions, according to us the indication is only to the individual atoms themselves arranged in a specific arrangement; and the endpoints are non-existences and terminations, meaning that there is no other atom after those atoms.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' arguments and the mutakallimun's response regarding the nature of magnitudes (al-maqādīr: lines, surfaces, volumes). The philosophers argue that magnitudes are accidents (aʿrāḍ), not substances (jawāhir), through two forms of proof. The first is an inductive argument: magnitudes change while the body remains the same, as in the example of a candle that can be shaped round or cubical, demonstrating that the surface and lines are not parts of the body but accidents. The second is a detailed argument: a surface depends on the finitude of the body, and finitude requires proof; if the surface were an actual part, it would not depend on finitude. They also give a geometric example involving a thread forming a sphere, which depends on motion and produces points and lines, none of which are parts of the thread. The philosophers further argue that magnitudes are existent (wujūdiyya) because they can be pointed to sensorily. They acknowledge that surface, line, and point are related to finitude (which is non-existence), but this does not prove they are non-existent, as an existent can be conditioned by a non-existent. The mutakallimun respond by asserting that what changes is merely the arrangement (waḍʿ) of individual atoms (jawāhir farda). They agree that if by 'magnitudes' one means these arrangements, there is no dispute; but if one means accidents distinct from atoms and their configurations, it is denied. They argue that the philosophers' proofs only work if atomism is rejected. The mutakallimun explain that the reality of surfaces and lines is the individual atoms arranged in a specific way—along length, or length and width—and what depends on something else is that specific arrangement, not a separate accident. Furthermore, when pointing, one points to the atoms themselves, and endpoints are non-existences (iʿdām) indicating the absence of further atoms.

الجوهر الفرد
العرض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Magnitudes are separate accidents that inhere in a body.
Correction: The mutakallimun argue that magnitudes are nothing but the positions and arrangements of indivisible atoms; any change in magnitude is a change in atomic arrangement, not a change in a separate accident.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun reduce all magnitudes to the specific arrangements of indivisible atoms, denying that they are separate accidents; endpoints are simply non-existences indicating the absence of further atoms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun say that what changes is only the positions of atoms while the body remains. In your own life, when you experience change—whether of your mood, your abilities, or your circumstances—do you focus on the underlying constant (the atoms) or only the shifting surface? What would it mean for you to see the unchanging essence beneath the fluctuations? How can this perspective help you anchor your faith in the midst of constant change?
?
The philosophers thought that pointing to a magnitude proved it was a separate accident. The mutakallimun replied that you are only pointing to the atoms in their arrangement. Think about something you confidently assume exists because you can point to it—like your status, your health, or your possessions. Could it be that you are only pointing to a temporary arrangement? How does this challenge your attachment to worldly things and your trust in the permanent reality of Allah?
?
Endpoints, according to the mutakallimun, are not positive existences but mere non-existences—the absence of further atoms. When you think about the end of your life, do you imagine it as a positive thing (an end that exists) or as a termination point that is simply a non-existence? How does the Islamic view of death as the end of this worldly arrangement affect your preparation for the eternal life to come?

Arguments on the Existence of Magnitudes and Their Reduction to Atomic Arrangements
Objection and answer on the existence of magnitudes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how atomists in kalam defended the doctrine that magnitudes like surfaces and lines are not separate accidents but are reducible to arrangements of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
Magnitudes are real but not separate accidents; they are the ordered arrangements of indivisible atoms.

This passage presents a key debate in kalam: are magnitudes (surfaces, lines, points) existing accidents? The opponents argue they are real and have positions. The atomist response, which we will follow, explains that magnitudes are nothing but the configurations of indivisible atoms. Understanding this reduces complex physical concepts to atomic ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The passage first gives an argument that magnitudes must exist because they can be pointed to. Notice the sensory indication argument.
2
Understand the initial counter
The objector tries to reduce surface/line/point to termination (tanahi) and claims that doesn't prove non-existence.
3
Follow the atomist answer
The author answers by stating that only positions of atoms change; magnitudes are just these arrangements. Distinguish between two meanings of 'magnitudes'.
4
Grasp the reduction
See how the atomist reduces surfaces and lines to atomic arrays: length only gives line, length and width gives surface. Indication points to atoms, not separate accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتتوقف على قطعهما لتحدث سطحاً مستديراً هو دائرة محيط بها خط مستدير وما يتوقف ثبوته للشيء على الغير لا يكون نفسه، ولا جزءاً منه، واحتجوا على كون المقادير وجودية بأنها ذوات أوضاع يشار إليها إشارة حسية بأنها هنا، ولا إشارة إلى العدم غاية ما في الباب أن عروض السطح للجسم التعليمي، وعروض الخط للسطح، وعروض النقطة للخط إنما يكون باعتبار التناهي وهو عدم الامتداد الأخذ في جهة ما بمعنى نفاد ذلك الامتداد وانقطاعه، وهذا القدر لا يقتضي عدمية هذه الأمور لجواز أن يكون الوجودي مشروطاً بالعدمي ومتصفاً به. وأجيب بأن الذي يتغير ويتبدل مع بقاء الجسم هو وضع الجواهر المفردة بعضها مع بعض فقد تجتمع، وقد تفترق ولكل من الاجتماع والافتراق هيئات مخصوصة، فإن أريد بثبوت المقادير هذا فلا نزاع، وإن أريد أعراض قائمة بالجسم، غير أجزائه وهيئات ترتبها فممنوع، ولا دلالة لما ذكرتم عليه، وإنما يتم لو ثبت نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وما ذكر من توقف السطح والخط على أمر خارج عن الجسم وعما يتوقف عليه الجسم ليلزم كونهما عرضين فراجع إلى ما ذكرناه إذ حقيقتهما عندنا الجواهر الفردة لكن على وضع وترتيب مخصوص بأن يترتب على الطول من غير عرض، أو على الطول والعرض من غير عمق والمتوقف على الغير هو تلك الحالة والترتيب المخصوص، وما ذكر من كونها ذوات أوضاع، فعندنا الإشارة إنما هي إلى نفس الجواهر الفردة المترتبة ترتباً مخصوصاً، والنهايات إعدام وانقطاعات بمعنى أنه ليست بعد تلك الجواهر جوهر آخر.
And it depends on cutting them to produce a round surface which is a circle surrounded by a circular line. And what depends for its subsistence on another is not itself nor part of it. And they argued that magnitudes are existent because they have positions that are pointed to by sensory indication as being here, and there is no indication to non-existence. The most that can be said is that the incidence of the surface to the educational body, and the incidence of the line to the surface, and the incidence of the point to the line occurs only in consideration of termination (tanāhī), which is the absence of extension in a certain direction, i.e., the exhaustion and cessation of that extension. And this does not imply the non-existence of these things because it is possible for an existent to be conditioned by non-existence and described by it. And it was answered that what changes and alters while the body remains is the position (waḍ‘) of the indivisible atoms (al-jawāhir al-mufrada) relative to each other; they may combine and they may separate, and each combination and separation has specific configurations (hay’āt). So if what is intended by the subsistence of magnitudes is this, then there is no dispute. But if what is intended is accidents subsisting in the body, distinct from its parts and the configurations of their arrangement, then it is denied, and what you have mentioned does not prove it. And it would only be complete if the negation of the indivisible part were established. And what was mentioned concerning the dependence of the surface and line on something external to the body and what the body depends on, in order to conclude that they are accidents, refers back to what we have said, for their reality according to us is the indivisible atoms but in a specific position and arrangement: arranged in length without width, or in length and width without depth. And that which depends on another is that state and specific arrangement. And what was mentioned that they have positions: according to us, indication is only to the same indivisible atoms arranged in a specific order. And the ends (al-nīhāyāt) are non-existences and cessations, meaning that there is no other atom after those atoms.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is part of a larger argument about whether magnitudes (such as surfaces, lines, and points) are existent accidents or merely privations. The author first recounts an objection: magnitudes must be existent because they have positions that can be pointed to sensorily, and non-existence cannot be pointed to. The opponent then tries to reduce the incidence of surface, line, and point to the notion of termination (tanāhī), i.e., the absence of further extension. He argues that this does not prove they are non-existent, since an existent can be conditioned by non-existence. The author then gives the answer from the perspective of those who hold the doctrine of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard). The answer is that what changes and varies in a body is only the positions of the indivisible atoms—how they come together or separate. Each configuration is a specific arrangement. If by 'magnitudes' one means these arrangements, then there is no dispute. But if one means accidents distinct from the parts and their arrangements, then it is denied. The proof of the opponent would only hold if the indivisible part were negated. The author clarifies that according to their school, surface and line reduce to atoms arranged in a specific order (e.g., in length only, or in length and width without depth). That which depends on something else is the particular state and arrangement, not a separate accident. Indication is to the atoms themselves, not to a separate entity, and ends are merely privations—absence of further atoms.

Correction: Atomists do not deny magnitudes as real; they reinterpret them as arrangements of atoms, not as separate accidents.

Key Takeaway

The atomists argue that what changes while a body remains is only the arrangement of its parts. Consider your own soul: you remain the same person over time, yet your thoughts, moods, and choices constantly shift. Are these changes merely rearrangements of something deeper, or do they reflect new accidents?
