The First Argument Against Time: Priority and Infinite Regress
Al-Taftazani presents the first argument of the mutakallimun against time and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's first argument against time based on temporal priority leading to infinite regress, and the rebuttal that priority can be essential rather than temporal.

Core Idea
Some priority is essential, not temporal—time is not needed for every 'before'.

This lesson introduces the first of two arguments used by the mutakallimun to deny the existence of time. The argument hinges on the idea that if time existed, its parts would require a temporal priority that would lead to an infinite regress. Al-Taftazani then presents a rebuttal that distinguishes between essential priority and temporal priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the concise structure: claim, argument, rebuttal.
The rebuttal is short but decisive.
2
Identify the logical chain
Understand why the priority of parts would lead to infinite regress and how the rebuttal breaks that chain by introducing essential priority.
Essential priority does not require a temporal medium.
3
Apply the concept
Consider the example of yesterday being prior to today. Why is that priority essential and not temporal?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني في الزمان
قال: أنكره المتكلمون لوجوده:
الأول: أنه لو وجد لتقدم بعض أجزائه بالضرورة وليس إلا بالزمان فيتسلسل.
ورد بأنه بالذات فإن تقدم الأمس على اليوم لا يفتقر إلى عارض.
الثاني: الزمان إما ماض أو مستقبل ولا وجود لهما أو حاضر ولو وجد لكان غير منقسم ضرورة امتناع اجتماع أجزاء الزمان في الوجود، وحينئذ يلزم تناهي الآنات المستلزم لوجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وهذا بخلاف الحركة، فإن الموجود منها هو الحصول في الوسط وهو مستمر من المبدأ إلى المنتهي ولا يصح في الزمان للقطع بأن زمان الطوفان لا يوجد الآن، ورد أنه لا وجود لهما مطلقاً، بل في الحال وعلى التبادل.
فلا الماضي في الماضي ولا المستقبل في المستقبل لأنه يعود التقسيم السابق. أجيب بأن الموجود في أحد الأزمنة أخص من مطلق الموجود وكذب الأخص لا يستلزم كذب الأعم، فإن قيل إذا انحصر العام في عدة أمور كل منها معدوم كان معدوماً بالضرورة ولذا قالوا لا وجود لجميع الحركات الماضية من الأزل وإلا فإما في الماضي أو المستقبل أو الحال والكل محال. أجيب بمنع الانحصار، فإن من الموجودات ما لا يكون في شيء من الأزمنة كالزمان، وإنما ذلك فيما يكون زمانياً كالحركة.
:
نعم يتم انحصار الزمان في الثلاثة بل في الماضي والمستقبل لكن وجودهما في نفسهما لا يستلزم وجودهما في زمان.
The second inquiry concerning time.
He said: The mutakallimun denied its existence with [two] proofs:
First: If it existed, some of its parts would necessarily be prior [to others], and that [priority] is only by time, leading to infinite regress.
It was replied: That [priority] is by essence, for the priority of yesterday over today does not require an accident.
Second: Time is either past or future—and there is no existence for them—or present; if it existed, it would be indivisible due to the impossibility of the parts of time coexisting, and then the finitude of instants would follow, entailing the existence of an indivisible part. This is unlike motion, for what exists of it is occurrence in the middle [point], which is continuous from the beginning to the end, and this is not valid for time, because it is known that the time of the Flood does not exist now. It was replied that they [past and future] have no existence absolutely, but rather in the state [of being past or future] and in alternation.
Thus, neither the past is [existent] in the past nor the future in the future, because the previous division would return. It was answered that existence in one of the times is more specific than absolute existence, and the falsity of the specific does not entail the falsity of the general. If it is said: If the general is confined to several things each of which is non-existent, then it is necessarily non-existent; hence they said there is no existence for all past motions from eternity, otherwise it would be either in the past, future, or present, all of which are impossible. It was answered by denying the confinement, for among existents there is what is not in any of the times, such as time itself. That [confinement] applies only to what is temporal, like motion.
:
Yes, the confinement of time to the three [divisions] is complete—rather to the past and future—but their existence in themselves does not entail their existence in time.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) against the existence of time, along with the responses to them. The first argument states that if time existed, its parts would necessarily be prior to one another, but that priority would itself require time, leading to an infinite regress. The response points out that priority can be essential (by essence) rather than temporal, just as yesterday is prior to today without requiring a further time. The second argument divides time into past, future, and present; past and future do not exist, and if the present existed, it would be indivisible because parts of time cannot coexist, leading to the conclusion that instants are finite and that there is an indivisible atom of time. This is contrasted with motion, which exists as a continuous occurrence. The response to the second argument distinguishes between existence in itself and existence in a particular time: past and future have no existence as actual, but they exist in the sense of being past or future and in alternation. A further objection is raised: if the past does not exist in the past nor the future in the future, the same division would recur. The reply is that existence in a time is more specific than absolute existence; denying the specific does not deny the general. Another objection is that if the general (existence) is confined to past, future, and present, and all are non-existent, then existence itself would be non-existent. The reply denies this confinement, noting that some existents (like time itself) are not in any time; only temporal things like motion are so confined. The passage concludes that time is indeed confined to past and future, but their existence in themselves does not require them to be in a further time.

Key Terms
الزمان (time)
المتكلمون (mutakallimun)
التسلسل (infinite regress)
بالذات (by essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of priority require time.
Correction: Priority can be essential (by essence) and does not require a temporal medium.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the first argument against time?
A) Time does not have parts.
B) Priority can be by essence.
C) Time is indivisible.
D) Past and future do not exist.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's first argument fails because priority can be essential, not temporal, so no infinite regress arises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah being 'al-Awwal' (the First), do you instinctively imagine a temporal beginning? How does learning about essential priority challenge that imagination and deepen your understanding of His preeternity?
?
In your daily life, what is one priority you treat as time-sensitive but is actually essential by nature? What would change if you acted on that essential priority without waiting for the 'perfect time'?

The Second Argument Against Time: Divisions and Existence
Al-Taftazani presents the second argument of the mutakallimun against time and a series of rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's second argument against time based on its divisions (past, future, present) and the resulting problems of existence and indivisibility, along with the multiple rebuttals and objections.

Core Idea
Time's divisions do not undermine its existence; existence in itself differs from existence in a time.

The second argument is more complex, involving the nature of past, future, and present. The mutakallimun claim that past and future do not exist, and the present would be indivisible, leading to unacceptable consequences. Al-Taftazani presents several responses, distinguishing between existence in itself and existence in a particular time, and addressing objections about confinement of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: argument, first response, objection, second response, third objection, final response, and concluding remark.
The passage is dense; take it step by step.
2
Understand the initial argument and its contrast with motion
The mutakallimun argue time is either past/future (nonexistent) or present (indivisible), unlike motion which is continuous. Grasp why this is problematic.
The indivisible present leads to finite instants and an indivisible part.
3
Follow the dialectic
Trace the objections and rebuttals: first response (existence in state/alternation), then objection about past/future not existing in themselves, then response about specificity, then confinement objection, and final response about confinement applying only to temporal things.
Each rebuttal refines the understanding of existence.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Al-Taftazani concludes that time is confined to past and future, but their existence in themselves does not require existence in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني في الزمان
قال: أنكره المتكلمون لوجوده:
الأول: أنه لو وجد لتقدم بعض أجزائه بالضرورة وليس إلا بالزمان فيتسلسل.
ورد بأنه بالذات فإن تقدم الأمس على اليوم لا يفتقر إلى عارض.
الثاني: الزمان إما ماض أو مستقبل ولا وجود لهما أو حاضر ولو وجد لكان غير منقسم ضرورة امتناع اجتماع أجزاء الزمان في الوجود، وحينئذ يلزم تناهي الآنات المستلزم لوجود الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، وهذا بخلاف الحركة، فإن الموجود منها هو الحصول في الوسط وهو مستمر من المبدأ إلى المنتهي ولا يصح في الزمان للقطع بأن زمان الطوفان لا يوجد الآن، ورد أنه لا وجود لهما مطلقاً، بل في الحال وعلى التبادل.
فلا الماضي في الماضي ولا المستقبل في المستقبل لأنه يعود التقسيم السابق. أجيب بأن الموجود في أحد الأزمنة أخص من مطلق الموجود وكذب الأخص لا يستلزم كذب الأعم، فإن قيل إذا انحصر العام في عدة أمور كل منها معدوم كان معدوماً بالضرورة ولذا قالوا لا وجود لجميع الحركات الماضية من الأزل وإلا فإما في الماضي أو المستقبل أو الحال والكل محال. أجيب بمنع الانحصار، فإن من الموجودات ما لا يكون في شيء من الأزمنة كالزمان، وإنما ذلك فيما يكون زمانياً كالحركة.
:
نعم يتم انحصار الزمان في الثلاثة بل في الماضي والمستقبل لكن وجودهما في نفسهما لا يستلزم وجودهما في زمان.
The second inquiry concerning time.
He said: The mutakallimun denied its existence with [two] proofs:
First: If it existed, some of its parts would necessarily be prior [to others], and that [priority] is only by time, leading to infinite regress.
It was replied: That [priority] is by essence, for the priority of yesterday over today does not require an accident.
Second: Time is either past or future—and there is no existence for them—or present; if it existed, it would be indivisible due to the impossibility of the parts of time coexisting, and then the finitude of instants would follow, entailing the existence of an indivisible part. This is unlike motion, for what exists of it is occurrence in the middle [point], which is continuous from the beginning to the end, and this is not valid for time, because it is known that the time of the Flood does not exist now. It was replied that they [past and future] have no existence absolutely, but rather in the state [of being past or future] and in alternation.
Thus, neither the past is [existent] in the past nor the future in the future, because the previous division would return. It was answered that existence in one of the times is more specific than absolute existence, and the falsity of the specific does not entail the falsity of the general. If it is said: If the general is confined to several things each of which is non-existent, then it is necessarily non-existent; hence they said there is no existence for all past motions from eternity, otherwise it would be either in the past, future, or present, all of which are impossible. It was answered by denying the confinement, for among existents there is what is not in any of the times, such as time itself. That [confinement] applies only to what is temporal, like motion.
:
Yes, the confinement of time to the three [divisions] is complete—rather to the past and future—but their existence in themselves does not entail their existence in time.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the mutakallimun (Islamic theologians) against the existence of time, along with the responses to them. The first argument states that if time existed, its parts would necessarily be prior to one another, but that priority would itself require time, leading to an infinite regress. The response points out that priority can be essential (by essence) rather than temporal, just as yesterday is prior to today without requiring a further time. The second argument divides time into past, future, and present; past and future do not exist, and if the present existed, it would be indivisible because parts of time cannot coexist, leading to the conclusion that instants are finite and that there is an indivisible atom of time. This is contrasted with motion, which exists as a continuous occurrence. The response to the second argument distinguishes between existence in itself and existence in a particular time: past and future have no existence as actual, but they exist in the sense of being past or future and in alternation. A further objection is raised: if the past does not exist in the past nor the future in the future, the same division would recur. The reply is that existence in a time is more specific than absolute existence; denying the specific does not deny the general. Another objection is that if the general (existence) is confined to past, future, and present, and all are non-existent, then existence itself would be non-existent. The reply denies this confinement, noting that some existents (like time itself) are not in any time; only temporal things like motion are so confined. The passage concludes that time is indeed confined to past and future, but their existence in themselves does not require them to be in a further time.

Key Terms
الزمان (time)
الماضي (past)
المستقبل (future)
الحال (present)
الآن (instant)
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ (indivisible part)
الوجود (existence)
الحركة (motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Past and future are absolutely non-existent.
Correction: Past and future have existence in themselves, but not in a specific time; they exist as pastness and futurity.

Checkpoint
What is the rebuttal to the confinement objection in the second argument?
A) Time is not divisible.
B) Time itself is not in any time.
C) Motion proves time's existence.
D) Instants are infinite.

Key Takeaway
The second argument fails because time's existence is not confined to temporal divisions; time itself is not in time, and existence in itself differs from existence in a time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you dwell on your past regrets or future anxieties, you treat them as real even though they are not present. This lesson shows that past and future exist in themselves but not in time. How does that distinction challenge your emotional attachment to what is gone or yet to come? What step will you take today to ground yourself only in what actually exists now?
?
The mutakallimun argue that time itself is not in time. Your sense of time passing often feels like an absolute reality. Are you willing to question whether your experience of time is a created thing, dependent on Allah, rather than an independent dimension? How might this shift your awareness of Allah's timelessness in your next prayer?
?
You have likely assumed that existence always occurs in time. This passage teaches that existence in itself is broader than existence in time. What good deeds or intentions exist in your heart that are not yet actualized in time? How will you commit to manifesting them in the present moment, knowing that only what is present can be acted upon?

The First Proof Against Time and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes the first proof against the reality of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the reality of time and how it is refuted by distinguishing a sixth kind of priority called 'priority by essence' (al-qidam bi al-dhat).

Core Idea
Time's sequential parts do not require another time to relate them.

The mutakallimun raised several proofs against the reality of time. The first argument claims that if time existed, its sequential parts would require another time to measure their succession, leading to an infinite regress. The refutation, however, shows that the priority among parts of time is not temporal in the usual sense; it is a self-evident relation known by necessity. This introduces a distinct kind of priority called 'priority by essence' (al-qidam bi al-dhat), which blocks the regress. Let's explore this argument and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the mutakallimun's reasoning: if time exists, its parts are sequential, implying a temporal relation that requires another time to contain them, leading to infinite regress.
Notice the reference to the impossibility of simultaneous coexistence of earlier and later parts.
2
Identify the problem of regress
The proof claims that the sequence of time-parts creates a infinite chain of times. Ask yourself: why would this be problematic?
3
Understand the refutation
Read the answer: priority among time-parts is self-evident and does not require an external time. This is a sixth kind of priority called 'priority by essence'.
Contrast this with the standard five kinds of priority discussed elsewhere in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني في الزمان .
احتج المتكلمون على نفيه بوجوه :
الأول : لو وجد لكان بعض أجزائه متقدماً على البعض، للقطع بأنه ليس أمراً قار الذات مجتمع الأجزاء، بحيث يكون الحادث الآن حادثاً يوم الطوفان، بل لو وجد لم يكن إلا أمراً متقضياً متصرماً يحدث جزء منه بعد جزء، بعدية زمانية امتناع اجتماع المتأخر مع المتقدم ها هنا، وإمكانه في سائر أقسام التقدم، فيكون للزمان زمان، وينقل الكلام إليه فيتسلسل وأجيب بأن تقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض نظراً إلى ذاته من غير أن يجتمعا في الوجود معلوم بالضرورة، ككون الأمس قبل اليوم نظراً إلى مجرد مفهوميهما من غير احتياج إلى عارض، وإن سمي مثله تقدماً زمانياً فلا إشكال، وإن اشترطاكون كل من المتقدم والمتأخر في زمان فلا حصر لأقسام التقدم في الخمسة، بل التقدم فيما بين أجزاء الزمان قسم سادس يناسب أن يسمى القدم بالذات .
The second investigation concerning time. The mutakallimun argued for its non-existence by several proofs: The first: if it existed, then some of its parts would be prior to others, because it is necessarily known that it is not a stable essence whose parts are simultaneous, such that the event now would be an event on the day of the flood. Rather, if it existed, it would not be except something that passes away and decays, one part of it occurring after another, with a temporal posteriority that prevents the simultaneous occurrence of the later and the earlier here, while it is possible in other kinds of priority. Thus time would have a time, and the discourse would be transferred to it, leading to infinite regress. And it was answered that the priority of some parts of time over others with respect to its essence (dhat) without their coexisting in existence is necessarily known, like the fact that yesterday is before today with respect to their mere concepts without need for an accident. If such is called temporal priority, then there is no problem. And if one stipulates that both the prior and the posterior must be in time, then the kinds of priority are not restricted to five; rather priority among parts of time is a sixth kind appropriate to be called priority by essence (al-qidam bi al-dhat).
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the existence of time and its refutation. The mutakallimun argue that if time existed, its parts would have to be sequentially ordered, with one part preceding another. This temporal sequence implies that time itself would require another time to measure its own parts, leading to an infinite regress. The answer refutes this by pointing out that the priority among parts of time is not temporal in the sense of requiring a container time; rather, it is a self-evident relation known by necessity, like yesterday being before today conceptually. Such priority is a distinct kind (the sixth) called priority by essence (qidam bi al-dhat), and thus no regress occurs.

تقدم
تأخر
بعدية زمانية
تسلسل
قدم بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument against time necessarily forces an infinite regress, making time impossible.
Correction: The answer shows that priority among time-parts is a different kind of priority (by essence) that does not require an external time, thus the regress does not arise.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the first proof against time, what is the sixth kind of priority?
A) Temporal priority (al-taqaddum al-zamani)
B) Priority by essence (al-qidam bi al-dhat)
C) Priority by rank (al-taqaddum bi al-rutba)
D) Natural priority (al-taqaddum al-tabi'i)

Key Takeaway
The possibility of time does not lead to infinite regress because the priority among its parts is either self-evident or a distinct kind called 'priority by essence'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you encounter an argument that seems to lead to infinite regress, do you tend to reject the premise outright, or do you search for alternative explanations like the 'sixth kind' here? Which approach reflects intellectual humility, and which reflects stubbornness? How does this passage challenge your default response?
?
The refutation offers two ways out: a self-evident relation or a new category. In your own life, when a problem seems to have no solution, do you look for a simple, self-evident answer or do you create a new category of thinking? What does your tendency reveal about your trust in the sufficiency of intuitive truths versus your willingness to expand your conceptual framework?

Temporal Precedence and the Sixth Division of Priority
Author discusses temporal precedence and adds a sixth division of priority among time-parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection regarding temporal priority leading to infinite regress, the author's response, and the introduction of a sixth division of priority among time-parts.

Core Idea
Temporal parts are prior by essence, not by coexistence.

This lesson examines a subtle objection about temporal posteriority: if the later cannot coexist with the prior, wouldn't that lead to infinite regress? The author clarifies that priority among parts of time is self-evident and introduces a sixth division of priority called 'priority by essence.' This deepens our understanding of the categories of priority used in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then go through the literal translation. Let the author's own argument flow before interpreting.
Notice the dense style: the objection and answer are compressed.
2
Identify the objection and answer
Look for the infinite regress objection (if later cannot coexist with prior, then time would have a time, leading to regress). Then find how the author answers: priority among time-parts is known necessarily, like yesterday being before today.
3
Understand the sixth division
See how the author concludes that if we insist both prior and later must be within time, then we have a sixth division of priority: priority among parts of time, which is called 'priority by essence.'
This is distinct from the usual five divisions (by essence, by time, by rank, etc.).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعدية زمانية امتناع اجتماع المتأخر مع المتقدم ها هنا، وإمكانه في سائر أقسام التقدم، فيكون للزمان زمان، وينقل الكلام إليه فيتسلسل وأجيب بأن تقدم بعض أجزاء الزمان على البعض نظراً إلى ذاته من غير أن يجتمعا في الوجود معلوم بالضرورة، ككون الأمس قبل اليوم نظراً إلى مجرد مفهوميهما من غير احتياج إلى عارض، وإن سمي مثله تقدماً زمانياً فلا إشكال، وإن اشترط كون كل من المتقدم والمتأخر في زمان فلا حصر لأقسام التقدم في الخمسة، بل التقدم فيما بين أجزاء الزمان قسم سادس يناسب أن يسمى القدم بالذات.
Temporal posteriority: impossibility of the later coexisting with the prior here, and its possibility in the other divisions of priority, so time would have a time, and the discussion would transfer to it and result in an infinite regress. It was answered that the priority of some parts of time over others by virtue of themselves without their coexisting in existence is known necessarily, like yesterday being before today by virtue of their mere concepts without need for an accident. If such is called temporal priority, there is no problem. If it is conditioned that both the prior and the later be within time, then the divisions of priority are not restricted to five; rather, priority among parts of time is a sixth division appropriate to be called 'priority by essence'.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection regarding temporal posteriority. The objection argues that if something comes later in time, the earlier and later cannot coexist, leading to an infinite regress. The response clarifies that the priority of one time-part over another is self-evident and does not require coexistence. For example, 'yesterday' is prior to 'today' purely conceptually, without any need for an accidental relation. If one insists that both terms must exist within time itself, then this introduces a sixth division of priority beyond the standard five (such as priority by essence, by time, by rank, etc.), namely priority among temporal parts themselves. The author affirms that this sixth division is appropriate and should be called 'priority by essence' (al-qidam bi al-dhat).

Definition of Essential Necessity (al-Wujub al-Dhati)
Al-Taftazani defines essential necessity and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of essential necessity and how it differs from entailing existence.

Core Idea
Essential necessity does not require existence; it only implies perpetual renewal.

This lesson introduces the concept of essential necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) as defined by al-Taftazani. Understanding this term is crucial for following later arguments about time and the philosophers' proofs. The passage clarifies what essential necessity entails and what it excludes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get a feel for the definition and its nuances.
Pay attention to the key terms.
2
Identify what essential necessity precludes
The passage states that essential necessity precludes composition (tarkib) and temporality (taqaddi). Reflect on why these are excluded.
Composition means being made of parts; temporality means having a beginning or end.
3
Understand the meaning of 'perpetual through renewal'
The author says the utmost of essential necessity is that it is perpetual through the renewal of parts continuously. Consider what this implies about the nature of time.
This is a key point for later arguments about the philosophers' proofs.
4
Clarify that essential necessity does not entail existence
Note the explicit statement that essential necessity does not necessitate existence in itself. This corrects a common misunderstanding.
This nuance is critical for the discussion of possibility and necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Essential necessity, which precludes composition and temporality, because it does not entail existence with respect to its essence; its utmost is that it is perpetual through the renewal of parts in a continuous manner, and there is no impossibility in it.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage defines essential necessity (al-wujub al-dhati). The author states that essential necessity precludes composition (tarkib) and temporality (taqaddi). It does not necessitate existence in itself; rather, its utmost is that it is perpetual with continuously renewing parts. There is no impossibility in this conception. This definition sets the stage for the discussion of time, as the philosophers' proofs rely on certain notions of necessity and possibility.

The Self-Evident Nature of Time and the Ash'ari Definition
Taftazani states the self-evidence of time and presents the Ash'ari definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that time is a self-evident concept while its true essence is disputed, and that the Ash'ari definition as 'a known renewed thing measuring an unknown renewed thing' is merely ostensive and does not reveal time's quiddity.

Core Idea
Time is self-evident, but its quiddity is hidden; Ash'ari definition is merely ostensive.

Al-Taftazani begins by affirming that time is so self-evident that to deny it is to deny primary axioms. The real disagreement among scholars concerns its true nature. He then presents the view of some theologians (often identified as Ash'aris) that time is a known renewed entity used to measure other renewed events. Through examples of relative estimation, he shows that this definition fails to convey the actual essence of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening on self-evidence
Read the first two lines where Taftazani says denying time is like denying primary axioms. Notice that time is considered a necessary concept known to all.
This establishes time as an undeniable reality.
2
Identify the Ash'ari definition
Find the phrase 'فزعم البعض أنه متجدد معلوم يقدر به متجدد موهوم'. This is the Ash'ari definition: time is a known renewed thing by which an unknown renewed thing is measured.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the example of answering 'when did Zaid stand?' with 'when Amr sat'. Note how the definition relies on relative knowledge.
The passage shows time is often defined contextually.
4
Understand the critique
Read the concluding sentence: 'ولا يخفي أن ليس في هذا إفادة تصور'. Taftazani argues that this definition does not provide a true conceptualization of time's essence.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: the Ash'ari view fails to define time absolutely, leaving the quiddity obscure. This sets up the need for a different definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنكاره مجرى إنكار الأوليات وإنما الخفاء في حقيقته اختلاف المتكلمين والفلاسفة في حقيقة الزمان قال فزعموا أن المقصود التنبيه وإلا فوجود امتداد يتصف بالمضي والاستقبال ضروري يعترف به العامة وتقسيمه إلى السنين والشهور والأيام والساعات وإنما الخفاء في حقيقته فزعم البعض أنه متجدد معلوم يقدر به متجدد موهوم وربما يتعاكس بحسب علم المخاطب كما يقال حين قعد عمرو في جواب متى قام زيد وبالعكس ولا يخفي أن ليس في هذا إفادة تصور وذهب أرسطو وأتباعه إلى أنه مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم لأنه لتفاوته كم ولا متناع تألفه من الآنات المتتالية لاستلزامه الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ متصل ولعدم استقراره مقدار لهيئة غير قارة وهي الحركة ولامتناع فنائه ضرورة أن بعدية العدم لا تكون إلا بالزمان مقدراً لحركة مستديرة إذا المستقيمة يجب انقطاعها لما سيأتي ولتقدر جميع الحركات به مقدار لأسرعها الذي هو الحركة اليومية إذ الأكبر يقدر بالأصغر والأكثر بالأقل كالفرسخ بالذراع والمائة بالعشرات دون العكس ورد ذلك بأنه مع الابتناء على الأصول الفاسدة إنما يتم إن لو كان قبوله التفاوت لذاته قال فزعمت الأشاعرة القوم وإن ادعى بعضهم ظهور آنية الزمان فقد اتفقوا على خفاء ماهيته فقال كثير من المتكلمين هو متجدد معلوم مقدر به متجدد غير معلوم كما يقال آتيك عند طلوع الشمس وربما يتعاكس بحسب علم المخاطب حتى لو علم وقت قعود عمرو فقال متى قام زيد يقال في جوابه حين قعد عمرو ولو علم وقت قيام زيد فقال متى قعد عمرو يقال في جوابه حين قام زيد ولذلك يختلف تقدير المتجددات باختلاف ما يعتقد المقدر ظهور عند المخاطب كما تقول العامة للعامة اجلس يوماً والقارئ اجلس قدر ما تقرأ الفاتحة والكاتب قدر ما تكتب صحيفة والتركي قدر ما ينضج مرجل لحم ولا يخفى إذ ليس في هذا التفسير إفادة تصور ماهية الزمان وأما الفلاسفة فذهب أرسطو وأشياعه إلى أنه مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم واحتجوا على ذلك بأنه مقداري أي كم متصل أما الكمية فلقبوله المساواة واللامساواة فإن زمان دورة من الفلك مساو لزمان دورة أخرى منه وأقل من زمان دورتين وأكثر من زمان نصف دورة وأما الاتصال فإنه لو كان منفصلاً لانتهى إلى ما لا ينقسم أصلاً كوحدات العدد لأن هذا حقيقة الانفصال فيكون تألفه من الآنات المتتالية ويلزم منه الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لانطباقه على الحركة المنطبقة على المسافة ثم
Denying it is like denying primary axioms, and the obscurity is only in its essence. The disagreement of the theologians and philosophers concerning the reality of time. He said: They claimed that the intended purpose is notification; otherwise, the existence of an extension characterized by past and future is necessary (self-evident) and acknowledged by the common people, and its division into years, months, days, and hours—the obscurity is only in its essence. So some claimed that it is a known renewed thing by which an unknown renewed thing is measured, and sometimes it is reversed according to the knowledge of the addressee, as it is said 'when Amr sat' in answer to 'when did Zaid stand?' and vice versa. And it is not hidden that this does not convey a conceptualization (of its essence). And Aristotle and his followers held that it is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, because it is quantitative due to its admitting inequality, and because its composition from consecutive instants is impossible since it necessitates the indivisible part (atom) being continuous, and because it is not stable as a measure for a non-stationary form which is motion, and because its annihilation is impossible since posteriority to non-existence cannot be except by time, measuring a circular motion. As for rectilinear motion, it must cease for what will come. And all motions are measured by the fastest of them, which is the daily motion, because the greater is measured by the smaller and the more by the less, like the farsakh by the cubit and the hundred by tens, not the opposite. This was refuted by saying that, based on corrupt principles, it is only valid if it admitted inequality by itself. He said: The Ash'ari group claimed—even if some of them claimed the apparentness of the 'nows' of time, they agreed on the obscurity of its quiddity—so many of the theologians said: It is a known renewed thing by which an unknown renewed thing is measured, as it is said: 'I will come to you at sunrise.' And sometimes it is reversed according to the knowledge of the addressee, such that if he knew the time of Amr's sitting and said 'When did Zaid stand?' it would be said in answer: 'When Amr sat.' And if he knew the time of Zaid's standing and said 'When did Amr sit?' it would be said in answer: 'When Zaid stood.' Therefore the estimation of renewed things varies according to what the estimator believes is apparent to the addressee, as the common people say to the commoner: 'Sit for a day,' and to the reciter: 'Sit for the amount you recite the Fatiha,' and to the writer: 'for the amount you write a page,' and to the Turk: 'for the amount a pot of meat cooks.' And it is not hidden that this interpretation does not convey a conceptualization of the quiddity of time. As for the philosophers, Aristotle and his followers held that it is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, and they argued for that by saying it is a quantity—that is, a continuous quantity. As for quantity, because it admits equality and inequality, for the time of one revolution of the sphere is equal to the time of another revolution from it and less than the time of two revolutions and greater than the time of half a revolution. As for continuity, because if it were discrete, it would end at what is indivisible like the units of number, for this is the essence of discreteness; thus its composition would be from consecutive instants, and from that follows the indivisible part (atom) because it applies to motion which applies to distance. Then
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the nature of time (zaman). It begins by stating that denying time is like denying primary axioms—it is self-evident. The disagreement is only about its essence. The author then presents the view of some (identified later as the Ash'ari theologians) that time is a known renewed thing by which an unknown renewed thing is measured. Examples are given showing how time is estimated relative to the knowledge of the interlocutor—e.g., answering 'when did Zaid stand?' by saying 'when Amr sat'—but this does not provide a true conceptualization of time's essence. The author then presents Aristotle's view: time is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere. Supporting arguments are given: time is a continuous quantity because it admits equality/inequality (a revolution is equal to another, less than two, etc.) and because if it were discrete, it would be composed of instants, leading to the indivisible atom. Additionally, motion is measured by the fastest motion (daily motion). A refutation follows: this argument relies on corrupt principles and only works if time admitted inequality by itself. The Ash'ari position is then restated with more examples (the common people, reciter, writer, Turk cooking meat) and it is emphasized that this does not convey the quiddity of time. Finally, the philosophers' view is detailed again with the proof from continuity and quantity, ending abruptly with 'then'.

Key Terms
الزمان
الأوليات
متجدد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time can be defined simply as a measure of events.
Correction: The Ash'ari definition, while capturing common usage, fails to explain the essence of time.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is denying time compared to denying primary axioms?
A) Because time is a metaphysical concept that cannot be proven
B) Because time is self-evident and known to all people
C) Because time was invented by philosophers
D) Because time is a physical quantity

Key Takeaway
Time is self-evident but its quiddity is disputed; the Ash'ari definition is ostensive, not definitive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live every moment measured by time, yet the passage reveals that its true essence is hidden from even the greatest minds. Does this not shake your confidence in your own understanding? How will you use the time you have to seek Allah before it runs out?
?
The passage says that time is self-evident to common people, but theologians still debate its nature. Are you content to remain in a 'common' level of understanding, or will you push deeper into the knowledge of Allah's signs? What concrete step will you take today to deepen your understanding?
?
If the definition of time does not convey its essence, then what about your own existence? Are you living with a superficial understanding of your purpose, or have you delved into the reality of your servitude to Allah? What will you do to align your life with the truth that time is a fleeting gift?

The Aristotelian Definition of Time and Its Refutation
Taftazani describes and critiques the Aristotelian definition of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's definition of time as the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere, the arguments used to support it, and the Ash'ari refutation that it relies on corrupt principles.

Core Idea
Aristotle defines time as measure of celestial motion; Ash'ari reject as based on falsified cosmology.

After presenting the Ash'ari view, al-Taftazani turns to Aristotle's definition: time is the measure of the motion of the outermost sphere. He outlines arguments based on time being a continuous quantity and its incompatibility with composition from instants. However, he then presents a refutation noting that these arguments are built on corrupt principles, and reiterates the Ash'ari position that time's quiddity remains obscure.

Guided Path
1
Read Aristotle's definition
Find the statement 'وذهب أرسطو وأتباعه إلى أنه مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم'. This is Aristotle's definition of time.
2
Follow the arguments
Identify the arguments: time is a continuous quantity, cannot be composed of instants, motion is a non-stationary form, time does not perish, and all motions are measured by the fastest (daily motion).
These arguments rely on Aristotelian physics.
3
Note the refutation
Read 'ورد ذلك بأنه مع الابتناء على الأصول الفاسدة'. Taftazani dismisses these arguments as based on falsified principles.
4
Review the Ash'ari restatement
The Ash'ari view is repeated with more examples (e.g., 'sit for a day,' 'sit for the amount you recite the Fatiha'). Taftazani again says this does not convey the quiddity of time.
5
Consider the overall debate
Note that the passage ends with another iteration of the philosophical proof, suggesting the debate continues. Reflect on the inconclusive nature of the discussion.

Time, Duration, and Eternity in Philosophical Classification
Classification of time-related terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct definitions of zaman, dahr, sarmad, dawam mutlaq, azal, and abad as relations between constant and changing things.

Core Idea
Time, duration, and eternity are defined by relations between changing and constant entities.

Philosophers have a precise vocabulary for discussing time and eternity. This passage introduces six key terms: zaman, dahr, sarmad, dawam mutlaq, azal, and abad. Each term is defined by a specific relation between what changes and what remains constant. Understanding these definitions is crucial for theological debates about God's relationship to time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's classification come through.
Notice the pattern: each term is a relation between two types of entities.
2
Identify each relation pair
Identify the three definitions: zaman (changing to changing), dahr (constant to changing), sarmad (constant to constant). See how they differ.
3
Note the encompassing term and temporal directions
Observe that absolute continuity (dawam mutlaq) encompasses all. Then note that past is azal and future is abad.
This connects to theological discussions of God's eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على كل ما قالوا إن نسبة المتغير إلى المتغير هو الزمان ونسبة الثابت إلى المتغير هو الدهر، ونسبة الثابت إلى الثابت هو السرمد، ويعمها الدوام المطلق، والذي في الماضي أزل، والذي في المستقبل هو الأبد
In any case, they said: the relation of the changing to the changing is time (zaman); the relation of the constant to the changing is duration (dahr); the relation of the constant to the constant is eternity (sarmad); and absolute continuity (al-dawam al-mutlaq) encompasses them. What is in the past is pre-eternity (azal), and what is in the future is post-eternity (abad).
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents a technical classification of concepts related to time and duration as held by philosophers. It begins with the statement that the relation of a changing thing to another changing thing is called time (zaman). The relation of a constant (thabit) thing to a changing thing is called duration (dahr). The relation of a constant to a constant is called eternity (sarmad). All of these are encompassed by absolute continuity (al-dawam al-mutlaq). Additionally, the aspect referring to the past is called pre-eternity (azal), and the aspect referring to the future is called post-eternity (abad). The passage thus provides four interrelated terms that are foundational in discussions of temporality and eternity, especially in debates about God's relationship to time.

Key Terms
زمان
دهر
سرمد
دوام مطلق
أزل
أبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dahr and sarmad are synonyms for time.
Correction: Each term has a specific pair of relata: dahr relates constant to changing, sarmad relates constant to constant.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of dahr (duration)?
A) The relation of a changing thing to another changing thing
B) The relation of a constant thing to a changing thing
C) The relation of a constant thing to another constant thing
D) Absolute continuity

Key Takeaway
Time, duration, and eternity are defined by specific relations between changing and constant entities, with absolute continuity encompassing them and temporal directions distinguishing past and future.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You constantly experience change—your body ages, your thoughts shift, your circumstances fluctuate. In light of this lesson, how does knowing that God relates to you as constant to changing (dahr) affect your trust in Him when everything around you is unstable? Will you choose to anchor your heart in His constancy today, even if you cannot see it?
?
If every moment of your past (azal) and every moment of your future (abad) are encompassed by God's absolute continuity, what does that mean for the mistakes you cannot undo and the fears you have about tomorrow? Can you release control of both to God who relates to all time as constant?
?
You often live as if time is the only reality—rushing, planning, regretting. But this lesson classifies time as merely one relation among others. How would your daily decisions change if you truly believed that God's perspective is not bound by your timeline? What would you do differently this week to align with His eternal view?

Al-Imam's Critique and the Concept of Companionship
Al-Imam refutes the classification and defends the distinction between 'in time' and 'with time'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Imam's critique of the classification of 'was' and 'will be' into changing/constant and his alternative concept of companionship (ma'iyya).

Core Idea
Time-related terms only apply between like natures; true companionship transcends change and constancy.

Al-Imam sharply criticizes the earlier classification of temporal terms into 'was' and 'will be' as applied to the Eternal. He shows that such classification fails because it assumes those terms can span the divide between the changing and the constant. An objection is raised, but al-Imam's answer refines the discussion by introducing the concept of companionship (ma'iyya), which leads to a more nuanced understanding of how the Eternal relates to time.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening critique
Notice how al-Imam dismisses the classification as 'bluster devoid of substantiation'. He argues that 'was' and 'will be' must be either changing or constant, and cannot apply across categories.
This sets up the core problem.
2
Examine the objection
An objector defends the classification using the example of a person living a thousand years. Understand why the objector thinks this shows application across change/constancy is possible.
The objector uses 'lived a thousand years' as evidence.
3
Follow the answer
Al-Imam responds that the example only works because both duration and revolution are changing (mutaghayyir). Therefore it does not bridge the constant-changing divide.
Duration is time, revolution is motion—both non-persistent.
4
Understand the alternative
Al-Imam reinterprets the relationship via companionship (ma'iyya), defining dahr (constant with non-constant) and sarmad (constant with constant). This reframes the entire discussion.
This is the positive contribution of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام : وهذا تهويل خال عن التحصيل لأن ما يفهم من كان ويكون إذا كان موجوداً في الأعيان فإما أن يكون متغيراً فلا ينطبق على الثابت أو ثابتاً فلا ينطبق على المتغير، وهذا التقسيم لا يندفع بالعبارة، واعترض بأنه لا استحالة في الانطباق بين المتغير والثابت فإنا نقول : عاش فلان ألف سنة فإنا نطبق مدة بقائه على ألف دورة من الشمس والمتكلمون يقولون : القديم موجود في أزمنة مقدرة لأنها لا نهاية لأولها، والجواب أنه لا يصح حينئذ ما ذكر أن الزمان لما كان غير قار استحال أن يكون مقدار الهيئة قارة، على أن انطباق مدة البقاء على ألف دورة إنما هو من انطباق المتغير على المتغير لأن المدة زمان والدورة حركة ثم لا يخفى إذ ليس الزمان نفس النسبة، بل للمتغير الذي ينسب إليه المتغير وليس المراد مطلق النسبة، بل نسبة المعية على ما صرح به البعض، إلا أنه اقتصر من بيان هذه المعية على أنها ليست معية شيئين يقعان في زمان واحد . ثم قال : وغير الحركة إذ المتحرك إنما ينسب إلى الزمان بالحصول معه لا فيه وهذه المعية وإن كانت بقياس ثابت إلى غير ثابت فهو الدهر وإن كانت بقياس ثابت إلى ثابت فهو السرمد، وهذا الكون أعني كون الثابت مع غير الثابت والثابت مع الثابت بإزاء كون الزماينات في الزمان، فتلك المعية
The Imam said: This is bluster devoid of substantiation, because what is understood from 'was' and 'will be' when it exists in external things, it is either changing, so it does not apply to the constant, or constant, so it does not apply to the changing. This division cannot be repelled by mere expression. And it was objected that there is no impossibility in the application between the changing and the constant, for we say: So-and-so lived a thousand years, so we apply the duration of his existence to a thousand revolutions of the sun. And the theologians say: The Eternal exists in estimated times because they have no beginning. The answer is that it does not hold, then, what was mentioned: that since time is non-persistent, it is impossible for it to be the measure of a persistent state. Moreover, the application of the duration of existence to a thousand revolutions is only from the application of the changing to the changing, because the duration is time and the revolution is motion. Then it is not hidden, for time is not the relation itself, but rather [time is] that to which the changing is related, and the intent is not the absolute relation, but the relation of companionship (ma'iyya), as some have stated, except that they limited the explanation of this companionship to its not being the companionship of two things occurring in one time. Then he said: And non-motion? When the moving thing is related to time by occurring with it not in it. This companionship, if it is by measuring a constant to a non-constant, it is dahr; and if by measuring a constant to a constant, it is sarmad. This existence — I mean the existence of the constant with the non-constant and the constant with the constant — is in exchange for the existence of times in time, so that companionship.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the previously presented classification. Al-Imam (the author) begins by declaring the classification to be 'bluster devoid of substantiation'. His argument is that what is understood from the words 'was' and 'will be', when applied to actual existents, must be either changing or constant. If it is changing, it cannot be applied to something constant; if it is constant, it cannot be applied to something changing. The classification cannot be rescued by mere wording. Then an objection is raised: there is no impossibility in applying such terms across the changing-constant divide; for instance, we say 'so-and-so lived a thousand years', applying his duration to solar revolutions. Theologians also say the Eternal exists in estimated times without beginning. The answer provided is that such application only works when both relata are changing: the duration is time, the revolution is motion, so it is changing-to-changing. The passage then clarifies that time is not the relation itself but something else, and the intended relation is that of companionship (ma'iyya). Finally, al-Imam explains that the companionship of a constant with a non-constant yields dahr, and of a constant with a constant yields sarmad, thus reinterpreting the classification in terms of companionship rather than the problematic 'in time'.

يكون
متغير
ثابت
المعية
الانطباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection disproves al-Imam's critique because we commonly say 'lived a thousand years'.
Correction: al-Imam's answer shows that such an application only works when both relata are changing (the person's life duration is applied to changing revolutions), so it does not bridge the constant-changing divide.

Checkpoint
What is the answer given to the objection that we can apply 'a thousand years' to a person's life?
A) That is an invalid comparison
B) That application is of changing to changing because both duration and revolution are dynamic
C) The person's existence is constant
D) The theologians reject such talk

Key Takeaway
Al-Imam refutes the simplistic classification of temporal terms and instead proposes a framework of companionship (ma'iyya) to account for the relationship between the Eternal and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence is not contained by time but rather 'companion' to it in a way that transcends your measurements, how often do you actually live as if He is bigger than your timeline? When was the last time you waited on Him without trying to force His timing into your schedule? Will you commit this week to pause before planning and acknowledge that He is not bound by your 'when'?
?
Al-Imam distinguished between changing and constant existence. Much of your life is a stream of changes—moods, health, circumstances. But you claim belief in a Constant, Unchanging God. How deeply does that belief affect your stability when everything else shifts? Do your emotions and reactions betray that you trust the Constant less than the changing things? Starting tomorrow, when you feel anxiety about change, will you consciously remind yourself: 'I am with the One who does not change'?
?
The passage says companionship (ma‘iyya) is not two things occurring in the same time, but a relation of measurement. Your relationship with God is meant to be this intimate companionship—not a co-location in time, but a real connection beyond time. Is that how you experience your prayer? Your dua? Or have you reduced your connection to a slot in your daily schedule? If you were to treat your next prayer as a moment of dahr or sarmad—a companionship outside of time—what would you do differently?

Definitions of Dahr, Sarmad, Azal, and Abad
Defines al-dahr, al-sarmad, al-azal, al-abad and their relation to al-dawam al-mutlaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct meanings of dahr, sarmad, azal, abad, and how they relate to absolute perpetuity.

Core Idea
Time in Islamic theology has precision: every term points to a specific relation.

This passage from a classical kalam text clarifies four key temporal terms often used in discussions of God's eternity and creation. By understanding these distinctions, you will grasp how theologians talk about time without falling into confusion. Let's read the passage and then unpack each term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the passage flows from one definition to the next.
2
Identify each term and its definition
The passage gives definitions for dahr, sarmad, azal, and abad. Find each one and note the specific relation it describes.
3
Understand the overarching concept
The passage mentions that absolute perpetuity (al-dawam al-mutlaq) encompasses these terms. Reflect on how azal and abad are temporal aspects of this absolute perpetuity.
This connects to the idea that God's eternity is beyond all temporal relations.
4
Reflect on why these distinctions matter
Ask yourself why theologians would need separate terms for different temporal relations. The commentary will help you see how this avoids confusion.
This relates to the common misunderstanding that these terms are interchangeable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى المتغير هو الدهر، ونسبة الثابت إلى الثابت هو السرمد، ويعمها الدوام المطلق، والذي في الماضي أزل، والذي في المستقبل هو الأبد.
The relation to the changing is dahr, the relation of the constant to the constant is sarmad, and the absolute perpetuity encompasses them, and that which is in the past is azal, and that which is in the future is abad.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage defines four key temporal terms in Islamic theology. It classifies each term based on its object of relation: dahr is the relation of something to mutable things, sarmad is the relation of a constant to another constant, azal is past eternity, and abad is future eternity. All these are encompassed by absolute perpetuity (al-dawam al-mutlaq), which is the overarching concept.

Key Terms
الدهر
السرمد
الأزل
الأبد
الدوام المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All these terms mean the same thing and are interchangeable.
Correction: Each term refers to a specific temporal relation: dahr (relation to mutable), sarmad (constant to constant), azal (past eternity), abad (future eternity). They are distinct but encompassed by absolute perpetuity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, al-dahr is the relation to which of the following?
A) the constant
B) the changing
C) the absolute
D) the non-existent

Key Takeaway
Absolute perpetuity encompasses all temporal relations: dahr (relation to mutable), sarmad (constant to constant), azal (past), and abad (future).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live in the realm of dahr—constantly changing. Does the reality of al-abad (the eternal future) change how you prioritize your actions today? Will you invest in what lasts forever or only in what passes?
?
God's relation to Himself is sarmad—unchanging and constant. But your faith fluctuates. What is one concrete step you can take today to make your devotion more constant, reflecting His constancy?
?
You are here because of God's decree in azal (past eternity). Do you live as if that decree has no bearing on your choices, or do you submit to it? Will you seek to align your will with His eternal decree starting now?

Imam al-Razi's Critique of Temporal Definitions
Critique and objection to temporal definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi rejects the previous definitions of 'was' and 'will be' as mere bluster.

Core Idea
Definitions that try to cover both changing and constant entities are logically flawed.

Imam al-Razi opens with a sharp critique of the definitions of temporal terms presented earlier. He argues that the terms 'was' and 'will be' cannot simultaneously apply to changing and constant entities, making the definitions empty. He then presents an objection that attempts to defend the application, setting up his deeper analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the Imam's initial critique
Read the first part of the passage where the Imam calls the definitions 'bluster' and gives a division based on changing vs. constant.
Focus on the logical division: either changing or constant.
2
Identify the objection
Notice the objection that starts with 'An objection was made...' and see how it challenges the Imam's critique.
The objector uses the example of a person living a thousand years.
3
Reflect on the tension
Consider why the objector thinks the division is not a problem, and how the Imam might respond.
This sets up the need for a more precise answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام : وهذا تهويل خال عن التحصيل لأن ما يفهم من كان ويكون إذا كان موجوداً في الأعيان فإما أن يكون متغيراً فلا ينطبق على الثابت أو ثابتاً فلا ينطبق على المتغير، وهذا التقسيم لا يندفع بالعبارة، واعترض بأنه لا استحالة في الانطباق بين المتغير والثابت فإنا نقول : عاش فلان ألف سنة فإنا نطبق مدة بقائه على ألف دورة من الشمس والمتكلمون يقولون : القديم موجود في أزمنة مقدرة لأنها لا نهاية لأولها ، والجواب أنه لا يصح حينئذ ما ذكر أن الزمان لما كان غير قار استحال أن يكون مقدار الهيئة قارة، على أن انطباق مدة البقاء على ألف دورة إنما هو من انطباق المتغير على المتغير لأن المدة زمان والدورة حركة ثم لا يخفى إذ ليس الزمان نفس النسبة، بل للمتغير الذي ينسب إليه المتغير وليس المراد مطلق النسبة، بل نسبة المعية على ما صرح به البعض، إلا أنه اقتصر من بيان هذه المعية على أنها ليست معية شيئين يقعان في زمان واحد . ثم قال : وغير الحركة إذ المتحرك إنما ينسب إلى الزمان بالحصول معه لا فيه وهذه المعية وإن كانت بقياس ثابت إلى غير ثابت فهو الدهر وإن كانت بقياس ثابت إلى ثابت فهو السرمد، وهذا الكون أعني كون الثابت مع غير الثابت والثابت مع الثابت بإزاء كون الزماينات في الزمان، فتلك المعية كأنها متى للأمور الثابتة ولا يتوهم في الدهر ولا في السرمد امتداد، وإلا لكان مقداراً بالحركة، ثم الزمان كمعلول للدهر والدهر كمعلول للسرمد، فإنه لولا دوام نسبة علل الأجسام إلى مبادئها ما وجدت الأجسام فضلا عن حركاتها، ولولا دوام نسبة الزمان إلى مبدأ الزمان لم يتحقق الزمان .
The Imam said: This is a bluster devoid of substance, because what is understood from 'was' and 'will be' if it exists in external entities, then either it is changing and does not apply to the constant, or it is constant and does not apply to the changing. This division cannot be refuted by mere words. An objection was made that there is no impossibility in the application between the changing and the constant, for we say: So-and-so lived a thousand years, so we apply the duration of his existence to a thousand cycles of the sun, and the theologians say: The eternal exists in measured times because they have no beginning. The answer is that then what was mentioned is not valid, because since time is non-stationary, it is impossible for the measure of a stationary structure to be stationary. Moreover, the application of the duration of existence to a thousand cycles is actually an application of the changing to the changing, because the duration is time and the cycle is motion. Then it is not hidden that time is not the relation itself, but rather belongs to the changing thing to which the changing is related, and the intended meaning is not the absolute relation, but rather the relation of accompaniment (ma'iyya) as some have stated, except that they limited their explanation of this accompaniment to it not being the accompaniment of two things occurring in one time. Then he said: And not motion, for the moving thing is related to time by occurring with it, not in it. This accompaniment: if it is a comparison of a constant to a non-constant, then it is dahr; if it is a comparison of a constant to a constant, then it is sarmad. And this existence—I mean the existence of the constant with the non-constant and the constant with the constant—is opposite to the existence of temporal things in time. So that accompaniment is as if it is a 'when' for constant things, and no extension is imagined in dahr or in sarmad, otherwise it would be measured by motion. Then time is like an effect of dahr, and dahr is like an effect of sarmad; for without the permanence of the relation of the causes of bodies to their principles, bodies would not exist, let alone their motions, and without the permanence of the relation of time to the principle of time, time would not be realized.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's critique of the earlier definitions, followed by an objection and a detailed answer. The Imam first dismisses the definitions as 'bluster' because the terms 'was' and 'will be' apply either to changing or constant entities, but the previous definitions try to apply them to both. An objector gives the example of a person living a thousand years, claiming that we apply a constant duration to changing cycles. The answer refines the concept: time is non-stationary, so it cannot be a measure of a stationary structure. Moreover, the example actually applies changing to changing (duration is time, cycles are motion). The Imam then explains that the intended relation is one of accompaniment (ma'iyya), not absolute relation. He further elaborates that dahr is the accompaniment of a constant with a non-constant, while sarmad is that of a constant with a constant. These accompaniments have no extension, otherwise they would be measured by motion. Finally, a causal hierarchy is described: time is an effect of dahr, and dahr is an effect of sarmad, emphasizing the dependence of temporal existence on eternal principles.

Key Terms
تهويل
انطباق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Imam al-Razi calls the definitions 'bluster'?
A) They are too complex
B) They try to apply terms to both changing and constant entities simultaneously
C) They ignore the existence of motion
D) They are too simplistic

Key Takeaway
The initial division of changing vs. constant challenges the coherence of temporal definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah always exists' or 'Allah is eternal,' do you ever pause to consider whether your words are logically coherent when applied to a Being beyond time? How often do you examine the precision of your statements about Allah? Will you commit to thinking more carefully before uttering such phrases?
?
Imam al-Razi calls a definition 'bluster devoid of substance.' Is there any belief or assumption you hold about religion that, upon honest examination, might be based on similarly vague or contradictory definitions? Are you willing to identify and discard such intellectual laziness?
?
The objection in the lesson shows how easy it is to think a counterexample refutes a logical point. In your own life, have you ever rejected a truth because you found an apparent counterexample without deeply understanding the original argument? Will you train yourself to first fully grasp a position before dismissing it?

The Answer and the Concept of Ma'iyya
Answer to objection and introduction of ma'iyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi refutes the objection and introduces the concept of accompaniment (ma'iyya).

Core Idea
Time is non-stationary, so it cannot measure stationary structures; true relation is accompaniment.

In response to the objection, the Imam provides a detailed answer. He argues that time is non-stationary, so it cannot be a measure of a stationary structure. Moreover, the example actually involves two changing things. He then clarifies that the intended relation is not absolute but one of accompaniment (ma'iyya), a key concept in the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer to the objection
Read the passage starting with 'The answer is...' and see how the Imam refutes the objection.
Pay attention to the concept of 'ghayr qarr' (non-stationary).
2
Focus on why time cannot measure stationary structures
Understand the Imam's reasoning that because time is non-stationary, it cannot be a measure of a stationary structure.
This is a key logical move.
3
Notice the shift to ma'iyya
The Imam reinterprets the relation as accompaniment (ma'iyya) rather than absolute relation.
Ma'iyya will be further defined in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Imam said: This is a bluster devoid of substance, because what is understood from 'was' and 'will be' if it exists in external entities, then either it is changing and does not apply to the constant, or it is constant and does not apply to the changing. This division cannot be refuted by mere words. An objection was made that there is no impossibility in the application between the changing and the constant, for we say: So-and-so lived a thousand years, so we apply the duration of his existence to a thousand cycles of the sun, and the theologians say: The eternal exists in measured times because they have no beginning. Then he said: And not motion, for the moving thing is related to time by occurring with it, not in it. This accompaniment: if it is a comparison of a constant to a non-constant, then it is dahr; if it is a comparison of a constant to a constant, then it is sarmad. And this existence—I mean the existence of the constant with the non-constant and the constant with the constant—is opposite to the existence of temporal things in time. This passage presents Imam al-Razi's critique of the earlier definitions, followed by an objection and a detailed answer. The Imam first dismisses the definitions as 'bluster' because the terms 'was' and 'will be' apply either to changing or constant entities, but the previous definitions try to apply them to both. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is a stationary container for events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Because time is non-stationary and you are constantly in motion, do you evaluate your spiritual state at every moment, or do you rely on past good deeds as if you are stationary? What step will you take today to accompany your actions with sincere intention all day long?
?
The Imam teaches that the intended relation is accompaniment (ma'iyya), not just any relation. In your relationship with Allah, are you content with a mere historical connection—like an event long past—or do you strive for ongoing accompaniment with Him in every changing circumstance? How will you renew your awareness of His presence this hour?
?
If time cannot measure stationary structures, have you become 'stationary' in your faith—stuck in a past experience or a fixed practice—while the world moves? What one change can you make to move with time while staying anchored to truth?

Dahr, Sarmad, and the Causal Hierarchy
Definitions of dahr, sarmad, and causal hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of dahr and sarmad and how they relate to time in a causal hierarchy.

Core Idea
Dahr and sarmad are modes of accompaniment without extension, and they are causal foundations of time.

Having introduced the concept of accompaniment (ma'iyya), the Imam now distinguishes two types: dahr (constant with non-constant) and sarmad (constant with constant). He emphasizes that these have no extension, otherwise they would be measured by motion. Finally, he describes a causal hierarchy: time depends on dahr, and dahr depends on sarmad, grounding temporal existence in eternal principles.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish dahr and sarmad
Read the definitions: dahr is constant with non-constant; sarmad is constant with constant.
Think of them as types of accompaniment.
2
Understand why they have no extension
The Imam says no extension is imagined in dahr or sarmad, otherwise they would be measured by motion.
Extension belongs to time, not these.
3
Trace the causal hierarchy
Note the chain: sarmad → dahr → zaman (time). Understand why each is dependent.
This shows the derived nature of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام : وهذا تهويل خال عن التحصيل لأن ما يفهم من كان ويكون إذا كان موجوداً في الأعيان فإما أن يكون متغيراً فلا ينطبق على الثابت أو ثابتاً فلا ينطبق على المتغير، وهذا التقسيم لا يندفع بالعبارة، واعترض بأنه لا استحالة في الانطباق بين المتغير والثابت فإنا نقول : عاش فلان ألف سنة فإنا نطبق مدة بقائه على ألف دورة من الشمس والمتكلمون يقولون : القديم موجود في أزمنة مقدرة لأنها لا نهاية لأولها ، والجواب أنه لا يصح حينئذ ما ذكر أن الزمان لما كان غير قار استحال أن يكون مقدار الهيئة قارة، على أن انطباق مدة البقاء على ألف دورة إنما هو من انطباق المتغير على المتغير لأن المدة زمان والدورة حركة ثم لا يخفى إذ ليس الزمان نفس النسبة، بل للمتغير الذي ينسب إليه المتغير وليس المراد مطلق النسبة، بل نسبة المعية على ما صرح به البعض، إلا أنه اقتصر من بيان هذه المعية على أنها ليست معية شيئين يقعان في زمان واحد . ثم قال : وغير الحركة إذ المتحرك إنما ينسب إلى الزمان بالحصول معه لا فيه وهذه المعية وإن كانت بقياس ثابت إلى غير ثابت فهو الدهر وإن كانت بقياس ثابت إلى ثابت فهو السرمد، وهذا الكون أعني كون الثابت مع غير الثابت والثابت مع الثابت بإزاء كون الزماينات في الزمان، فتلك المعية كأنها متى للأمور الثابتة ولا يتوهم في الدهر ولا في السرمد امتداد، وإلا لكان مقداراً بالحركة، ثم الزمان كمعلول للدهر والدهر كمعلول للسرمد، فإنه لولا دوام نسبة علل الأجسام إلى مبادئها ما وجدت الأجسام فضلا عن حركاتها، ولولا دوام نسبة الزمان إلى مبدأ الزمان لم يتحقق الزمان .
The Imam said: This is a bluster devoid of substance, because what is understood from 'was' and 'will be' if it exists in external entities, then either it is changing and does not apply to the constant, or it is constant and does not apply to the changing. This division cannot be refuted by mere words. An objection was made that there is no impossibility in the application between the changing and the constant, for we say: So-and-so lived a thousand years, so we apply the duration of his existence to a thousand cycles of the sun, and the theologians say: The eternal exists in measured times because they have no beginning. The answer is that then what was mentioned is not valid, because since time is non-stationary, it is impossible for the measure of a stationary structure to be stationary. Moreover, the application of the duration of existence to a thousand cycles is actually an application of the changing to the changing, because the duration is time and the cycle is motion. Then it is not hidden that time is not the relation itself, but rather belongs to the changing thing to which the changing is related, and the intended meaning is not the absolute relation, but rather the relation of accompaniment (ma'iyya) as some have stated, except that they limited their explanation of this accompaniment to it not being the accompaniment of two things occurring in one time. Then he said: And not motion, for the moving thing is related to time by occurring with it, not in it. This accompaniment: if it is a comparison of a constant to a non-constant, then it is dahr; if it is a comparison of a constant to a constant, then it is sarmad. And this existence—I mean the existence of the constant with the non-constant and the constant with the constant—is opposite to the existence of temporal things in time. So that accompaniment is as if it is a 'when' for constant things, and no extension is imagined in dahr or in sarmad, otherwise it would be measured by motion. Then time is like an effect of dahr, and dahr is like an effect of sarmad; for without the permanence of the relation of the causes of bodies to their principles, bodies would not exist, let alone their motions, and without the permanence of the relation of time to the principle of time, time would not be realized.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's critique of the earlier definitions, followed by an objection and a detailed answer. The Imam first dismisses the definitions as 'bluster' because the terms 'was' and 'will be' apply either to changing or constant entities, but the previous definitions try to apply them to both. An objector gives the example of a person living a thousand years, claiming that we apply a constant duration to changing cycles. The answer refines the concept: time is non-stationary, so it cannot be a measure of a stationary structure. Moreover, the example actually applies changing to changing (duration is time, cycles are motion). The Imam then explains that the intended relation is one of accompaniment (ma'iyya), not absolute relation. He further elaborates that dahr is the accompaniment of a constant with a non-constant, while sarmad is that of a constant with a constant. These accompaniments have no extension, otherwise they would be measured by motion. Finally, a causal hierarchy is described: time is an effect of dahr, and dahr is an effect of sarmad, emphasizing the dependence of temporal existence on eternal principles.

Key Terms
دهر
سرمد
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dahr and sarmad are just other terms for time with extension.
Correction: They are modes of accompaniment without extension.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between time (zaman) and dahr according to the Imam?
A) Time and dahr are identical
B) Time is an effect of dahr
C) Dahr is an effect of time
D) They are unrelated

Key Takeaway
Dahr and sarmad are non-extended accompaniments that serve as the causal foundations of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about the things in your life that are constant—your faith, your identity, your values—and the things that are constantly changing—your circumstances, emotions, possessions. Which do you invest your soul in? The passage tells us that without the constant (principles), the changing (bodies and time) would not exist. What does that say about where you should anchor your hope? Are you living as if the changing is more real than the constant?
?
The passage describes a hierarchy: time depends on dahr, and dahr depends on sarmad. This means that everything you experience in time is ultimately rooted in an eternal, non-extended reality. When you feel anxious about time slipping away or about deadlines and aging, does your heart turn to what is beyond time? Or do you remain trapped in the motion of the world? How can you actively remind yourself of the sarmad—the eternal accompaniment—so that your life is lived in light of the timeless?
