Ibn Sina on Time, Dahr, and Sarmad
Ibn Sina’s relational definitions of zaman, dahr, and sarmad, with a critical note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's tripartite classification of temporal concepts (zaman, dahr, sarmad) and the critique of its obscurity.

Core Idea
Time is relative: three modes of consideration define existence.

Ibn Sina offers a precise relational definition of time, eternity, and perpetuity, distinguishing them according to the subjects considered. However, the author of the commentary, echoing Imam al-Razi, finds the explanation lacking clarity. This lesson unpacks the definitions and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the original formulation.
Note the precise terms: زمان, دهر, سرمد.
2
Identify the three definitions
Find how Ibn Sina defines each term based on the consideration (اعتبار) of changing and constant things.
Each term is a relation between two categories.
3
Understand the relationship between dahr and sarmad
Ibn Sina says dahr in itself is part of sarmad, but when related to time it is called dahr. Try to see why.
This is a nested relation.
4
Consider the critique
The passage concludes that this explanation appears meaningless. Reflect on why a great thinker like Ibn Sina might be criticized.
This shows the importance of clear definitions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابن سينا: إن اعتبار أحوال المتغيرات مع المتغيرات هو الزمان واعتبار أحوال الأشياء الثابتة مع الأشياء المتغيرة هو الدهر، ومع الأشياء الثابتة هو السرمد، والدهر في ذاته من السرمد وهو بالقياس إلى الزمان، يسمى دهراً وهو أن الدهر في نفسه شيء ثابت إلا أنه إذا نسب إلى الزمان الذي هو متغير ذاته دهراً، هذا ما وقع إلينا من شرح هذا الكلام والظاهر أنه ليس له معنى يحصل على ما قال الإمام .
And Ibn Sina said: Indeed, the consideration of the states of changing things with respect to changing things is time (zaman), and the consideration of the states of constant things with respect to changing things is dahr, and with respect to constant things is sarmad. And dahr in itself belongs to sarmad, and in relation to time it is called dahr. It is that dahr in itself is a constant thing, but when it is related to time which is changing in itself, it is dahr. This is what has come to us of explanation of this statement, and it appears that it has no meaningful content as the Imam said.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage records Ibn Sina's position on the three terms. He defines time (zaman) as the consideration of changing things relative to each other. Dahr is the consideration of constant things relative to changing things, and sarmad is the consideration of constant things relative to each other. He further explains that dahr in itself is part of sarmad, but when related to time it is called dahr. The author (al-Taftazani?) then comments that this explanation is unclear and seems to lack meaningful content, echoing Imam al-Razi's earlier critique.

Key Terms

دهر
سرمد
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's definitions are clear and universally accepted.
Correction: The passage itself notes that the explanation appears obscure and without meaning, as stated by al-Razi.

Checkpoint

A) zaman
B) dahr
C) sarmad
D) azal

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's relational framework for time, dahr, and sarmad is philosophically ambitious but criticized as obscure by later scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does recognizing the limits of your temporal perspective change the way you approach your daily struggles? What concrete step can you take today to ground yourself in the unchanging reality of Allah's eternal nature?
?
The author dismisses Ibn Sina's definitions as having no meaningful content. Did you feel a similar confusion while reading? Time as Measure of Motion and the Objection of Successive Instants
Defends time as measure of motion (qat') and faces objection of tatali' al-anat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Avicennan view of time as the measure of motion in the sense of 'cutting', the distinction between real and imaginary aspects of time, and the objection that this view leads to the theory of successive instants.

Core Idea
Time is real only in its successive parts; the whole is imaginary.

This passage defends a key philosophical distinction about time. The author argues that time is the measure of motion taken as a process of 'cutting'—each part exists successively, not simultaneously. An objector then raises a challenge: this view seems to entail an infinite succession of indivisible instants. Let's examine the argument and the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the key terms like 'qat' (cutting), 'ghayr qar' (non-stationary), and 'wahmi' (imaginary).
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off just as the objection is being stated.
2
Identify the author's main claim
The author states that time is the measure of motion in the sense of 'cutting' (qat'). Understand what 'cutting' means here: motion as a successive process, not a whole.
3
Distinguish real vs. imaginary time
Notice the distinction: the extended whole from beginning to end is imaginary (wahmi), while the parts that exist successively are real. This is parallel to motion itself.
The author uses an analogy: an indivisible thing flows to produce time, just as 'being in the middle' produces motion.
4
Recognize the objection
The objector claims that this view implies 'tatali' al-anat' (succession of instants). Understand why: if time is made of successive non-stationary parts, each part might be an indivisible instant leading to an infinite regress.
The passage ends before the answer is given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن الثاني : فبأنا نختار أن الزمان مقدار للحركة بمعنى القطع وهي أمر غير قار يوجد منها جزء فجزء من غير أن يحصل جزءان دفعة وهذا معنى وجودها في الخارج، وإنما الوهمي هو المجموع الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهى فكذا مقدارها الذي هو الزمان يكون بحسب المجموع وهمياً لا يوجد منه جزءان دفعة بل لا يزال يتجدد وينصرم ويوجد منه شيء فشيء، وهذا يقال : إن هناك أمراً غير منقسم يفعل سيلانه (الزمان) كما أن في الحركة معنى هو الكون في الوسط يفعل سيلانه الحركة بمعنى القطع . واعترض بأن هذا قول بتتالي الآنات لأن ذلك الأمر الغير المنقسم ليس
As for the second [objection]: we choose that time is the measure of motion in the sense of cutting (qat'), which is a non-stationary thing; part after part of it exists without two parts occurring at once, and this is the meaning of its existence externally. The imaginary is only the extended whole from beginning to end. So likewise its measure, which is time, is imaginary according to the whole, not existing as two parts at once, but rather it is constantly renewed and passes away, and something of it exists thing after thing. And it is said: there is an indivisible thing that produces its flowing (of time), just as in motion there is a meaning of 'being in the middle' that produces its flowing, motion in the sense of cutting. And it was objected that this is a statement of the succession of instants (tatali' al-anat), because that indivisible thing is not...
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defends the Avicennan view that time (zaman) is the measure of motion taken in the sense of 'cutting' (qat'), meaning the motion itself as a non-stationary process. The author argues that only the whole collection of parts is imaginary; in reality, parts of time exist successively. An analogy is given: an indivisible thing (the 'now' or instant) flows to produce time, just as 'being in the middle' flows to produce motion. An objection is raised that this view implies the theory of successive instants (tatali' al-anat), which is problematic. The passage cuts off before presenting the answer.

الحركة

Misconception: Time is a continuous, extended whole that exists as a single entity.
Correction: The author argues that only the successive parts of time exist in reality; the whole from beginning to end is merely an imaginary construct.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson says time exists only part after part, never two parts at once. Right now, you are in one part. If this part were your last, would you be content with what you have done in the previous parts? What specific deed have you been postponing that you can do in this very part?
?
You habitually plan for a future whole—next week, next year—but this lesson teaches that the extended whole is merely imaginary. Which of your plans are you assuming will definitely exist, and what does that assumption say about your reliance on Allah?
?
The objection of infinite instants (tatali' al-anat) shows that thinking too deeply about time can lead to confusion. But the simple truth is: you only have this instant. Will you use it to draw closer to Allah, or will you let it slip away like the previous imaginary whole?

Time as a Continuous Quantity: Imaginary Parts and Mental Extension
Al-Taftazani responds to the objection that time has no existence because its parts are imaginary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time is considered a continuous quantity divisible only in the mind, and how this leads to the conclusion that priority and posteriority are mental considerations.

Core Idea
Time's parts are imaginary; priority and posteriority are mental, not external realities.

This lesson addresses the first objection to the nature of time: if time has no actual parts, how can it exist? Al-Taftazani explains that time is a continuous quantity whose divisibility is only in imagination, unlike distance and motion which have external continuous existence. He then argues that priority and posteriority belong only to the mental extension of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Taftazani uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection that time has no existence because its parts are imaginary. Note how the author presents it.
3
Examine the analogies
The author uses distance and motion as analogies. Understand why they are different from time in terms of external existence.
Distance's continuous whole exists externally; motion has a continuous thing. Time's extension is purely mental.
4
Understand the answer
The answer states that in the mind there is an extension arising from a non-fixed external thing. This extension, if supposed divided, gives priority and posteriority without requiring another time.
5
Conclude the mental nature
Since the parts exist only in the intellect, priority and posteriority are also mental. If they were external, time would be fixed in essence, which is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوجد غير الآن، وأجيب بأنه لا أجزاء هناك بالفعل لأن الزمان كمية متصلة يعرض لها التجزؤ والانقسام بحسب الغرض والوهم دون الخارج فورد الإشكال بأنه لا وجود للزمان حينئذ لأن نفس الامتداد موهوم والجزء معدوم فماذا منه؟ وهذا بخلاف المسافة فإن أجزاءها وإن لم تكن بالفعل إلا أن المجموع المتصل الذي يتجزأ في الوهم موجود في الخارج، وبخلاف الحركة فإنه يوجد منها أمر مستمر هو الكون في الوسط من غير لزوم محال . وأجيب بأن المراد أن في الفعل امتداد لا وجود له في الخارج لكنه بحيث لو فرض وجوده وتجزؤه عرضت لأجزائه المفروضة قبليات وبعديات متجددة ومنصرفة، ولا يكون الامتداد في العقل كذلك إلا إذا كان في الخارج شيء غير قار يحصل في العقل بحسب استمراره وعدم استقراره، ذلك الامتداد الذي إذا فرض تجزية كان لحوق التقدم والتأخر لأجزائه المفروضة لذاتها من غير اقتضاء زمان آخر وكذا معيته للحركة وإذا لا وجود للجزأين معاً إلا في العقل لزم كون القبلية والبعدية العارضتين لهما كذلك، ولهذا يعرضان للعدم كيف ولو وجدتا في الخارج وهما متضايفان لزم وجود معروضيهما معاً في الخارج، ويلزم كون الزمان قار الذات، وما يقال من أن الموجود في الخارج من الزمان معروض للقبلية والبعدية فمجاز والمراد أنه متعلق بهما بمعنى أنهما بسببه يعرضان للأجزاء المفروضة للزمان المعقول، هذا غاية تحقيقهم في هذا المقام دفعاً للإشكالات الموردة من قبل الإمام، مثل أن قبلية عدم الحادث على وجوده لو اقتضت زماناً كانت قبلية الأمس على الغد، ومعية الحركة للزمان كذلك، وأن القبلية والبعدية لو وجدتا لا متنع اتصاف العدم بهما، ولكان وجودهما بالزمان وتسلسل وللزم وجود معروضيهما معاً ضرورة كونهما متضايفين فيكون الزمان قار الذات لاجتماع أجزائه المفروضة للقبلية والبعدية، ولو كانتا من الاعتبارات العقلية التي لا وجود لها في الأعيان لم يلزم وجود معروضيهما من الخارج فلم يدل على وجود الزمان وأن أجزاء الزمان إما أن تكون متماثلة فتمتنع اختلافها بالقبلية والبعدية الذاتيتين، أو متخالفة يكون الزمان متصلاً، وأنت خبير بأن قولهم لا بد في الخارج من أمر غير قار يحصل منه في العقل ذلك الامتداد مجرد ادعاء لجواز أن يحصل لا عن موجود، أو عن موجود قار بحسب ما له من النسب والإضافات إلى المتغيرات على ما سيجيء وأما عن الثالث فبأن القطع بوجود امتداد به التقدم والتأخر ومنه الماضي والمستقبل على تقدير أن لا يكون فلك ولا حركة أصلاً ، أو يكون له عدم سابق، أو لاحق ، إنما هو من الأحكام الكاذبة للوهم كحكمة بأن خارج الفلك فضاء لا يتناهى واعترض بأنا نجد القطع بهذا الامتداد في حالتي وجود الحركة وعدمها على السواء إن حقاً فحق، وإن وهماً فوهم، والتفرقة تحتاج إلى البرهان .
There is [no time] other than the now, and it was answered that there are no [actual] parts there because time is a continuous quantity that admits division and partition according to purpose and imagination (wahm) not [in] the external [world]. So the objection arose that there is no existence for time then because the extension itself is imaginary (mawhūm) and the part is non-existent, so what [is it] from it? This is unlike distance (masāfa) for its parts, even if they are not actual, the continuous whole that is divided in imagination exists in the external [world]; and unlike motion (ḥaraka) for there is from it a continuous thing which is the 'being in the middle' without necessitating an impossibility. And it was answered that what is meant is that in the mind (fi al-ʿaql) there is an extension that has no existence in the external [world] but such that if its existence and division were supposed, there would arise for its supposed parts priority and posteriority (qabaliyya wa baʿdiyya) that are renewed and passing away, and the extension would not be in the intellect thus unless there is in the external [world] something non-fixed (ghayr qār) which gives rise in the intellect, according to its continuity and instability, that extension which, if its division is supposed, the attachment of precedence and posteriority (taqaddum wa taʾakhkhur) to its supposed parts would be by themselves without requiring another time, and likewise its simultaneity (maʿiyya) with motion. And if there is no existence of the two parts together except in the intellect, then it follows that the priority and posteriority that attach to them are likewise; and therefore they attach to non-existence. How [could it be otherwise]? For if they existed in the external [world] while they are two correlatives (muḍāyifān), there would necessarily follow the existence of their two substrates (maʿrūḍayhima) together in the external [world], and it would follow that time is fixed in essence (qār al-dhāt). And what is said: that what exists in the external [world] of time is a substrate for priority and posteriority is metaphorical; rather what is meant is that it is related to them in the sense that through it they attach to the supposed parts of the intelligible time. This is the utmost of their investigation (taḥqīq) in this place to repel the objections raised by the Imām, such as that the priority of the non-existence of an event over its existence, if it required time, would be the priority of yesterday over tomorrow, and the simultaneity of motion with time likewise; and that if priority and posteriority existed, it would not be impossible for non-existence to be described by them, and their existence would be by time and lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul), and the existence of their two substrates would necessarily follow simultaneously because they are two correlatives, so time would be fixed in essence due to the aggregation of its supposed parts for priority and posteriority. And if they were among the mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya) that have no existence in external entities (aʿyān), then the existence of their two substrates from the external [world] would not be necessary, so it would not indicate the existence of time; and that the parts of time are either similar, so their difference by essential priority and posteriority is impossible, or different, so time would be continuous. And you are aware that their saying: 'There must be in the external [world] a non-fixed thing from which that extension arises in the intellect' is a mere claim, for it is possible that it arises not from an existent, or from a fixed existent according to its relations and additions (nisab wa iḍāfāt) to changing things, as will come later. And as for the third [objection] — that the certainty of the existence of an extension with precedence and posteriority, and from it the past and future, on the assumption that there is no sphere (falak) nor motion at all, or that it has a prior non-existence or a posterior [non-existence] — this is only from the false judgments of the imagination (awhām) like the judgment that outside the sphere there is an infinite void. And it was objected: we find certainty of this extension in both states of existence and non-existence of motion equally; if it is true then it is true, and if it is imaginary then it is imaginary, and the distinction needs a proof (burhān).
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of objections regarding the nature of time. It begins by addressing the claim that time has no actual parts because it is a continuous quantity divisible only in imagination (wahm), not externally. The author then discusses the analogy with distance and motion, showing that unlike them, time's extension is purely mental. He explains that the supposed priority and posteriority of parts of time are only in the intellect, and if they were external, they would entail time being fixed (qār al-dhāt) and lead to absurdities. The passage then lists specific objections from the Imam (likely al-Razi) and answers them, ultimately arguing that the claim of an external non-fixed entity is unproven, and that certainty about time's extension without motion is a false judgment of the imagination. The conclusion is that the distinction between real and imaginary certainty requires a proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that time must have actual parts to have priority and posteriority.
Correction: The passage shows that time is a continuous quantity whose parts are divided only in imagination, and that priority and posteriority are mental relations that do not require actual external parts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does time not have actual parts externally?
A) Because time is a fixed substance (qār al-dhāt)
B) Because time is a continuous quantity that is divisible only in imagination (wahm), not externally
C) Because time is identical with motion
D) Because time has no existence at all

Key Takeaway
Time is a continuous quantity; its parts are divided only in imagination, and priority/posteriority are mental relations, not external realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your sense of the past and future often feels solid. But this passage argues that priority and posteriority are mental, not external. How much of your regret or anxiety is built on the illusion that time's parts are real? What would change if you truly saw the past and future as mental extensions?
?
The passage indicates that time's extension arises from a non-fixed external thing. In your life, what non-fixed, unstable things (emotions, circumstances) do you project onto time as if they were permanent? How can you stop treating your mental timeline as if it were the only reality?
?
If priority and posteriority are not external, then every moment you cling to 'before' or 'after' might be an attachment to a mental construct. Which habits or fears are rooted in this illusion? What concrete step will you take today to break free from a time-bound perspective?

The Imam's Objections: Priority, Posteriority, and the Impossibility of External Time
Al-Taftazani lists and answers objections from al-Razi about the nature of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised by al-Razi regarding priority/posteriority and time, and al-Taftazani's refutation that the claim of a non-fixed substrate is unproven.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority cannot be external realities; they are mental considerations.

After establishing that priority and posteriority are mental, al-Taftazani presents the objections of the Imam (al-Razi) who argues that if they were external, absurdities follow, such as infinite regress and time being fixed. Al-Taftazani then responds by calling the claim of an external non-fixed thing a mere assertion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. This passage lists the Imam's objections and the beginning of the response.
2
Identify the four objections
Find the four specific objections: priority of non-existence over existence, infinite regress, mental considerations not indicating external time, and the problem of parts being similar or different.
3
Understand the common thread
All objections aim to show that if priority/posteriority were external, they would lead to absurdities, implying they must be mental.
4
Analyze the response
The response says that the claim of an external non-fixed thing is a mere assertion, and the mental extension could arise from nothing or from a fixed existent via relations.
5
Reflect on the implications
This response shifts the burden of proof onto the opponent; the existence of time as an external entity is not proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوجد غير الآن، وأجيب بأنه لا أجزاء هناك بالفعل لأن الزمان كمية متصلة يعرض لها التجزؤ والانقسام بحسب الغرض والوهم دون الخارج فورد الإشكال بأنه لا وجود للزمان حينئذ لأن نفس الامتداد موهوم والجزء معدوم فماذا منه؟ وهذا بخلاف المسافة فإن أجزاءها وإن لم تكن بالفعل إلا أن المجموع المتصل الذي يتجزأ في الوهم موجود في الخارج، وبخلاف الحركة فإنه يوجد منها أمر مستمر هو الكون في الوسط من غير لزوم محال . وأجيب بأن المراد أن في الفعل امتداد لا وجود له في الخارج لكنه بحيث لو فرض وجوده وتجزؤه عرضت لأجزائه المفروضة قبليات وبعديات متجددة ومنصرفة، ولا يكون الامتداد في العقل كذلك إلا إذا كان في الخارج شيء غير قار يحصل في العقل بحسب استمراره وعدم استقراره، ذلك الامتداد الذي إذا فرض تجزية كان لحوق التقدم والتأخر لأجزائه المفروضة لذاتها من غير اقتضاء زمان آخر وكذا معيته للحركة وإذا لا وجود للجزأين معاً إلا في العقل لزم كون القبلية والبعدية العارضتين لهما كذلك، ولهذا يعرضان للعدم كيف ولو وجدتا في الخارج وهما متضايفان لزم وجود معروضيهما معاً في الخارج، ويلزم كون الزمان قار الذات، وما يقال من أن الموجود في الخارج من الزمان معروض للقبلية والبعدية فمجاز والمراد أنه متعلق بهما بمعنى أنهما بسببه يعرضان للأجزاء المفروضة للزمان المعقول، هذا غاية تحقيقهم في هذا المقام دفعاً للإشكالات الموردة من قبل الإمام، مثل أن قبلية عدم الحادث على وجوده لو اقتضت زماناً كانت قبلية الأمس على الغد، ومعية الحركة للزمان كذلك، وأن القبلية والبعدية لو وجدتا لا متنع اتصاف العدم بهما، ولكان وجودهما بالزمان وتسلسل وللزم وجود معروضيهما معاً ضرورة كونهما متضايفين فيكون الزمان قار الذات لاجتماع أجزائه المفروضة للقبلية والبعدية، ولو كانتا من الاعتبارات العقلية التي لا وجود لها في الأعيان لم يلزم وجود معروضيهما من الخارج فلم يدل على وجود الزمان وأن أجزاء الزمان إما أن تكون متماثلة فتمتنع اختلافها بالقبلية والبعدية الذاتيتين، أو متخالفة يكون الزمان متصلاً، وأنت خبير بأن قولهم لا بد في الخارج من أمر غير قار يحصل منه في العقل ذلك الامتداد مجرد ادعاء لجواز أن يحصل لا عن موجود، أو عن موجود قار بحسب ما له من النسب والإضافات إلى المتغيرات على ما سيجيء وأما عن الثالث فبأن القطع بوجود امتداد به التقدم والتأخر ومنه الماضي والمستقبل على تقدير أن لا يكون فلك ولا حركة أصلاً ، أو يكون له عدم سابق، أو لاحق ، إنما هو من الأحكام الكاذبة للوهم كحكمة بأن خارج الفلك فضاء لا يتناهى واعترض بأنا نجد القطع بهذا الامتداد في حالتي وجود الحركة وعدمها على السواء إن حقاً فحق، وإن وهماً فوهم، والتفرقة تحتاج إلى البرهان .

Certainty of Time Without Motion: Imagination or Reality?
Al-Taftazani refutes the objection that time can be known without motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection about our certainty of time in the absence of motion and why al-Taftazani considers it a false judgment of the imagination.

Core Idea
Certainty of time without motion is like the false belief in infinite space outside the cosmos.

The third objection challenges the necessity of motion for time, arguing that we can be certain of an extension with past and future even without motion. Al-Taftazani responds that this certainty is a false judgment of the imagination, similar to the belief in an infinite void outside the sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. This short passage presents the third objection and the response.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that we can be certain of an extension with precedence and posteriority, and thus past and future, even if there is no sphere or motion.
3
Understand the response
Al-Taftazani says this certainty is a false judgment of the imagination, like the judgment that outside the sphere there is infinite void.
4
Consider the counter-objection
The counter-objection says we find this certainty equally whether motion exists or not, so the distinction between true and false needs a proof.
5
Reflect on the implication
This shows that the burden of proof is on those who claim certainty of time without motion; it may be an illusion.

And what is said: that what exists in the external [world] of time is a substrate for priority and posteriority is metaphorical; rather what is meant is that it is related to them in the sense that through it they attach to the supposed parts of the intelligible time. This is the utmost of their investigation (taḥqīq) in this place to repel the objections raised by the Imām, such as that the priority of the non-existence of an event over its existence, if it required time, would be the priority of yesterday over tomorrow, and the simultaneity of motion with time likewise; and that if priority and posteriority existed, it would not be impossible for non-existence to be described by them, and their existence would be by time and lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul), and the existence of their two substrates would necessarily follow simultaneously because they are two correlatives, so time would be fixed in essence due to the aggregation of its supposed parts for priority and posteriority. And if they were among the mental considerations (iʿtibārāt ʿaqliyya) that have no existence in external entities (aʿyān), then the existence of their two substrates from the external [world] would not be necessary, so it would not indicate the existence of time; and that the parts of time are either similar, so their difference by essential priority and posteriority is impossible, or different, so time would be continuous. And you are aware that their saying: 'There must be in the external [world] a non-fixed thing from which that extension arises in the intellect' is a mere claim, for it is possible that it arises not from an existent, or from a fixed existent according to its relations and additions (nisab wa iḍāfāt) to changing things, as will come later. And as for the third [objection] — that the certainty of the existence of an extension with precedence and posteriority, and from it the past and future, on the assumption that there is no sphere (falak) nor motion at all, or that it has a prior non-existence or a posterior [non-existence] — this is only from the false judgments of the imagination (awhām) like the judgment that outside the sphere there is an infinite void. And it was objected: we find certainty of this extension in both states of existence and non-existence of motion equally; if it is true then it is true, and if it is imaginary then it is imaginary, and the distinction needs a proof (burhān).
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of objections regarding the nature of time. It begins by addressing the claim that time has no actual parts because it is a continuous quantity divisible only in imagination (wahm), not externally. The author then discusses the analogy with distance and motion, showing that unlike them, time's extension is purely mental. He explains that the supposed priority and posteriority of parts of time are only in the intellect, and if they were external, they would entail time being fixed (qār al-dhāt) and lead to absurdities. The passage then lists specific objections from the Imam (likely al-Razi) and answers them, ultimately arguing that the claim of an external non-fixed entity is unproven, and that certainty about time's extension without motion is a false judgment of the imagination. The conclusion is that the distinction between real and imaginary certainty requires a proof.

Key Terms
قطع
وهم
فضاء
لا يتناهى
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that we can perceive time directly without any motion or change.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that such certainty is a false judgment of the imagination, analogous to believing in an infinite void outside the cosmos.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, the certainty of time without motion is compared to which false belief?
A) The belief that time is a fixed substance
B) The belief that outside the sphere there is an infinite void
C) The belief that motion is essential to time
D) The belief that time has actual parts

Key Takeaway
The certainty of time's extension in the absence of motion is an illusion of the imagination, requiring proof to distinguish true from false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain about a belief—perhaps about time, space, or even your own existence—only to later realize it was an assumption you never questioned? How does this passage challenge you to re-examine your most basic certainties today, not as abstract possibilities but as concrete matters in your daily life? Will you actively seek proof (burhān) for one such certainty this week?
?
The passage equates the 'self-evident' feeling of time without motion to the false belief in infinite space—both products of imagination. What is one belief you hold that feels undeniable but might actually be a comfortable illusion? What would it cost you to subject it to scrutiny? Are you willing to pay that price?
?
The counter-objection states that the distinction between true and false certainty requires proof. In your own life, when have you assumed something was true without proof because it 'felt right' or 'everyone knows it'? How can you cultivate the discipline to demand proof for your convictions, especially those that are most emotionally charged?

Place (Space) in Philosophy and Kalam
Presents and contrasts definitions of place by philosophers and mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major definitions of place (space) in classical Islamic thought: the Aristotelian concept of place as the inner surface of the container, and the kalam concept of place as a hypothetical void that can be empty.

Core Idea
Place is either the inner surface of what contains a body or an imagined void that could be empty.

The concept of place or space has been debated for centuries. In this passage, the author presents the two main views that were accepted among philosophers and theologians. One view defines place as the inner surface of the container (like the inside of a cup), while the other sees it as a dimension that can exist even when empty. Let's explore these definitions and the key difference between Plato's position and that of the mutakallimun.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the language, then read the literal translation closely. Notice how the author introduces the two definitions of place.
The term 'place' (المكان) is the central concept.
2
Understand the two definitions
The passage gives two definitions for place: (1) the inner surface of the container, and (2) the dimension in which the body's dimension penetrates. Identify these and note how they are related.
The second definition is split into material and separable dimensions.
3
Contrast Plato and the mutakallimun
The passage contrasts the views of Plato (place cannot be empty) and the mutakallimun (place can be empty, it is an imagined void). Ask yourself: what difference does this make for theology?
This distinction is important for discussions about God's relation to space.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثالث: في المكان. والمعتبر من المذاهب أنه السطح الباطن من الحاوي، أو البعد الذي ينفذ فيه بعد الجسم، فإن من البعد مادياً يحل في الجسم ويمانع ما يماثله، ومفارقاً يحل فيه الجسم ويلاقيه بجملته، بحيث ينطبق على بعد الجسم، ويتحد به إلا أنه عند أفلاطون موجود يمتنع خلوه عن شاغل. وعند المتكلمين مفروض يمكن خلوه، وهو المعنى بالفراغ المتوهم الذي لو لم يشغله شاغل لكان خالياً. فها هنا المقامان.
He said: The third investigation: on place. And the accepted among the schools is that it is the inner surface of the container, or the dimension in which the body's dimension penetrates. For from dimension is material, which settles in the body and prevents what is like it, and separable, in which the body settles and meets it in its entirety, such that it coincides with the body's dimension and unites with it, except that with Plato it exists, its emptiness of an occupier being impossible. And with the mutakallimun it is hypothetical, its emptiness being possible, and it is what is meant by the imagined void which, if no occupier occupied it, would be empty. So here are the two positions.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of place (space) and contrasts two main views. The author begins by stating that the third investigation is about place. The accepted view among schools is that place is either the inner surface of the container (Aristotelian view) or the dimension in which the body's dimension penetrates. The author then explains that 'dimension' can be material, residing in the body and preventing a similar dimension, or separable, such that the body resides in it entirely, coinciding and uniting with it. Next, the author contrasts the views of Plato and the mutakallimun. For Plato, place is a real entity that cannot be empty; it always contains a body. For the mutakallimun, place is a hypothetical void that can be empty; it is an imagined vacuum that, if not occupied, would be empty. The passage concludes that these are the two positions.

Misconception: Some may think that all Islamic theologians agreed with Aristotle's definition of place as the inner surface of the container.
Correction: The passage shows that the mutakallimun had a different view: place as an imagined void that can be empty.

Checkpoint

C) Plato says place is eternal, mutakallimun say it is created
D) Plato says place is material, mutakallimun say it is immaterial

Key Takeaway
Place is understood either as the inner surface of a container or as an imagined void that can be empty, with the key difference being whether emptiness is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You, like everything material, occupy place. The mutakallimun say that place could be empty – a void. If the space around you is ultimately a void that could be empty, what does that say about the permanence of your surroundings? Are you clinging to a world that is, in truth, a mere hypothetical arrangement? How should this reshape your attachment to the material world?
?
Plato held that the void can never be empty; it always has an occupier. The mutakallimun say it can be empty. Which view describes your heart? Do you believe your heart must always be filled with something – relationships, ambitions, distractions – so that you never let it be empty? Or do you acknowledge that emptiness is possible, and that emptiness is the only state in which the Divine can truly fill it?
?
The passage teaches that place is either the inner surface of a container or an imagined void. Right now, consider your life as a container. What is occupying its inner surface? Is it filled with the love of the Creator, or is it filled with everything else? If it is filled with other things, what would it take to empty it so that only He remains?

Two Definitions of Place: Surface vs Dimension
Taftazani presents the two main definitions of place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main definitions of place in Islamic philosophy: the surface definition (Aristotelian) and the dimension definition (favored by many philosophers and mutakallimun).

Core Idea
Place is defined either as the containing surface or as the dimension occupied by the body.

Taftazani begins by stating the common understanding of place as the vessel in which a body moves and rests. He then identifies the two major schools of thought: the Peripatetics, who define place as the inner surface of the container, and many philosophers and theologians, who define it as a dimension that the body's dimension penetrates. This lesson introduces these foundational views that set the stage for further debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the two definitions: surface (Aristotelian) and dimension (philosophers/mutakallimun).
Notice that the surface definition is attributed to Aristotle and the Peripatetics.
2
Compare the definitions
Contrast the two definitions. The surface definition focuses on the container's inner boundary; the dimension definition sees place as the space occupied by the body.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider why the dimension definition might be more flexible for theological discussions, especially concerning divine attributes.
This connects to broader kalam debates about God's relationship to space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاخفاء في آنية شيء ينتقل الجسم عنه وإليه ، ويسكن فيه، ولا يسع معه غيره، وهو المسمى بالمكان والمعتبر من المذاهب : أن ماهية السطح الباطن من الجسم الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى . وإليه ذهب أرسطو وأشياعه من المشائين ، أو البعد الذي ينفذ فيه بعد الجسم، ويتجدد به وإليه ذهب كثير من الفلاسفة والمتكلمين وزعموا أن من البعد ما هو مادي يحل في الجسم، ويقوم به، ويمتنع اجتماعه مع بعد آخر ما مماثل له قائم بذلك الجسم، وهو المسمى بالجسم التعليمي، ومنه مفارق لا يقوم بمحل، بل يحل فيه الجسم ويلاقيه بجملته، ويجامعه بعد الجسم منطبقاً عليه متحداً به . هو إلا إنه عند المتكلمين عدم محض ونفي صرف، يمكن أن لا يشغله شاغل، وهو المعنى بالفراغ المتوهم، الذي لو لم يشغله شاغل لكان فارغاً . وعند بعض الفلاسفة : امتداد موجود قد يكون ذراعاً، وقد يكون أقل، أو أكثر، وقد يسع هذا الجسم، وقد يسع ما هو أصغر منه، أو أكبر وتوضيحه أنا إذا توهمنا خلواً لإناء من الماء والهواء وغيرهما، ففيما بين أطرافه امتداد قد يشغله الماء، وقد يشغله الهواء، فكذا عند الامتلاء ويسمونه البعد المفطور . بمعنى أنه مشهور مفطور عليه البديهة، فإن كل أحد يحكم بأن الماء فيما بين أطراف الإناء وقيل بمعنى أنه ينشق، فيدخل فيه الجسم
There is no hiddenness in the vessel of a thing; the body moves from it and to it, and rests in it, and nothing else can fit with it, and it is what is called place. And the considered among the schools is that its essence is the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body. This is the view of Aristotle and his followers among the Peripatetics. Or it is the dimension that penetrates the dimension of the body and is renewed by it, and this is the view of many philosophers and mutakallimun. They claimed that of dimension there is a material one that inheres in the body and subsists by it, and it is impossible for it to coexist with another similar dimension subsisting in that same body, and it is called the 'teaching body' (al-jism al-taʿlīmī). And of it there is a separate [dimension] that does not subsist in a locus, but rather the body inheres in it and meets it in its entirety, and the dimension of the body joins it, corresponding to it and united with it. However, according to the mutakallimun it is pure non-existence and sheer negation, which can be unoccupied by any occupant, and that is what is meant by the imaginary void, which, if no occupant occupies it, would be empty. And according to some philosophers: it is an existing extension that may be a cubit, or less, or more, and may contain this body, and may contain something smaller or larger. And its clarification is that if we imagine a vessel empty of water and air and other things, then between its boundaries there is an extension that water may occupy and air may occupy, and likewise when full. And they call it the 'innate dimension' (al-buʿd al-mafṭūr), meaning that it is well-known and innate to the intuition, for everyone judges that the water is within the boundaries of the vessel. And it is said that it means it splits so that the body enters into it.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the dimension definition of place. It first restates the two main views: the surface view (attributed to Aristotle and the Peripatetics) and the dimension view (attributed to many philosophers and mutakallimun). It then explains that within the dimension view, there are two kinds of dimension: material dimension, which inheres in the body and prevents a similar dimension from coexisting (called the 'teaching body', al-jism al-taʿlīmī), and separable dimension, which does not subsist in a body but rather the body inheres in it and unites with it. The passage then notes that according to the mutakallimun, this separable dimension is pure non-existence and sheer negation—the imaginary void that can be empty. In contrast, some philosophers hold it to be an existing extension that can vary in size. The passage clarifies this with the example of a vessel: when empty, there is an extension between its boundaries that can be filled with water or air, and this extension is called the 'innate dimension' (al-buʿd al-mafṭūr), meaning it is a common-sense notion evident to intuition.

Key Terms
مكان (place)
بعد (dimension)
سطح (surface)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is the definition of place attributed to Aristotle?
A) The dimension that penetrates the body's dimension
B) The inner surface of the containing body touching the outer surface of the contained body
C) The vessel that holds the body
D) The imaginary void

Key Takeaway
Place is defined either as the inner surface of the container or as the dimension occupied by the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the space you occupy right now, do you experience it as a boundary (like the walls around you) or as an extension you fill? Which definition of place feels more immediate to your own existence?
?
The passage says place is 'the vessel of a thing' that 'nothing else can fit with it simultaneously'. How does this confront our common experience of sharing space with others? What does this teach you about the uniqueness and exclusivity of every created thing's position?
?
If place can be defined in two fundamentally different ways, what does that tell you about the limits of human conceptualization? How does this humility push you to rely on revelation for truths that reason cannot settle?

Material and Separable Dimensions: The Teaching Body
Taftazani distinguishes material and separable dimensions within the dimension theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the material dimension (the teaching body) and the separable dimension within the dimension theory of place.

Core Idea
Dimension is of two kinds: one material (inhering in body) and one separable (body inheres in it).

After introducing the dimension definition, Taftazani explains that those who adopt it further split dimension into two types: material and separable. The material dimension, called the 'teaching body', inheres in a body and prevents another similar dimension. The separable dimension does not subsist in a locus but rather the body inheres in it. This distinction is crucial for understanding later debates on the nature of space.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two dimensions
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the two types: material and separable.
Note that the material dimension is called 'teaching body' (al-jism al-taʿlīmī).
2
Understand material dimension
The material dimension inheres in the body, subsists by it, and cannot coexist with another similar dimension in the same body.
3
Understand separable dimension
The separable dimension does not subsist in a locus; rather, the body inheres in it, meeting it entirely and uniting with it.
This dimension is more abstract and leads to the concept of void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Imaginary Void and the Innate Dimension
Taftazani explains the disagreement on the separable dimension and introduces the innate dimension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the mutakallimun's view of the separable dimension as pure non-existence (the imaginary void) and the philosophers' view of it as an existing extension, and the concept of the innate dimension.

Core Idea
For mutakallimun, the separable dimension is a void of non-existence; for philosophers, it is an existing extension—the innate dimension.

After distinguishing material and separable dimension, Taftazani notes a critical disagreement. The mutakallimun consider the separable dimension to be pure non-existence and sheer negation—an imaginary void that can be empty. In contrast, some philosophers view it as an actually existing extension that can vary in size. The passage then clarifies with the example of a vessel and introduces the term 'innate dimension' (al-buʿd al-mafṭūr) as a common-sense notion evident to intuition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on separable dimension
Read the passage. Note that the mutakallimun see it as pure non-existence (imaginary void), while some philosophers see it as an existing extension.
The passage contrasts 'pure non-existence' with 'existing extension'.
2
Understand the imaginary void concept
The imaginary void is defined as a negation that can be unoccupied; it is what would be empty if no occupant filled it.
3
Understand the innate dimension
The innate dimension is explained through the example of a vessel: when empty, there is an extension between its boundaries that can be filled. This is common sense, hence 'innate'.
The term 'innate' (mafṭūr) means it is a natural intuition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاخفاء في آنية شيء ينتقل الجسم عنه وإليه ، ويسكن فيه، ولا يسع معه غيره، وهو المسمى بالمكان والمعتبر من المذاهب : أن ماهية السطح الباطن من الجسم الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى . وإليه ذهب أرسطو وأشياعه من المشائين ، أو البعد الذي ينفذ فيه بعد الجسم، ويتجدد به وإليه ذهب كثير من الفلاسفة والمتكلمين وزعموا أن من البعد ما هو مادي يحل في الجسم، ويقوم به، ويمتنع اجتماعه مع بعد آخر ما مماثل له قائم بذلك الجسم، وهو المسمى بالجسم التعليمي، ومنه مفارق لا يقوم بمحل، بل يحل فيه الجسم ويلاقيه بجملته، ويجامعه بعد الجسم منطبقاً عليه متحداً به . هو إلا إنه عند المتكلمين عدم محض ونفي صرف، يمكن أن لا يشغله شاغل، وهو المعنى بالفراغ المتوهم، الذي لو لم يشغله شاغل لكان فارغاً . وعند بعض الفلاسفة : امتداد موجود قد يكون ذراعاً، وقد يكون أقل، أو أكثر، وقد يسع هذا الجسم، وقد يسع ما هو أصغر منه، أو أكبر وتوضيحه أنا إذا توهمنا خلواً لإناء من الماء والهواء وغيرهما، ففيما بين أطرافه امتداد قد يشغله الماء، وقد يشغله الهواء، فكذا عند الامتلاء ويسمونه البعد المفطور . بمعنى أنه مشهور مفطور عليه البديهة، فإن كل أحد يحكم بأن الماء فيما بين أطراف الإناء وقيل بمعنى أنه ينشق، فيدخل فيه الجسم
There is no hiddenness in the vessel of a thing; the body moves from it and to it, and rests in it, and nothing else can fit with it, and it is what is called place. And the considered among the schools is that its essence is the inner surface of the containing body that touches the outer surface of the contained body. This is the view of Aristotle and his followers among the Peripatetics. Or it is the dimension that penetrates the dimension of the body and is renewed by it, and this is the view of many philosophers and mutakallimun. They claimed that of dimension there is a material one that inheres in the body and subsists by it, and it is impossible for it to coexist with another similar dimension subsisting in that same body, and it is called the 'teaching body' (al-jism al-taʿlīmī). And of it there is a separate [dimension] that does not subsist in a locus, but rather the body inheres in it and meets it in its entirety, and the dimension of the body joins it, corresponding to it and united with it. However, according to the mutakallimun it is pure non-existence and sheer negation, which can be unoccupied by any occupant, and that is what is meant by the imaginary void, which, if no occupant occupies it, would be empty. And according to some philosophers: it is an existing extension that may be a cubit, or less, or more, and may contain this body, and may contain something smaller or larger. And its clarification is that if we imagine a vessel empty of water and air and other things, then between its boundaries there is an extension that water may occupy and air may occupy, and likewise when full. And they call it the 'innate dimension' (al-buʿd al-mafṭūr), meaning that it is well-known and innate to the intuition, for everyone judges that the water is within the boundaries of the vessel. And it is said that it means it splits so that the body enters into it.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the dimension definition of place. It first restates the two main views: the surface view (attributed to Aristotle and the Peripatetics) and the dimension view (attributed to many philosophers and mutakallimun). It then explains that within the dimension view, there are two kinds of dimension: material dimension, which inheres in the body and prevents a similar dimension from coexisting (called the 'teaching body', al-jism al-taʿlīmī), and separable dimension, which does not subsist in a body but rather the body inheres in it and unites with it. The passage then notes that according to the mutakallimun, this separable dimension is pure non-existence and sheer negation—the imaginary void that can be empty. In contrast, some philosophers hold it to be an existing extension that can vary in size. The passage clarifies this with the example of a vessel: when empty, there is an extension between its boundaries that can be filled with water or air, and this extension is called the 'innate dimension' (al-buʿd al-mafṭūr), meaning it is a common-sense notion evident to intuition.

Key Terms
فراغ متوهم (imaginary void)
امتداد موجود (existing extension)
بعد مفطور (innate dimension)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the mutakallimun consider the separable dimension to be?
A) An existing extension that varies in size
B) Pure non-existence and sheer negation
C) A material dimension called the teaching body
D) The inner surface of the container

Key Takeaway
The separable dimension is either a void of non-existence (mutakallimun) or an existing extension (philosophers), with the latter illustrated by the innate dimension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at an empty cup, do you instinctively feel that the emptiness inside is 'something' or 'nothing'? How does your gut feeling align with the mutakallimun's position that it is pure non-existence? What does this reveal about your underlying assumptions about reality?
?
The philosophers claimed the innate dimension is self-evident. But if it is merely an imaginary void as the theologians assert, what does that imply about the physical world you experience? Are you willing to accept that what seems obvious to your senses might be a mental construct? How does that change your trust in your own reasoning?
?
If the separable dimension is pure non-existence, then before the universe was created, was there a 'place' where it could exist? Or is the very concept of 'place' dependent on existing things? How does your answer affect your understanding of God's act of creation from nothing?

Introduction: The Debate on Place and Void
Al-Taftazani introduces the debate on place and void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two central questions in the Islamic discussion of place: whether place is surface or dimension, and whether void is possible.

Core Idea
The nature of place and void is debated between philosophers and theologians.

Al-Taftazani begins by clarifying Plato's ambiguous concept of dimension, then sets out the two key problems: the nature of place and the possibility of void. This foundation is essential for grasping the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note how Al-Taftazani defines Plato's terms.
Pay attention to the terms 'hyle' and 'form'.
2
Identify the two stations
The passage mentions two investigations (maqaman). Find them and note what each covers.
The first station is about place being surface or dimension; the second about void.
3
Understand Plato's ambiguity
Plato uses both 'hyle' and 'form' for dimension. Consider why this causes confusion.
4
Reflect on the context
Think about why defining place and void matters for theology and philosophy.
This debate influences arguments about God's relationship to space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بماله من البعد ويعبر عنه أفلاطون تارة بالهيولي لتوارد الأجسام عليه توارد الصور على المادة وتارة بالصورة لكونه عبارة عن الأبعاد. إذ الممتدة في الجهات بمنزلة الصورة الاتصالية الجسمية التي بها يقبل الجسم الأبعاد ويتميز عن المجردات، وعلى هذا لا يرد ما يقال، إن امتناع كون حيز الجسم جزءاً منه، في غاية الظهور، فكيف يذهب إليه العاقل. ثم إن هذا البعد عند أفلاطون وأتباعه، ممتنع الخلو عن شاغل، وعند البعض ممكن الخلو عنه، وأصحاب الخلاء هم المتكلمون وبعض الفلاسفة. ففي هذا البحث مقامان: أحدهما في أن المكان هو السطح أو البعد، وثانيهما في أن الخلاء ممكن أو ممتنع.

قال: المقام الأول: ان المكان هو السطح أو البعد. وحجة السطح بوجوه.

الأول: أنه موجود يقبل التفاوت والإشارة والانتقال منه وإليه. والبعد الموجود إن قبل الحركة كان له مكان وتسلسل، على أن جميع الأمكنة يفتقر إلى مكان فيكون داخلاً فيها بكونه أحدها خارجاً منها بكونه ظرفاً لها، وإن لم يقبل لم يقبلها الجسم، لما فيه من البعد اللازم.

الثاني: إن تمكن الجسم حينئذ يستلزم نفوذ بعده في البعد المكاني فيكون فيه بعدان، ويجتمع المثلان ويرتفع الآنات عن وحدة هذه مثلا، وعن تساوي أصل المتمكن والمكان.

الثالث: أن البعد إما أن يفتقر إلى المحل فلا يتجرد، أو يستغني.

والجواب: أن مبنى الكل على تماثل البعدين وهو حجة.
By his dimension and Plato sometimes expresses it by hyle (hayula) because bodies come upon it as forms come upon matter, and sometimes by form because it is an expression of dimensions. Since the extension in directions is like the bodily continuous form by which the body receives dimensions and is distinguished from incorporeal entities. Thus, the objection that the impossibility of the body's space being a part of it is extremely obvious, so how could a rational person go to that? Then this dimension according to Plato and his followers is impossible to be empty of an occupier, and according to some it is possible to be empty of it, and the proponents of void are the mutakallimun and some philosophers. In this inquiry there are two stations: one is whether place is surface or dimension, and the second is whether void is possible or impossible. He said: The first station: That place is surface or dimension. And the proof for surface has aspects. First: That it (surface) exists and admits of inequality, indication, and movement from and to it. And the existing dimension, if it admits motion, has a place and leads to infinite regress, such that all places depend on a place, so it would be included among them as one of them and outside them as their container; and if it does not admit motion, then the body does not admit it due to its necessary dimension. Second: That the body's becoming located then necessitates the penetration of its dimension into the spatial dimension, so there would be two dimensions, and the two similars would coincide, and the 'nows' would be removed from the unity of these, for example, and from the equality of the origin of the located and the place. Third: That the dimension either depends on a locus so it does not become separate, or it is independent. And the answer: That the basis of all is the similarity of the two dimensions, and it is a proof.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage introduces the debate on the nature of place (makan) and void (khala'). It first clarifies that Plato's concept of dimension is ambiguous, sometimes called hyle (hayula) and sometimes form (sura). The text then states the two main questions: (1) is place surface or dimension? (2) is void possible? The first station is then addressed: place is either surface or dimension. Three arguments are given for the surface view: first, surface exists and is perceptible, whereas dimension if existing would either require motion (leading to infinite regress) or be immobile (contradicting the body's need for dimension). Second, if body is located, its dimension would penetrate the spatial dimension, causing two dimensions to coincide, which is absurd. Third, dimension is either dependent on a substrate (hence not separate) or independent. The response refutes these by noting they all assume similarity between the two dimensions, which itself requires proof.

Key Terms
مكان
خلاء
هيولي
صورة
بعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Plato's view of place is clear and consistent.
Correction: Plato uses both 'hyle' and 'form' for dimension, leading to ambiguity.

Checkpoint
What are the two stations (maqaman) mentioned in this passage?
A) Whether place is surface or dimension, and whether void is possible
B) Whether place is body or void, and whether motion is possible
C) Whether dimension is hyle or form, and whether void is eternal
D) Whether place is created or eternal, and whether void is filled

Key Takeaway
The debate on place involves two distinct but related questions: the nature of place (surface or dimension) and the possibility of void.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you dismiss a teaching because it seems 'obvious' to you, just like the objection al-Taftazani mentions? What belief have you held that you never really examined? Will you challenge one such belief this week?
?
The mutakallimun considered void possible—space without occupants. What empty spaces in your schedule or soul do you fill with noise instead of allowing them to be truly void for reflection? What would happen if you left one space empty intentionally?
?
Plato described dimension as a continuous form giving structure to bodies. Your life too has a form—habits, routines, relationships. Is that form receiving 'divine dimensions' or is it shaped by fleeting whims? What one change would make your life's form more receptive to truth?

Three Arguments for Surface as Place and Their Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents three arguments for surface and a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments that place is surface and the response that they rest on an unproven assumption of similarity between dimensions.

Core Idea
The arguments for surface place all assume two dimensions are similar — an unproven claim.

The first station presents three arguments that place must be surface, not dimension. However, the response shows that these arguments are built on a hidden assumption of similarity between dimensions, which itself requires justification.

Guided Path
1
Read the three arguments
Read each argument carefully. Note how each one tries to prove that place is surface.
The arguments are numbered: first, second, third.
2
Analyze the first argument
It argues that surface exists and admits motion, while dimension either leads to regress or is immobile.
3
Analyze the second argument
It claims that if body is located, its dimension would coincide with spatial dimension, causing two dimensions to overlap.
4
Analyze the third argument
It says dimension is either dependent on a substrate (hence not separate) or independent.
5
Understand the response
The response says all arguments assume that two dimensions are similar, which is not proven.
This is a methodological critique, not a positive argument for dimension.

?

?
Al-Taftazani refutes the arguments without offering a full alternative. When you discover a flaw in someone's reasoning, do you feel compelled to supply a complete answer, or can you humbly say 'this argument does not prove the claim' and leave the question open? Which posture is more honest before truth?

Refutation of the Dimension View of Place
Al-Taftazani refutes the dimension view of place by dividing it into imaginary and real and showing both are invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's refutation of the view that place is dimension, by analyzing the two variants (imaginary and real) and their logical failures.

Core Idea
Place cannot be dimension, whether imaginary or real, because it requires properties only existence can have.

Al-Taftazani continues to defend the surface view of place by attacking the alternative that place is dimension. He splits the dimension view into two variants: the imaginary dimension of the mutakallimun and the real dimension of Plato. Both, he argues, lead to absurdities. This lesson walks through his reasoning, showing why place must have concrete properties that neither pure non-existence nor infinite regress can sustain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of dimension
Read the passage and note the two subdivisions: imaginary (suppositional) and real (subsisting). Understand who holds each view.
The mutakallimun hold the imaginary view; Plato holds the real view.
2
Understand the refutation of imaginary dimension
Examine why the author rejects the imaginary dimension: place has properties like equality, sensory indication, and motion—pure non-existence lacks these.
Focus on the phrase 'لا شيء من العدم المحض والنفي الصرف كذلك'.
3
Understand the refutation of real dimension
See the author's argument that a real dimension capable of motion would itself need a place, leading to infinite regress and contradiction.
The argument uses the concept of 'حركة آنية' (instantaneous motion).
4
Grasp the problem of infinite regress
Consider why an infinite series of places is impossible: each place would have to be both inside and outside the series, a contradiction.
This relies on the earlier refutation of تسلسل (infinite regress).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: اجتح القائلون بكون المكان هو السطح، بأنه لا يعقل منه إلا البعد أو السطح، والأول باطل لوجوه:

الأول: أنه لو كان هو البعد، فإما أن يكون متوهماً مفروضاً، على ما هو رأي المتكلمين، وهو باطل. لأن المكان موجود ضرورة واستدلالاً بأنه يقبل التساوي والتفاوت وحيث يقال: مكان هذا مساو لمكان ذاك، أو زائد عليه، أو ناقص عنه، نصف له أو ثلث أو ربع، أو غير ذلك، وبأنه يقبل الإشارة الحسية، وانتقال الجسم منه وإليه، حيث يقال: انتقل الجسم من هذا المكان إلى ذلك، والاتصاف بالصغر والكبر والطول والقصر، والقرب والبعد والاتصال والانفصال إلى غير ذلك، ولا شيء من العدم المحض والنفي الصرف كذلك.

وإما أن يكون متحققاً موجوداً، على ما هو رأي أفلاطون ومن تبعه، وهو أيضاً باطل، لأنه إن كان قابلاً للحركة الآنية التي هي الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان، كان له مكان. وينقل الكلام إليه ليلزم ترتب الأمكنة لا إلى نهاية، وهو محال لما مر في إبطال التسلسل، ولأن جميع الأمكنة الغير المتناهية، لكونه من جنس البعد على ما هو المفروض يكون قابلاً للحركة، مفتقراً إلى المكان فيلزم أن يكون ذلك المكان داخلاً في جملة الأمكنة، لكونه واحداً منها، وأن يكون خارجاً عنها لكونه ظرفاً لها، وذلك محال، وإن لم يكن ذلك البعد الذي هو...
He said: Those who say that place is surface argued that nothing is conceived of it except dimension or surface, and the first is invalid for reasons:

First: If it (place) were dimension, then either it is imagined and suppositional, as is the view of the mutakallimun, and it is invalid. Because place exists necessarily and by inference, as it admits equality and inequality, as it is said: the place of this is equal to the place of that, or greater or less, half or third or quarter, etc., and because it admits sensory indication, and the body's movement from it and to it, as it is said: the body moved from this place to that, and being described as small and large, long and short, near and far, connected and separated, etc., and nothing of pure non-existence and absolute negation is like that.

Or it is real and existing, as is the view of Plato and those who follow him, and it is also invalid, because if it is capable of instantaneous motion which is transition from place to place, it would have a place. And the discourse would be transferred to it, leading to an infinite succession of places, which is impossible due to what has passed on the refutation of infinite regress, and because all infinite places, being of the kind of dimension as supposed, would be capable of motion, dependent on place, so that place must be included among the places as one of them, and outside them as their container, and that is impossible, and if that dimension is not...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents a further argument for the surface view by refuting the dimension view. It divides dimension into two types: imaginary (as held by the mutakallimun) and real (as held by Plato). The imaginary dimension is invalid because place is not a mental construct—it has properties like equality, inequality, sensory indication, and movement, which cannot belong to pure non-existence. The real dimension is also invalid because if it were capable of motion, it would itself need a place, leading to an infinite regress of places. Moreover, if an infinite series of places existed, each place would both belong to the series and contain it, which is absurd. The text ends mid-argument, likely continuing to another case.

Key Terms
مكان
سطح
بعد
متوهم
متحقق
حركة آنية
تسلسل
خلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument shows that place cannot be a dimension of any kind whatsoever.
Correction: The argument only shows that place cannot be an imaginary dimension (conceptual) or a real dimension (subsisting), but it does not deny that dimension can be predicated of place in a different sense (e.g., as a property of surface). The surface view remains intact.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the view that place is an imaginary dimension?
A) Because imaginary things cannot have properties
B) Because place has sensory and quantitative properties that non-existence lacks
C) Because the mutakallimun are unreliable
D) Because Plato's view is more plausible

Key Takeaway
Place has real properties (equality, inequality, sensory indication) that cannot belong to non-existence, and if it were a real dimension it would lead to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about 'place' in relation to Allah, how often do you fall into the trap of imagining a location for Him, even though you know He is above such limitations? What concrete step can you take today to guard your heart against this subtle anthropomorphism?
?
The argument shows that imaginary concepts cannot have real properties. Are there areas in your faith where you treat intellectual abstractions (like 'divine love' or 'mercy') as if they were merely mental constructs rather than real attributes of Allah? How will you remind yourself tomorrow that His attributes are real and not imaginary?
?
If the real dimension view leads to an infinite regress, consider your own reasoning about matters of faith: are there any beliefs you hold that, when traced back, lead to an infinite chain of causes or justifications? Where must you stop and say 'this is where I submit to Allah's decree without further explanation'?

Al-Taftazani's Objections to the Surface Theory of Place
Al-Taftazani lists absurd consequences of the surface theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised by Al-Taftazani against the surface theory of place and why they only amount to strong probability rather than certain proof.

Core Idea
If place were a surface, absurd consequences would follow.

Al-Taftazani continues his critique of the prevailing definitions of place. Here he attacks the view that place is the inner surface of the containing body. He presents a series of hypothetical scenarios that would lead to contradictions or impossible outcomes. However, he concedes that these arguments, while compelling, do not constitute a formal proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the list of objections and the concluding remark.
The passage is dense; take time to understand each example.
2
Identify each objection
List the four main objections: (1) surface size mismatch, (2) no container for celestial sphere, (3) bird and moon reversal, (4) Zayd's place vanishing.
Each objection highlights a contradiction if place were surface.
3
Analyze the author's conclusion
Reflect on why Al-Taftazani says these are only 'strong probability' and not a complete proof. Consider the role of such arguments in kalam.
This shows the cautious reasoning style of theologians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو أنه لو كان السطح لم يساو التمكن كما إذا جعلنا المدور صفحة دقيقة وبالعكس، ولم تقم الأجسام، إذ لا حاوي للمحيط وتبدلت الأحكام . إذ الطير الساكن في الهواء الهارب يستبدل السطوح، فيلزم تحركه، والقمر المتحرك لا يستبدلها فيلزم سكونه، ومكان زيد حين ملأه الهواء موجود، ويلزم عدمه، إلى غير ذلك من الأمارات التي ربما تفيد قوة الظن، وإن لم يتم برهانا) .
That if it were surface, it would not equal the occupant, as when we make the round one a thin sheet and vice versa, and bodies would not stand, because there is no container for the encompassing, and judgments would change. For the bird stationary in the air when fleeing changes surfaces, so its motion would be necessary, and the moving moon does not change them, so its rest would be necessary. And Zayd's place when air fills it exists, and its non-existence would be necessary, and other such indicators that might give strong probability, even if not a complete proof.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the view that place (al-makan) is the inner surface of the containing body. The author lists several absurd consequences that would follow if place were surface. The examples are: (1) the surface theory would imply that the same body could have different sizes of surface depending on shape, contradicting the equality of place and occupant; (2) there would be no place for the celestial sphere since it has no container; (3) the bird's motion and the moon's rest would be reversed; (4) Zayd's place would vanish when air enters. The author concludes that these are indications (amarat) that give strong probability (qawwat al-zann) against the surface theory, but not a conclusive proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Al-Taftazani disproves the surface theory definitively.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that these objections provide strong probability, not a complete proof.

A) They are conclusive proofs.
B) They are weak.
C) They give strong probability but not proof.
D) They are only suppositions.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani's objections to the surface theory of place are compelling but not decisive; they only provide strong probability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Eight Signs That Place Is Void
Al-Taftazani lists eight arguments for the void theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight key arguments that proponents of the void theory use to argue that place cannot be surface, and why these are considered strong indications but not definitive proofs.

Core Idea
Eight signs show why place must be void, not surface.

Al-Taftazani now presents the case for the void theory (place as bu'd) by compiling eight arguments from its proponents. Each argument shows that if place were surface, something we know by rational intuition would be contradicted. Read these carefully — they are a masterclass in using absurdity to prove a positive claim. Note at the end that the author calls these 'strong probability,' not certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage once
Read the Arabic passage in one go without pausing. Then read the translation. Let the rhythm of the arguments sink in before analysing.
This is a long passage with eight sub-arguments; a first pass helps you see the forest.
2
Identify each of the eight arguments
Go through the passage and label each argument from 1 to 8. Notice how each begins with 'منها' (among them).
The eighth argument is introduced differently: 'ومنها أنا نقطع'.
3
Understand the pattern of each argument
Each argument shows that if place were surface, an absurd or impossible consequence would follow. For each, ask: what is the absurd consequence, and why does it support the void theory?
Focus on the logical structure: 'if surface then absurd, therefore void'.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
Read the final sentence where Al-Taftazani says these are أمارات تفيد قوة الظن (indications that give strong probability). Ask yourself: why does he stop short of certainty?
This connects to the epistemological humility of kalam — signs point but do not compel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : حجة البعد احتج القائلون بكون المكان هو البعد، بأنه لو كان السطح، لزم انتفاء أمور يحكم بديهة العقل بثبوتها منها مساواة المكان للمتمكن فإن الشمعة المدورة إذا جعلناها صفحة رقيقة، كان السطح المحيط بها أضعاف المحيط بالمدورة ، وإذا جعلنا الصفحة المدورة كان السطح المحيط بها اقل من المحيط بالصفحة مع أن الجسم في الحالين واحد وكما إذا جعلنا في المكعب نقرة عميقة يزيد السطح المحيط به مع انتقاص الجسم، ومنها كون كل جسم في مكان، مع أن الجسم المحيط بالكل لا يحويه جسم ، ليكون سطحه الباطن مكاناً له، ومنها سكون الطير الواقف في الهواء عند هبوب الرياح، فإنه يتبدل عليه السطوح المحيطة به، مع أن تبدل الأمكنة إما نفس الحركة الأينية، أو ملزوم لها، ومنها حركة القمر الدائر، لأن السطح المحيط به من فلكه واحد لا يتبدل، وعدم تبدل المكان ملزوم السكون، لأن تبدله لازم الحركة، أو نفسها، ومنها بقاء المكان الذي خرج منه زيد، مع أنه قد ملأه الهواء ، فلم يبق ما كان فيه من السطح المحيط بزيد، ومنها كون كل جزء من أجزاء الجسم في حيز ، مع أن الأجزاء الباطنة من الماء مثلاً ، لا تكون في سطح من الهواء إلا بطريق التبعية والتجوز. كما يقال : الماء في الفلك. ومنها عدم توقف الحكم بكون الجسم ها هنا، أو هناك . على أنه هل يحيط به جسم أم لا . ؟ ومنها : أنا نتصور أن جسماً لا يماسه شيء، بل يوجد وحده مع امتناع يتصور جسماً لا يكون في حيز ، ومنها أنا نقطع بأن كلا من القطب الجنوبي، والشمالي في حيز آخر ، وأن كل نقطة على سطح الفلك المحيط يتحرك بحركة من موضع إلى موضع . وبالجملة فهذه وأمثالها أمارات تفيد قوة الظن بأن المكان هو البعد لا السطح، وإن كان للمناقشة مجال في استحالة بعض اللوازم ، أو في لزومها على ما لا يخفى .
He said: The argument of the void: Those who hold that place is the void argue that if it were surface, many things that reason judges by necessity would be negated. Among them: equality of place and occupant – if we make a round candle into a thin sheet, the surrounding surface becomes many times that of the round one, etc.; that every body is in a place, while the body encompassing all has no body to contain it; the rest of the bird in the air when winds blow – the surfaces surrounding it change, while change of places is either motion or its necessary concomitant; the motion of the revolving moon – its surrounding surface from its sphere is one and does not change, and non-change of place is necessary for rest; the persistence of the place Zayd left even after air fills it; that every part of a body is in a place, while internal parts like water are not in a surface except metaphorically; that the judgment of a body being here or there does not depend on it being surrounded by a body; that we can imagine a body touching nothing, but cannot imagine a body not in a place; that we are certain that each of the south and north poles is in a different place, and each point on the surface of the surrounding sphere moves from one position to another. In summary, these and similar indications give strong probability that place is void, not surface, although there is room for discussion in the impossibility of some necessary consequences or in their necessity.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for place being void (bu'd) rather than surface. The author lists eight sign-arguments (amarat) that the void proponents use. Each argument shows an absurdity or impossibility that would follow if place were surface, thereby supporting the void theory. The arguments cover: (1) equality of place and occupant despite shape changes; (2) the outermost sphere having no container; (3) bird's rest implying changing surfaces; (4) moon's motion with constant surface; (5) Zayd's place remaining after air fills; (6) internal parts not having their own surface; (7) independence of location judgment from being surrounded; (8) imaginability of a body with no contact but not without place. The author concludes that these give strong probability (zann) but not certainty, and there is still room for discussion.

مكان
