Critique of Ibn Sina's Descriptions of Psychic and Sensible Qualities
Critique of Ibn Sina's descriptions of psychic, dispositional, and sensible qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Imam's critique of Ibn Sina's definitions of psychic qualities, dispositions, and sensible qualities, and the inconsistency regarding heaviness and lightness.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's definitions of qualities are inconsistent and exclude some sensible qualities.

This lesson examines the first part of a critique of Ibn Sina's classification of qualities. The Imam contrasts his own expression of psychic qualities as perfection with Ibn Sina's negative definition, and exposes problems with Ibn Sina's description of sensible qualities as acting by assimilation. We will see how this leads to contradictions within Ibn Sina's own works.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on psychic qualities and dispositions
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the contrast between the Imam's and Ibn Sina's expressions. Notice that the Imam characterizes psychic qualities as perfection, while Ibn Sina defines them negatively as 'what does not pertain to bodies'.
The term 'psychic qualities' refers to states like happiness, sadness, etc.
2
Analyze Ibn Sina's definition of sensible qualities and the assimilation criterion
Ibn Sina describes sensible qualities as active and acting by assimilation (making something else similar). Read the examples of heat and blackness, and note the exception of heaviness, which acts by movement.
Assimilation means that the quality transfers its likeness to another object.
3
Understand the Imam's objection regarding heaviness and lightness
The Imam points out that this description implicitly excludes heaviness and lightness, but Ibn Sina elsewhere in the Shifā' includes them. Also note the doubt about wet and dry acting by assimilation.
This shows inconsistency in Ibn Sina's classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتعبير الإمام عن الكيفيات النفسانية بالكمال وتعبير ابن سينا عنها بما لا يتعلق بالأجسام، وعن الاستعدادات بما يخص الجسم من حيث الطبيعة وعن المحسوسات بما يكون هويتها (١) أنها ،فعل، وبعضها ليس شاملاً للأفراد كتعبيره عن المحسوسات بما يكون فعله بطريق التشبيه، أي جعل الغير شبيهاً به كالحرارة تجعل المجاور حاراً، والسواد يلقي شبحه(٢) أي مثاله على العين لا كالثقل فإن فعله في الجسم التحريك ، لا الثقل (۳). قال الإمام : وهذا تصريح منه : وهذا تصريح منه بإخراج الثقل والخفة عن (٤) المحسوسات مع تصريحه في موضع آخر من الشفاء (٥) بأنهما منها، وذكر في موضع آخر من أنه لم يثبت بالبرهان أن الرطب .. يجعل غيره رطباً، واليابس يجعل غيره يابساً، وكتعبيره عن الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات، بما يتعلق بالجسم من حيث الكمية، قال الإمام : وهذا تصنيع (٦) الكيفية المختصة بالعدد يعني من جهة أنها قد تتعلق بالمجردات، وبهذا اعترض على قولهم (٧) إن البحث عن أحوال ما يستغنى عن المادة في الذهن دون الخارج هي الرياضيات بأن من جملتها البحث عن أحوال العدد، وهو يستغنى عن المادة في الخارج أيضاً.
Like the Imam's expression for psychic qualities as perfection (kamāl) and Ibn Sina's expression for them as what does not pertain to bodies; and for dispositions as what pertains to the body from the aspect of nature; and for sensible qualities as those whose identity is that they are active, and some are not comprehensive of individuals, like his expression for sensible qualities as those whose action is by way of assimilation (tashbīh), i.e., making something else similar to it, like heat makes what is adjacent hot, and blackness casts its image (shabah) onto the eye—not like heaviness, for its action in the body is movement, not heaviness. The Imam said: This is a clear statement from him excluding heaviness and lightness from sensible qualities, while he stated in another place in the Shifā' that they are among them, and he mentioned in another place that it is not proven by demonstration that wet makes other wet and dry makes other dry. And like his expression for qualities specific to quantities as what pertains to the body from the aspect of quantity. The Imam said: This is a construction (taṣnī‘) of the quality specific to number (al-‘adad), meaning from the aspect that it may pertain to immaterial entities, and by this he objected to their saying that the inquiry into states of what is independent of matter in the mind but not in the exterior is mathematics, because among them is inquiry into states of number, and it is independent of matter in the exterior as well.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage critiques Ibn Sina's descriptions of the four kinds of quality. It begins by noting that Ibn Sina expresses psychic qualities as 'what does not pertain to bodies,' while the Imam (al-Rāzī?) expresses them as 'perfection.' For dispositions, Ibn Sina says they pertain to the body from a natural aspect. For sensible qualities, Ibn Sina says they are active, and further describes them as acting by assimilation, like heat making adjacent things hot, but notes that heaviness does not act by assimilation (it causes movement, not heaviness). The Imam points out that this implicitly excludes heaviness and lightness from sensible qualities, yet Ibn Sina elsewhere in the Shifā' includes them. Also, Ibn Sina doubts whether wet and dry act by assimilation. Regarding qualities specific to quantities, Ibn Sina defines them as pertaining to the body from the aspect of quantity, which the Imam criticizes as only fitting a quality specific to number, because number can also pertain to immaterial things. The Imam then objects to the claim that mathematics deals with what is independent of matter only in the mind, since the study of number is independent of matter in the exterior as well.

الكيفيات النفسانية
الاستعدادات

Correction: The text shows he contradicts himself by excluding heaviness and lightness here but including them in the Shifā'.

Critique of Ibn Sina's Description of Qualities Specific to Quantities and the Nature of Mathematics
Critique of Ibn Sina's description of qualities specific to quantities and the objection regarding mathematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Imam criticizes Ibn Sina's definition of qualities specific to quantities and uses it to object to a common characterization of mathematics.

Core Idea
Number can pertain to immaterial entities, so mathematics is not limited to what is independent of matter only in the mind.

In this final part of the critique, the Imam turns to Ibn Sina's description of qualities that are specific to quantities. He points out that Ibn Sina's definition is too narrow and only fits number, which can also be abstract. This leads to an objection against the claim that mathematics deals only with what is independent of matter in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on qualities specific to quantities
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It contains Ibn Sina's definition, the Imam's criticism, and the objection regarding mathematics.
2
Understand the Imam's criticism of the definition
The Imam says that Ibn Sina's definition of qualities specific to quantities as 'what pertains to the body from the aspect of quantity' is really a construction (taṣnī‘) of the quality specific to number, because number can pertain to immaterial entities as well.
For example, numbers like 'two' can be abstractly conceived without matter.
3
Analyze the objection regarding mathematics
The Imam points out that if number is independent of matter in the exterior, then the claim that mathematics deals with what is independent of matter only in the mind is false, since the study of number is part of mathematics.
This challenges the definition of mathematics as limited to mental abstraction.

Commentary
This passage critiques Ibn Sina's descriptions of the four kinds of quality. It begins by noting that Ibn Sina expresses psychic qualities as 'what does not pertain to bodies,' while the Imam (al-Rāzī?) expresses them as 'perfection.' For dispositions, Ibn Sina says they pertain to the body from a natural aspect. For sensible qualities, Ibn Sina says they are active, and further describes them as acting by assimilation, like heat making adjacent things hot, but notes that heaviness does not act by assimilation (it causes movement, not heaviness). The Imam points out that this implicitly excludes heaviness and lightness from sensible qualities, yet Ibn Sina elsewhere in the Shifā' includes them. Also, Ibn Sina doubts whether wet and dry act by assimilation. Regarding qualities specific to quantities, Ibn Sina defines them as pertaining to the body from the aspect of quantity, which the Imam criticizes as only fitting a quality specific to number, because number can also pertain to immaterial things. The Imam then objects to the claim that mathematics deals with what is independent of matter only in the mind, since the study of number is independent of matter in the exterior as well.

Key Terms
الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات
العدد
المجردات
الرياضيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the Imam say Ibn Sina's definition of qualities specific to quantities is really a construction of?
A) Body
B) Number
C) Disposition
D) Sensible quality

Key Takeaway
The Imam uses the example of number to show that mathematics can be about what is independent of matter both in mind and exterior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider numbers like 'two' or 'three', do you typically think of them as mere mental tools or as real, independent entities? How does this lesson challenge you to re-evaluate your view of immaterial realities, such as the attributes of Allah?
?

?

In what area of your faith—whether belief in angels, the afterlife, or the nature of the soul—are you allowing this materialistic assumption to weaken your conviction? What concrete step can you take today to affirm the reality of the immaterial?

Critique of Definitions of Sensible Qualities
Critiques definitions of sensible qualities, dispositions, and more.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inconsistencies in Ibn Sina's and Imam al-Ghazali's definitions of psychological qualities, dispositions, sensibles, and qualities of quantities.

Core Idea
Definitions can fail to cover all instances or contradict other statements.

Imam al-Ghazali and Ibn Sina proposed definitions for various categories of qualities. However, their definitions sometimes exclude relevant instances or contradict what they said elsewhere. In this passage, the author critiques these definitions, focusing on psychological qualities, dispositions, sensibles, and qualities of quantities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of critiques.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the four categories critiqued
Notice that the critiques target psychological qualities, dispositions, sensibles, and qualities of quantities.
Each category has a specific criticism.
3
Examine the critique of sensibles
Focus on the criticism that Ibn Sina's definition of sensibles as 'act by imitation' does not include heaviness and lightness.
This shows inconsistency in Ibn Sina's own works.
4
Analyze the critique of qualities of quantities
Understand the objection that Ibn Sina's definition would include number, which is independent of matter externally.
This challenges the scope of mathematics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتعبير الإمام عن الكيفيات النفسانية بالكمال وتعبير ابن سينا عنها بما لا يتعلق بالأجسام، وعن الاستعدادات بما يخص الجسم من حيث الطبيعة وعن المحسوسات بما يكون هويتها أنها فعل، وبعضها ليس شاملاً للأفراد كتعبيره عن المحسوسات بما يكون فعله بطريق التشبيه، أي جعل الغير شبيهاً به كالحرارة تجعل المجاور حاراً، والسواد يلقي شبحه أي مثاله على العين لا كالثقل فإن فعله في الجسم التحريك، لا الثقل. قال الإمام : وهذا تصريح منه بإخراج الثقل والخفة عن المحسوسات مع تصريحه في موضع آخر من الشفاء بأنهما منها، وذكر في موضع آخر من أنه لم يثبت بالبرهان أن الرطب [؟] يجعل غيره رطباً، واليابس يجعل غيره يابساً، وكتعبيره عن الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات، بما يتعلق بالجسم من حيث الكمية، قال الإمام : وهذا تصنيع الكيفية المختصة بالعدد يعني من جهة أنها قد تتعلق بالمجردات، وبهذا اعترض على قولهم إن البحث عن أحوال ما يستغنى عن المادة في الذهن دون الخارج هي الرياضيات بأن من جملتها البحث عن أحوال العدد، وهو يستغنى عن المادة في الخارج أيضاً.
Such as the Imam's expression of the psychological qualities with perfection, and Ibn Sina's expression of them with what does not relate to bodies, and of dispositions with what is specific to the body from the perspective of nature, and of sensibles with what their quiddity is that it is an act; and some of them are not inclusive of individuals, like his expression of sensibles with what its action is by way of imitation, i.e., making another similar to it, like heat makes the adjacent hot, and blackness casts its phantom, i.e., its likeness, on the eye, not like heaviness, for its action in the body is movement, not heaviness. The Imam said: And this is a clear statement from him removing heaviness and lightness from the sensibles, while he stated in another place of the Shifa' that they are among them; and he mentioned in another place that it has not been proven by demonstration that the wet [؟] makes another wet, and the dry makes another dry; and his expression of qualities specific to quantities with what relates to the body from the perspective of quantity, the Imam said: And this is a fabrication of the quality specific to number, meaning with respect to that it may relate to immaterial things, and with this he objected to their statement that the investigation of the states of what is independent of matter in the mind but not in the external is mathematics, because among them is the investigation of the states of number, and it is independent of matter externally as well.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of various definitions of sensible qualities (الكيفيات المحسوسة) as proposed by Imam al-Ghazali and Ibn Sina. The author examines the definitions for comprehensiveness and consistency. First, the Imam defines psychological qualities (الكيفيات النفسانية) as perfection, and Ibn Sina defines them as what does not relate to bodies. For dispositions (الاستعدادات), Ibn Sina defines them as what is specific to the body from the perspective of nature. For sensibles (المحسوسات), Ibn Sina defines them as those whose quiddity is an act, but the author points out that this definition is not inclusive of all individuals, because some sensibles act by imitation (like heat making another hot, or blackness casting its image on the eye), while others like heaviness have a different mode of action (movement, not heaviness itself). Then the author reports that the Imam criticizes Ibn Sina for excluding heaviness and lightness from sensibles in this definition, while elsewhere in the Shifa' Ibn Sina includes them. Furthermore, Ibn Sina mentions that it has not been proven that the wet makes another wet etc. Then the author discusses Ibn Sina's definition of qualities specific to quantities as what relates to the body from the perspective of quantity. The Imam objects that this would include the quality specific to number, which can also relate to immaterial things, thus challenging the classification of mathematics as dealing only with what is independent of matter in the mind but not externally, since number is independent externally as well.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sensible qualities act by imparting similarity to their subjects.
Correction: Heaviness does not make another heavy; it causes movement, so the definition of sensibles as 'act by imitation' is not comprehensive.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Definitions must cover all instances and be consistent across an author's works; otherwise they invite critique.

The Five Types of Sensed Qualities
Sa'd al-Taftazani enumerates the five types of sensed qualities.

Core Idea
Sensed qualities are divided into five distinct types based on the five senses.

This passage introduces the foundational classification of sensed qualities (al-kayfiyyāt al-maḥsūsah) in Islamic theology. Before discussing their metaphysical implications, scholars first enumerate the types according to the five external senses. As you read, note how each term corresponds to a sense organ.

Guided Path

4

Stable and Unstable Qualities in Kalam
Classifies qualities into stable and unstable with examples and naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between stable and unstable qualities in Islamic theology and the terms used for each.

Core Idea
All qualities are either stable (rāsikha) or fleeting (infi'ālāt).

In this passage, the author categorizes qualities into two types: those that are stable and those that are not. Stable qualities are referred to as states or dispositions, while unstable ones are called affections. This classification helps in understanding how different attributes are described in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key terms for stable and unstable qualities.
Focus on the words 'راسخة' and 'غير راسخة'.
2
Identify the two categories

Stable qualities are called hay'ah or malakah, unstable are called infi'ālāt.
3
Examine the examples
Consider the examples given: heat of fire and whiteness. Why are they considered unstable? What does 'like being affected' mean?
The passage suggests these qualities are quick to pass, hence termed affections.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نوعاً لحرارة النار، وغيرها، لا حقيقياً ولا إضافياً، وكذا البياض لبياض الثلج والعاج على ما سيجيء، وإن كانت غير راسخة سميت انفعالات، لأنها لسرعة زوالها شديدة الشبه بأن ينفعل، فخصت بهذا الاسم تمييزاً بين القسمين.

Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage classifies qualities (probably accidents) into two kinds. First, stable qualities (rāsikha) which are termed 'hay'ah' (state) or 'malakah' (disposition), and second, unstable qualities (ghayr rāsikha) which are called 'infi'ālāt' (affections). The author gives examples: the heat of fire and whiteness (of snow and ivory) are not real or relative but inherent. The unstable ones are likened to being affected because they vanish quickly. The naming distinguishes the two categories.

Tangible Qualities and Their Investigations
Introduces tangible qualities and lists two investigations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two investigations into tangible qualities: their foundation in heat and the debate over i'timād.

Core Idea
Tangible qualities begin with heat, and their nature is debated among theologians.

The passage opens the discussion of tangible qualities by stating their foundational element—heat—and then flags a debate about whether reliance (i'timād) is identical to sensible resistance. These two points set the stage for deeper analysis of physical attributes in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

The passage is dense but concise.
2

3

The Unity of Heat and the Nature of Innate Heat
Discussion on the concept of heat and views on innate heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'hot' refers to a single sensible quality despite different types of heat, and you will learn the three main scholarly views on innate heat.

Core Idea
'Hot' names one quality, but innate heat has three theories.

The author opens by clarifying a linguistic point: the word 'hot' is not used ambiguously across different types of heat; rather, it points to a common sensible quality. This leads into a deeper investigation of one type of heat—innate heat—which is essential for life. Scholars differed: is it fiery, celestial, or something else altogether? This passage presents those views and a key response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the use of terms like 'الحرارة الغريزية' and 'الكيفية المحسوسة'.
2
Identify the main claim about 'hot'
The author argues against the idea that 'hot' is used homonymously. Find the sentence that asserts it is one concept: 'ليس بحسب حينئذ اشتراك اللفظ' and note the reason given.
The key is the 'specific sensible quality' (الكيفية المحسوسة المخصوصة).
3
Note the three views on innate heat
The passage presents three views: Imam al-Razi (fiery), Aristotle (celestial), and 'some' (distinct essence). List each view and its supporting reasoning.
Also pay attention to the objection about resistance and the response.
4
Consider the response to the objection
Imam al-Razi responds to the objection that innate heat resists foreign heat. Understand his reasoning: innate heat's cooking and ripening produce unity that disperses foreign heat.
This shows that the specificity of innate heat may not require a different essence.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that innate heat could be either fiery or celestial, with its specific effects explained by its occurrence in a balanced body or being part of the temperament.
This leaves the question open, but clarifies the conceptual framework.

وقد يقال : الحار إطلاق الحرارة على حرارة النار، وعلى الحرارة الفائضة عن الأجرام السماوية النيرة، وعلى الحرارة الغريزية وعلى الحرارة الحادثة بالحركة، ليس بحسب حينئذ اشتراك اللفظ على ما يتوهم لأنه كمفهوم واحد هو الكيفية المحسوسة المخصوصة، وإن كانت الحرارات متخالفة بالحقيقة، واختلاف المفهوم إنما هو في إطلاق الحار على مثل النار وعلى الأجرام السماوية التي تفيض منها الحرارة وعلى الدواء والغذاء اللذين يظهر منهما حرارة في بدن الحيوان وهو في كل من الكواكب والدواء والغذاء صفة مسماة بالحرارة كالكيفية المحسوسة في النار أم ذلك توسع وإطلاق للحار على ما منه الحرارة، وإن لم يقم به معنى مسمى بالحرارة فيه، تردد واختلفوا في الحرارة الغريزية التي بها قوام حياة الحيوان فاختار الإمام الرازي أنها هي النارية، فإن النار إذا خالطت سائر العناصر أفادت حرارتها للمركب طبخاً واعتدالاً وقواماً لتوسطها بانكسار سورتها عند تفاعل العناصر بين الكثرة المفيضة إلى إبطال القوام، والقلة القاصرة عن الطبخ الموجب للاعتدال، فتلك الحرارة هي بالحرارة الغريزية، وحكي عن أرسطو أنها من جنس الحرارة التي تفيض من الأجرام السماوية، فإن المزاج المعتدل يوجه ما يناسب لجوهر السماء لأنه يبعث عنه، يعني أنه إذا امتزجت العناصر وانكسرت سورة كيفياتها حصل للمركب نوع وحدة وبساطة بها يناسب البساطة السماوية، ففاضت عليه [؟] المسماة [؟] مزاج معتدل به حفظ التركيب وحرارة غريزية بها قوام الحياة، وقبول علاقة النفس، وبعضهم على أنها مخالفة بالماهية للحرارة النارية والحرارة السماوية لاختصاصها بمقاومة الحرارة الغريزية ودفعها عن الاستيلاء على الرطوبات الغريزية، وإبطال الاعتدال حتى إن السموم الحارة لا تدفعها إلا الحرارة الغريزية، فإنها آلة للطبيعة تدفع ضرر الحار الوارد بتحريك الروح إلى دفعه وضرر البارد الوارد بالمضادة. وأجاب الإمام : بأن تلك المقاومة إنما هي من جهة أن الحرارة الغريبة تحاول التفريق، والغريزية أفادت من النضج والطبخ ما يتعبر عنده كالحرارة الغريبة تفريق تلك الأجزاء، وبالجملة يجوز أن تكون هي الحرارة السماوية أو النارية، ومستند آثاره المختصة بها إلى خصوصية حصولها في البدن المعتدل، أو صيرورتها جزءاً من المزاج الخاص.
And it may be said: the hot (al-ḥār) is the application of heat (al-ḥarāra) to the heat of fire, and to the heat emanating from the luminous celestial bodies, and to the innate heat (al-ḥarāra al-gharīziyya) and to the heat arising from motion. It is not, then, a homonymous usage (ishtirāk al-lafẓ) as one might suppose, because it is one concept (mafhūm wāḥid) which is the specific sensible quality (al-kayfiyya al-maḥsūsa al-makhṣūṣa), even if the heats differ in reality. The difference in application is only in applying 'hot' to something like fire, and to the celestial bodies from which heat emanates, and to the medicine and food from which heat appears in the animal body. In each of the planets, medicine, and food, it is a quality called heat, like the sensible quality in fire. Or is that an extension and application of 'hot' to that from which heat comes, even if the meaning called heat does not exist in it? There is hesitation, and they differed concerning the innate heat by which the constitution of animal life subsists. Imam al-Razi chose that it is the fiery heat (al-nāriyya). For when fire mixes with the other elements, its heat benefits the compound by cooking (ṭabkh), moderation (iʿtidāl), and constitution (qiwām) through its mediation, by the breaking of its force (sawra) during the interaction of the elements, between excess that leads to destruction of constitution and deficiency that prevents cooking that requires moderation. Thus that heat is the innate heat. And it is narrated from Aristotle that it is of the genus of heat that emanates from celestial bodies; for the balanced temperament (al-mizāj al-muʿtadil) directs what is suitable to the substance of heaven because it is sent from it. He means that when the elements mix and the force of their qualities breaks, the compound acquires a kind of unity and simplicity by which it corresponds to celestial simplicity. Then there flows upon it [?] what is called [?] a balanced temperament by which the composition is preserved, and an innate heat by which life subsists, and the reception of the soul's relation. Some hold that it is different in essence from fiery heat and celestial heat, due to its specificity in resisting the innate heat and repelling it from dominating the innate moistures (al-ruṭūbāt al-gharīziyya) and destroying moderation, so that even hot poisons are not repelled except by the innate heat, for it is nature's instrument repelling the harm of incoming heat by moving the spirit to drive it away, and the harm of incoming cold by opposition. And the Imam answered: that resistance is only because the foreign heat attempts dispersion, while the innate heat, from ripening and cooking, has produced something such that the foreign heat disperses those parts. In summary, it is possible that it be the celestial heat or the fiery heat, and the basis of its specific effects lies in the particularity of its occurrence in the balanced body, or its becoming part of the special temperament.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the term 'hot' and the nature of innate heat (al-ḥarāra al-gharīziyya). The author begins by clarifying that the application of the term 'hot' to various types of heat—fire, celestial, innate, and that from motion—is not mere homonymy but refers to a single concept: the specific sensible quality. However, the reality of these heats may differ. The author then notes a hesitation: is it that the term is applied to the quality itself, or is it an extension to that which produces heat? This leads into the main topic: different opinions on innate heat. Imam al-Razi holds it to be fiery heat, explaining how fire's heat when mixed with elements produces cooking, moderation, and constitution. Aristotle holds it to be of the celestial type, arguing that the balanced temperament acquires a simplicity akin to the celestial. Some others hold it to be different in essence from both, citing its unique property of resisting external heat and maintaining moderation; for example, hot poisons are repelled only by innate heat. The Imam (al-Razi) responds that this resistance is due to the different effects of foreign and innate heat; the innate heat's cooking and ripening produce unity that disperses foreign heat, and it is possible that innate heat is either celestial or fiery, with its specific effects arising from its occurrence in a balanced body.

Correction: The author insists that 'hot' refers to a single concept—the specific sensible quality—even if the heats differ in reality.

B) A specific sensible quality (al-kayfiyya al-maḥsūsa al-makhṣūṣa)

Key Takeaway
The term 'hot' denotes a single sensible quality, and innate heat is debated among three theories: fiery, celestial, or a distinct essence.

?
The author leaves the nature of innate heat unresolved. Are you comfortable with not having a final answer about what sustains your own life? Does this uncertainty drive you to a state of humility and reliance on the Creator, or does it provoke anxiety? How will you actively cultivate trust in Allah's design despite such ambiguity?

Imam al-Razi argued that innate heat's effects come from its specific location in the body, not a different essence. How does this passage challenge you to see your spiritual life as intimately tied to your physical temperament, and what responsibility does that place on you to maintain your health and balance?

The Definition of Moisture and Objections Concerning Adhesion and Shape
The author examines definitions of moisture and responds to criticisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different proposed definitions of moisture (al-ruṭūba) and the key objections raised against them, particularly concerning adhesion and ease of shaping.

Core Idea
Moisture is not simply adhesion; ease of shaping is the true criterion.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid explores the precise definition of moisture. The author presents objections to two proposed definitions—adhesion and ease of shaping—along with their refutations. Through this examination, we gain insight into how kalam scholars refine technical terms to accurately describe physical qualities. By following the arguments, you will grasp the nuance required in defining simple qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the flow of objections and answers.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two definitions of moisture
The passage discusses definitions based on adhesion and ease of shaping. Note the objections raised against each.
Also note the intermediate definitions proposed by predecessors and Ibn Sina.
3
Understand the refutations
Examine how each objection is answered: for adhesion, the criterion is ease of adhesion with separation; for ease of shaping, the answer involves the nature of fire and definition of softness.
The hesitation about softness/hardness is a side point.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note Ibn Sina's refutation and the Imam's alternative definition. Consider which definition seems most plausible.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the main argument is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأورد على اعتبار الالتصاق أنه يوجب كون العسل أرطب من الماء فدفع بأن المراد سهولة الالتصاق، بل مع سهولة الانفصال، وعلى اعتبار سهولة التشكل أنه يوجب رطوبة النار، وكون اللين في الرطوبة. وأجيب بمنع سهولة التشكل في النار البسيطة، وبأن اللين كيفية تقتضي قبول الغمز إلى الباطن مع عسر تفرق الأجزاء، وفي كون اللين والصلابة من الملموسات، أو الاستعدادات تردد. قال : وأورد المذكور في كلام بعض المتقدمين أن الجسم إنما كان رطباً إذا كان بحيث يلتصق بما يلامسه وفهم منه أن الرطوبة كيفية تقتضي التصاق الجسم، ورده ابن سينا بأن الالتصاق لو كان للرطوبة لكان الأشد رطوبة أشد التصاقاً، فيكون العسل أرطب من الماء، بل المعتبر في الرطوبة سهولة قبول الشكل وتركه فهي كيفية بها يكون الجسم سهل التشكل، وسهل الترك للشكل. وأجاب الإمام بأن المعتبر فيها سهولة الالتصاق ويلزمها بالغير سهولة
He said: It was objected concerning adhesion (al-iltiṣāq) that it would make honey wetter (arṭab) than water. He refuted by saying that what is meant is ease of adhesion (suhūlat al-iltiṣāq), especially with ease of separation. And concerning ease of shaping (suhūlat al-tashakkul) that it would imply the moisture (ruṭūba) of fire, and that softness (al-līn) is in moisture. It was answered by preventing (manʿ) ease of shaping in simple fire, and that softness is a quality that requires accepting pressure into the interior with difficulty of separation of parts. And concerning whether softness and hardness (al-ṣalāba) are among the tangible things (al-malmūsāt) or dispositions (al-istiʿdādāt), there is hesitation. He said: It was objected from what is mentioned in the speech of some predecessors that a body is wet if it adheres to what touches it, and it was understood that moisture is a quality requiring adhesion of the body. Ibn Sina refuted this: if adhesion were due to moisture, then the moister would be more adhesive, so honey would be wetter than water. Rather, the criterion in moisture is ease of accepting shape and leaving it; so it is a quality by which the body is easy to shape (sahl al-tashakkul) and easy to leave the shape. And the Imam answered that the criterion is ease of adhesion, and ease [of shape] follows from it.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage deals with the definition of moisture (al-ruṭūba) and addresses criticisms of different definitions. It begins with an objection to a definition based on adhesion (al-iltiṣāq): if moisture means adhesion, then honey, being stickier, would be wetter than water. The answer clarifies that the intended meaning is ease of adhesion (suhūlat al-iltiṣāq), especially combined with ease of separation. Next, an objection concerning ease of shaping (suhūlat al-tashakkul): if moisture is defined by ease of shaping, then fire would be wet. The answer is that simple fire lacks ease of shaping, and softness (al-līn) is defined as accepting pressure into the interior with difficulty of separation. The passage then notes a hesitation about whether softness and hardness are tangible qualities or dispositions. Then, a further objection is cited from some predecessors who defined moisture as adhesion to what touches it, making it a quality requiring adhesion. Ibn Sina refutes this: if adhesion were the criterion, then the moister would adhere more, making honey wetter than water; instead, the true criterion is ease of accepting and leaving shape. The Imam (al-Razi) responds that the criterion is ease of adhesion, and ease of shaping follows from that.

الالتصاق
سهولة التشكل

الصلابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moisture is simply the quality of adhesion (iltiṣāq) to other bodies.
Correction: Moisture involves ease of adhesion with ease of separation, or ease of shaping. Mere adhesion would make honey wetter than water, which is false.

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Sina reject the definition of moisture as adhesion (iltiṣāq)?
A) Because fire is also adhesive
B) Because it would make honey wetter than water
C) Because ease of shaping is more fundamental
D) Because adhesion cannot be measured

Key Takeaway
Moisture is defined by ease of adhesion combined with ease of separation, or by ease of shaping, not by mere adhesion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have probably many times mistaken outward stickiness for true wetness, just as the scholars warn. Is there an area in your life where you are confusing surface appearances with deeper realities—perhaps in your judgments of people's character? Take a moment to write down one assumption you hold that might be reversed upon closer examination.
?
The scholars debated fiercely to get the definition right because incorrect definitions lead to false conclusions. When have you held onto a wrong definition or label that caused you to misunderstand something important about your faith or yourself? Are you willing to revise it today, even if it humbles you?
?
Ibn Sina's refutation shows that a single logical misstep can cascade into absurdity. Are you aware of any belief or habit in your life that, if followed to its logical end, would lead to an outcome you know is false? Commit to tracing one such chain today and cutting it at its root.

Definitions of Tangible Qualities
Lists and defines seven tangible qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of seven tangible qualities: dryness, viscosity, friability, subtlety, density, numbness, and tearing.

Core Idea
Tangible qualities are defined by their effects on moisture, shape, separation, and sensation.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid lists and defines several key tangible qualities that are important in the study of physical bodies. Each quality is described in terms of its effect on the properties of objects, such as ease of shaping, separation, and transmission of sensation. The definitions provide a precise vocabulary for discussing the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, paying attention to how each quality is defined. Notice the contrast between viscosity and friability, and the multiple applications of subtlety.
The definition of subtlety has four applications listed.
2
Identify opposite pairs
Note that viscosity and friability are opposites, as are subtlety and density. Understanding these oppositions helps clarify each quality.
3
Understand numbness and tearing
Numbness involves cooling and thickening that blocks psychic powers, while tearing involves many small separations felt collectively. Reflect on how these differ from other qualities.
Numbness affects the spirit carrying sensation and motion.
4
Relate to everyday examples
The text gives examples for subtlety: water, air, sugar candy, rose, celestial sphere. Try to connect these to your own experience.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من شدة الانتفاع (۱) وإلا ظهر (۳) أن الجفاف عدم ملكة البلة واللزوجة وهي كيفية تمتضي سهولة الشكل مع عسر التفرق واتصال الامتداد، وتحدث امتزاج الرطب الكثير باليابس القليل، ويقابله الهشاشة، وهي ما تقتضي صعوبة التشكل (۳) ، وسهولة التفرق واللطافة . وقد يقال لرقة القوام كما في الماء والهواء ولسهولة قبول الانقسام إلى أجزاء صغيرة جداً كما في القند) ، ولسرعة التأثر من الملاقي كما في الورد وللشفافية كما في الفلك، والكثافة تقابلها بمعاينها، والتخدير (ه) وهـو تبريد للعضو بحيث يصير جوهر الروح الحامل قوة الحس والحركة إليه بارداً في مزاجه غليظاً في جوهر ، فلا تستعملها القوى النفسانية، ويجعل مزاج العضو كذلك ، فلا يقبل (٦) تأثير القوى النفسانية، والمزع (۷) وهي كيفية نفاذه جداً لطيفة تحدث في الاتصال تفرقاً كثير العدد، متقارب (۸) الوضع، صغير المقدار، فلا يحس كل واحد بانفراده ويحس بالجملة كالوجع الواحد، وأما الملاسة والخشونة فالجمهور على أنها من الكيفيات الملوسة، وقال الإمام بل من الوضع، لأن الملاسة عبارة عن استواء أجزاء الجسم في الوضع بحيث لا يكون بعضها أرفع وبعضها أخفض والخشونة عن اختلافها، ورد بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك مبدأهما لأنفسهما .
From the intensity of benefiting (1) otherwise it appears (3) that dryness is the lack of the faculty of moisture, and viscosity is a quality requiring ease of shape with difficulty of separation and continuity of extension, and it occurs from mixing much wet with little dry, and its opposite is friability, which is what requires difficulty of shaping (3) and ease of separation and subtlety. And it is sometimes said for thinness of consistency as in water and air, and for ease of accepting division into very small parts as in sugar candy, and for quickness of being affected by contact as in a rose, and for transparency as in the celestial sphere; and density is its opposite in its meanings. And numbness (5) is a cooling of the limb such that the substance of the spirit carrying the power of sensation and motion becomes cold in its temperament and thick in its substance, so the psychic powers do not use it, and it makes the temperament of the limb likewise, so it does not accept (6) the influence of the psychic powers. And tearing (7) is a quality very penetrating and subtle that occurs in the continuity as a separation many in number, close in position, small in amount, so that each one is not felt individually but is felt collectively like a single pain. As for smoothness and roughness, the majority hold that they are among the qualities of touch, and al-Imam said rather they are from position, because smoothness is the evenness of parts of the body in position such that none is higher and none lower, and roughness is from their difference; and it was replied that it is possible that that is the principle of the two themselves.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines several tangible qualities and then presents a debate on smoothness and roughness. It begins by defining dryness as the privation of the faculty of moisture, and viscosity as a quality that facilitates shape but hinders separation and maintains continuity. Friability is the opposite, making shaping difficult but separation easy. Subtlety is used for thin consistency, divisibility, quick affectability, and transparency; density is its counterpart. Numbness is described as a cooling and thickening of the limb's spirit, incapacitating psychic powers. Tearing is a fine, penetrating quality causing many small separations felt collectively. Finally, the majority position holds that smoothness and roughness are qualities of touch, while al-Imam (al-Razi) argues they belong to the category of position (wad') because they consist in the evenness or unevenness of parts. The rebuttal is that these could be the principles of the qualities themselves, not the qualities.

The Debate on Smoothness and Roughness
Presents a debate on the classification of smoothness and roughness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing views on whether smoothness and roughness are qualities of touch or modes of position (wad').

Core Idea
Smoothness and roughness may be about the arrangement of parts, not inherent feel.

After defining numerous tangible qualities, the text turns to a debate about two: smoothness and roughness. The majority view holds they are qualities of touch, but al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argues they belong to the category of position (wad') because they consist in the evenness or unevenness of parts. The rebuttal suggests that evenness/unevenness could be the principle of the qualities themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the two opposing positions: majority vs. al-Imam.
2
Understand al-Imam's argument
Al-Imam defines smoothness as evenness of parts in position, and roughness as difference. Consider why this would make them a mode of position rather than a tactile quality.
He compares it to the category of 'wad'' which is about spatial arrangement.
3
Consider the rebuttal
The rebuttal points out that evenness/unevenness could be the cause (mabda') of smoothness and roughness, not the qualities themselves. This leaves the door open for the majority view.
The rebuttal is concise: 'يجوز أن يكون ذلك مبدأهما لأنفسهما'.

من شدة الانتفاع (۱) وإلا ظهر (۳) أن الجفاف عدم ملكة البلة واللزوجة وهي كيفية تمتضي سهولة الشكل مع عسر التفرق واتصال الامتداد، وتحدث امتزاج الرطب الكثير باليابس القليل، ويقابله الهشاشة، وهي ما تقتضي صعوبة التشكل (۳) ، وسهولة التفرق واللطافة . وقد يقال لرقة القوام كما في الماء والهواء ولسهولة قبول الانقسام إلى أجزاء صغيرة جداً كما في القند) ، ولسرعة التأثر من الملاقي كما في الورد وللشفافية كما في الفلك، والكثافة تقابلها بمعاينها، والتخدير (ه) وهـو تبريد للعضو بحيث يصير جوهر الروح الحامل قوة الحس والحركة إليه بارداً في مزاجه غليظاً في جوهر ، فلا تستعملها القوى النفسانية، ويجعل مزاج العضو كذلك ، فلا يقبل (٦) تأثير القوى النفسانية، والمزع (۷) وهي كيفية نفاذه جداً لطيفة تحدث في الاتصال تفرقاً كثير العدد، متقارب (۸) الوضع، صغير المقدار، فلا يحس كل واحد بانفراده ويحس بالجملة كالوجع الواحد، وأما الملاسة والخشونة فالجمهور على أنها من الكيفيات الملوسة، وقال الإمام بل من الوضع، لأن الملاسة عبارة عن استواء أجزاء الجسم في الوضع بحيث لا يكون بعضها أرفع وبعضها أخفض والخشونة عن اختلافها، ورد بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك مبدأهما لأنفسهما .
From the intensity of benefiting (1) otherwise it appears (3) that dryness is the lack of the faculty of moisture, and viscosity is a quality requiring ease of shape with difficulty of separation and continuity of extension, and it occurs from mixing much wet with little dry, and its opposite is friability, which is what requires difficulty of shaping (3) and ease of separation and subtlety. And it is sometimes said for thinness of consistency as in water and air, and for ease of accepting division into very small parts as in sugar candy, and for quickness of being affected by contact as in a rose, and for transparency as in the celestial sphere; and density is its opposite in its meanings. And numbness (5) is a cooling of the limb such that the substance of the spirit carrying the power of sensation and motion becomes cold in its temperament and thick in its substance, so the psychic powers do not use it, and it makes the temperament of the limb likewise, so it does not accept (6) the influence of the psychic powers. And tearing (7) is a quality very penetrating and subtle that occurs in the continuity as a separation many in number, close in position, small in amount, so that each one is not felt individually but is felt collectively like a single pain. As for smoothness and roughness, the majority hold that they are among the qualities of touch, and al-Imam said rather they are from position, because smoothness is the evenness of parts of the body in position such that none is higher and none lower, and roughness is from their difference; and it was replied that it is possible that that is the principle of the two themselves.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines several tangible qualities and then presents a debate on smoothness and roughness. It begins by defining dryness as the privation of the faculty of moisture, and viscosity as a quality that facilitates shape but hinders separation and maintains continuity. Friability is the opposite, making shaping difficult but separation easy. Subtlety is used for thin consistency, divisibility, quick affectability, and transparency; density is its counterpart. Numbness is described as a cooling and thickening of the limb's spirit, incapacitating psychic powers. Tearing is a fine, penetrating quality causing many small separations felt collectively. Finally, the majority position holds that smoothness and roughness are qualities of touch, while al-Imam (al-Razi) argues they belong to the category of position (wad') because they consist in the evenness or unevenness of parts. The rebuttal is that these could be the principles of the qualities themselves, not the qualities.

I'timād as Resistance: Two Meanings
Defines two meanings of i'timād: as sensible resistance and as its principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of i'timād (cohesion/pressure) in kalām: as the sensible resistance itself and as its underlying principle, and how they are distinguished from motion and nature.

Core Idea
I'timād is not motion or nature—it is the felt resistance that exists even without motion.

This passage from al-Sanūsī's commentary tackles a key contested term in natural philosophy: i'timād, often translated as 'cohesion' or 'pressure'. Some theologians identify it directly with the felt resistance a body offers when prevented from moving. Others see it as an intelligible principle behind that resistance. The passage clarifies both views and grounds them in tangible examples, like a stone suspended in air.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for its overall flow, then the translation. Notice how the text presents two distinct definitions of i'timād.
The passage ends mid-sentence; this is the end of the available text for this section.
2
Identify the two meanings
Identify the two meanings of i'timād: first, as the felt resistance itself (mudāfa'a mahsūsa), and second, as the principle of resistance (mabda’ al-mudāfa'a).
3
Analyze the examples given
Examine the examples: a stone suspended in air and a waterskin under water. Ask yourself: Why do these show that i'timād is distinct from motion and nature?
The passage contrasts these with a body at rest in its natural place, where i'timād is absent while nature remains.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني: فيمن يجعل الاعتماد نفس المدافعة

قال : المبحث الثاني: من الملموسات الاعتماد فيمن يجعله نفس المدافعة المحسوسة لما يمنع الحركة إلى جهة ما لا مبدأها المعقول. قال : قد يراد بالاعتماد المدافعة المحسوسة للجسم، لما يمنعه من الحركة إلى جهة فيكون من الكيفيات الملموسة ولا يقع اشتباه في تحققه ومغايرته للحركة وللطبيعة، لكونه محسوساً يوجد حيث لا حركة، كما في الحجر المسكن في الجو، والزق المفتوح المسكن تحت الماء، وينعدم مع بقاء الطبيعة كما في الجسم الساكن في حيزه الطبيعي، وقد يراد به مبدأ المدافعة فيفسر بكيفية يكون بها الجسم مدافعاً لما يمنعه عن الحركة إلى جهة جميع
The second investigation: concerning those who make al-i'timad (cohesion/pressure) identical to al-mudafa'a (resistance).

He said: The second investigation: among the tangible things is al-i'timad, concerning those who make it identical to the felt resistance that prevents motion toward a direction, not its intelligible principle. He said: Al-i'timad may be intended as the felt resistance of the body to that which prevents it from motion toward a direction, and thus it is among the tangible qualities, and no confusion occurs in its reality and its being distinct from motion and from nature, because it is something sensible that exists where there is no motion, as in a stone suspended in the air, and an open waterskin suspended under water, and it ceases while nature remains, as in a body at rest in its natural place. And it may be intended as the principle of resistance, and is then interpreted as a quality by which the body resists what prevents it from motion toward a direction all
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a definition and analysis of the concept of i'timad (cohesion/pressure) as a tangible quality. It begins by stating the subject: those who consider i'timad to be identical to mudafa'a (resistance). Two meanings are distinguished: first, i'timad as the actual felt resistance that prevents motion; this is a sensible quality distinct from motion and nature, evidenced by situations like a stone suspended in air or a waterskin underwater where resistance is felt without motion. Second, i'timad as the underlying principle or cause of that resistance, which is an intelligible quality that enables the body to resist. The passage emphasizes that the first meaning is directly sensible and not subject to confusion, while the second is a theoretical interpretation. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing on the next page.

Classification of I'timad: Six Directions Reduced to Two Real Ones
Classification of i'timad into six directions and critique reducing to two real ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars reduce the six directional dependencies to only two real types: heaviness and lightness.

Core Idea
Only up and down are real directions; the rest are relative and changeable.

This passage tackles a key conceptual issue in natural philosophy: how to classify the tendencies (i'timad) that cause motion. While one might think there are six directions each with its own property, the author argues that only upward and downward are immutable and thus correspond to real properties of heaviness and lightness. You'll see how the six-direction classification is ultimately conventional.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author first presents the six-direction classification, then immediately qualifies it.
2
Identify the six directions and the distinction
The passage mentions six directions. Find where it says only 'above' and 'below' are natural and real, while the others are relative and change.
The author uses 'natural' (طبيعي) vs 'relative' (إضافي).
3
Understand the two weaknesses
The author gives two arguments against making i'timad into six types. First, only ascending and descending inclinations are immutable. Second, the enumeration into six is merely conventional. Read these carefully.
First argument uses the example of a person inverted; second discusses the origin of the six directions.
4
Grasp the definitions of heaviness and lightness
The passage defines heaviness (thiqal) as a quality that moves a body toward the center of the world, and lightness (khiffa) as the opposite. Note that these correspond to the two real directions.
These are the only real types of i'timad.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
The author concludes that the real types that never change are two: heaviness and lightness. Ask yourself why this conclusion matters for understanding motion.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد يجعل أنواعه ستة بحسب الجهات، إلا أن الطبيعي منها إنما يكون إلى فوق أو تحت لما أنهما الجهتان الحقيقيتان، والبواقي إضافية تتبدل فلا تكون أنواعاً . على أن الحصر في الست عرفي لا حقيقي، إذ الجهات متكثرة جداً كأجزاء الجسم، أو غير منحصرة أصلاً كانقساماته . بیه . فالطبيعي (١) من الاعتماد الثقل، وهي كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم إلى حيث ينطبق مركزه على مركز العالم، وإلى صوب المركز في أكثر المسافة و بين المحيط من غير أن يبلغه والخفة وهي العكس. قال : وقد تجعل أنواعه أي أنواع الاعتماد ستة بحسب الحركات في الجهات الست ويدعى تضادها مطلقاً إن لم تشترط بين المتضادين غاية الخلاف، وإن اشترط انحصر التضاد فيما بين المتقابلين كالاعتماد والصاعد والهابط مثلاً، وفي جعل أنواع الاعتماد شيئاً ضعف من وجهين : أحدهما أن الاعتماد الطبيعي الذي يتصور فيه الاختلاف بالحقيقة إنما الصاعد والهابط أعني الميل إلى العلو والسفل اللذين هما الجهتان الحقيقتيان اللتان لا يتبدلان أصلاً، حتى لو تنكس الإنسان لم يصر فوقه تحتاً، وتحته ،فوقاً، بل صار رجله إلى فوق ورأسه إلى تحت، بخلاف سائر الجهات، فإنها إضافية تتبدل كالمواجه للمشرق إذا واجه المغرب صار قدامه خلفاً ، شمالاً، وبالعكس، فتتبدل الاعتمادات أي يصير اعتماده إلى قدام اعتماداً إلى خلف وبالعكس ، وكذا إلى اليمين والشمال فلا يكون أنواعاً مختلفة . وثانيهما: أن حصر الجهات في الستة أمر عرفي، اعتبره العوام من الإنسان في أن له رأساً ،وقدماً، وظهراً وبطناً، ويميناً وشمالاً، والخواص من حال الجسم في أن له أبعاداً ثلاثة متقاطعة على زوايا قوائم ولكل بعد طرفان، وأما بحسب الحقيقة فالجهات متكثرة جداً، غير محصورة بحسب ما للجسم من الأجزاء عند من يقول بالجوهر الفرد، أو غير متناهية أصلاً، بحسب ما يفرض فيه من الانقسامات عند من لا يقول به . وبالجملة فالحقيقي من أنواع الاعتمادات الذي لا يلحقه التبدل أصلاً اثنان هما الثقل والخفة أعني الميل الهابط والصاعد.
He said: And it may be classified into six types according to the directions, except that the natural of them is only to above or below because they are the two real directions, and the rest are relative and change, so they are not types. And the enumeration into six is conventional, not real, for directions are very numerous like the parts of the body, or originally unlimited like its divisions. So the natural (1) of i'timad is heaviness (thiqal), which is a quality that necessitates the motion of the body to where its center coincides with the center of the world, and towards the center in most of the distance between the center and the circumference without reaching it; and lightness (khiffa) is the opposite. He said: And it may be made into six types according to the movements in the six directions, and their contrariety is called absolute if the utmost difference is not stipulated between the two contraries, and if stipulated, the contrariety is restricted to the opposites like rising and falling, for example. And in making the types of i'timad a thing, there is weakness from two aspects: One is that the natural i'timad in which difference is conceived in reality is only the rising and falling, i.e., the inclination to height and depth, which are the two real directions that do not change at all, such that if a person is inverted, his above does not become below and his below above, but his leg becomes above and his head below, unlike the other directions, for they are relative and change, like the one facing east: if he faces west, his front becomes back, right becomes left, and vice versa. Thus the i'timads change, i.e., his i'timad forward becomes an i'timad backward and vice versa, and similarly to right and left, so they are not different types. The second is that the enumeration of directions into six is a conventional matter, considered by the common people from the human having a head, foot, back, belly, right, and left, and the elite from the state of the body having three dimensions intersecting at right angles, each dimension having two ends. But in reality, directions are very numerous, not limited according to the parts of the body for one who believes in the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), or originally infinite according to the divisions supposed in it for one who does not believe in it. In sum, the real types of i'timad that are not subject to change at all are two: heaviness and lightness, i.e., the descending and ascending inclination.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a classification and critique of the types of i'timad (dependence/weight). The author begins by stating that i'timad can be classified into six types according to the six directions, but immediately qualifies that the natural ones are only upward and downward because these are the two real directions; the others are relative and changeable, hence not true types. He notes that the enumeration of six is conventional, not real, as directions are either very numerous (if we consider parts of the body) or infinite (if we consider endless divisions, depending on one's view on the indivisible atom).

He then explains the natural type: heaviness (thiqal) is a quality that moves a body towards the center of the world, and lightness (khiffa) is the opposite. He then revisits the six-type classification, noting that contrariety among them is either absolute (if no further condition) or restricted to opposites like rising and falling.

He identifies two weaknesses in making i'timad into six types. First, the only real difference in natural i'timad is between ascending and descending inclinations, as these correspond to the two real directions of up and down, which are immutable. He gives a vivid example: if a person is turned upside down, their 'up' and 'down' do not swap; rather their foot becomes up and head down, showing the objectivity of these directions. In contrast, other directions like front/back, left/right are relative to the observer's orientation and thus change, so the i'timads associated with them are not distinct types. Second, the enumeration into six is merely conventional, arising either from common human experience (head, feet, back, belly, right, left) or from the three dimensions of the body, each with two ends. In reality, directions are many or infinite, so the six-type classification is not a real division. He concludes that the real types of i'timad that never change are only two: heaviness (thiqal) and lightness (khiffa), i.e., the descending and ascending inclination.

ثقل (thiqal)
خفة (khiffa)
جهة (jiha)
طبيعي (tabi'i)
إضافي (idafi)
جوهر فرد (jawhar fard)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all six directions correspond to distinct real properties of i'timad.
Correction: Only up and down are immutable; front/back, left/right are relative to orientation and change, so they are not distinct real types.

Checkpoint

B) Forward and backward
C) Right and left

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Absolute and Relative Heaviness in Kalam
Definition of absolute and relative heaviness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam defines absolute and relative heaviness and the cosmic reference points for each.

Core Idea
Heaviness is a quality driving matter toward the world's center.

In classical kalam, heaviness is understood as a real quality that moves bodies toward specific cosmic locations. This lesson unpacks two kinds of heaviness: absolute, which targets the very center of the world, and relative, which moves toward the center but falls short. By learning these definitions, you will see how the elemental world is organized around a central point.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of absolute heaviness
Notice the phrase 'center of gravity' and how it must coincide with the center of the world. The example of earth illustrates this.
The center of gravity is defined as the point where forces balance.
2
Read the definition of relative heaviness
Note the condition 'does not reach the center' and the example of water, which is heavy relative to fire and air but lighter than earth.
3
Compare absolute and relative heaviness
Identify what makes heaviness absolute versus relative: the destination (center vs. near center) and the examples (earth vs. water).
This distinction is crucial for understanding elemental motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالثقل المطلق كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم إلى حيث ينطبق مركز ثقله، أعني النقطة التي تتعادل ما على جوانبها على مركز العالم كما في الأرض، والمضاف كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم في أكثر المسافة الممتدة بين المركز والمحيط حركة إلى المركز، لكنه لا يبلغ المركز كما للماء، فإنه ثقيل بالإضافة إلى النار، والهواء دون الأرض، والخفة المطلقة كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم إلى حيث ينطبق سطحه على سطح مقعر فلك القمر كما للنار والمضاف كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم في أكثر المسافة الممتدة بين المركز والمحيط وحركة إلى المحيط، لكنه لا يبلغ المحيط كما للهواء.
Absolute heaviness is a quality that necessitates the motion of the body to where its center of gravity, i.e., the point at which what is on its sides is balanced, coincides with the center of the world, as in earth; and relative heaviness is a quality that necessitates the motion of the body in most of the distance extending between the center and the circumference, a motion towards the center, but it does not reach the center, as in water, for it is heavy relative to fire and air, but lighter than earth. Absolute lightness is a quality that necessitates the motion of the body to where its surface coincides with the concave surface of the sphere of the moon, as in fire; and relative lightness is a quality that necessitates the motion of the body in most of the distance extending between the center and the circumference, and a motion towards the circumference, but it does not reach the circumference, as in air.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines the two real types of i'timad—heaviness (thiqal) and lightness (khiffa)—each in absolute and relative forms. Absolute heaviness is defined as a quality that moves a body so that its center of gravity aligns with the center of the world; this is exemplified by earth. Relative heaviness moves a body towards the center for most of the distance but it does not actually reach the center; water is given as an example: it is heavy relative to fire and air, but not as heavy as earth. Absolute lightness is defined as a quality that moves a body so that its surface aligns with the concave surface of the lunar sphere; fire is the example. Relative lightness moves a body towards the circumference for most of the distance but does not reach it; air is the example. Thus, the author provides clear physical criteria for these four concepts, grounding them in observable behavior of elements.

Key Terms
ثقل مطلق (thiqal mutlaq)
ثقل مضاف (thiqal mudhaf)
مركز العالم (markaz al-'alam)
مركز ثقل (markaz thiqal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Heaviness is merely a relative concept with no absolute reference.
Correction: The passage distinguishes absolute heaviness, which targets the fixed center of the world, from relative heaviness, which only approaches it. Even relative heaviness has a defined direction (toward center), not arbitrary comparison.

Checkpoint

A) The center of the world
B) The surface of the lunar sphere
C) The center of the earth
D) The circumference

Key Takeaway
Absolute heaviness drives a body to the world's center; relative heaviness only moves toward it without reaching it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The passage defines heaviness as a quality that necessitates motion. What quality in your heart is currently 'necessitating' your daily motion? Is it the weight of this world or the weight of the next? How can you increase the 'absolute heaviness' of faith in your soul so that it drives you unswervingly to the Center?

Absolute and Relative Lightness in Kalam
Definition of absolute and relative lightness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam defines absolute and relative lightness and the cosmic reference points for each.

Core Idea
Lightness drives matter away from the center toward the lunar sphere.

Just as heaviness pulls bodies toward the world's center, lightness propels them outward. This lesson explains the two forms of lightness—absolute and relative—and the cosmic boundaries they target. Fire exemplifies absolute lightness, while air is relatively light. Understanding these terms is key to grasping the kalam account of elemental motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of absolute lightness
Notice that the body's surface must coincide with the concave surface of the lunar sphere. Fire is the example.
The lunar sphere marks the outermost boundary for elemental motion.
2
Read the definition of relative lightness
The body moves toward the circumference for most of the distance but does not reach it. Air is the example.
3
Compare absolute and relative lightness
Identify the difference: absolute lightness reaches the lunar sphere, relative lightness only approaches it. Consider why air does not go all the way.
This mirrors the distinction in heaviness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالثقل المطلق كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم إلى حيث ينطبق مركز ثقله، أعني النقطة التي تتعادل ما على جوانبها على مركز العالم كما في الأرض، والمضاف كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم في أكثر المسافة الممتدة بين المركز والمحيط حركة إلى المركز، لكنه لا يبلغ المركز كما للماء، فإنه ثقيل بالإضافة إلى النار، والهواء دون الأرض، والخفة المطلقة كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم إلى حيث ينطبق سطحه على سطح مقعر فلك القمر كما للنار والمضاف كيفية تقتضي حركة الجسم في أكثر المسافة الممتدة بين المركز والمحيط وحركة إلى المحيط، لكنه لا يبلغ المحيط كما للهواء.
Absolute heaviness is a quality that necessitates the motion of the body to where its center of gravity, i.e., the point at which what is on its sides is balanced, coincides with the center of the world, as in earth; and relative heaviness is a quality that necessitates the motion of the body in most of the distance extending between the center and the circumference, a motion towards the center, but it does not reach the center, as in water, for it is heavy relative to fire and air, but lighter than earth. Absolute lightness is a quality that necessitates the motion of the body to where its surface coincides with the concave surface of the sphere of the moon, as in fire; and relative lightness is a quality that necessitates the motion of the body in most of the distance extending between the center and the circumference, and a motion towards the circumference, but it does not reach the circumference, as in air.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines the two real types of i'timad—heaviness (thiqal) and lightness (khiffa)—each in absolute and relative forms. Absolute heaviness is defined as a quality that moves a body so that its center of gravity aligns with the center of the world; this is exemplified by earth. Relative heaviness moves a body towards the center for most of the distance but it does not actually reach the center; water is given as an example: it is heavy relative to fire and air, but not as heavy as earth. Absolute lightness is defined as a quality that moves a body so that its surface aligns with the concave surface of the lunar sphere; fire is the example. Relative lightness moves a body towards the circumference for most of the distance but does not reach it; air is the example. Thus, the author provides clear physical criteria for these four concepts, grounding them in observable behavior of elements.

Key Terms
خفة مطلقة (khiffa mutlaqa)
خفة مضاف (khiffa mudhaf)
فلك القمر (falak al-qamar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lightness is merely the absence of heaviness with no fixed endpoint.
Correction: The passage defines absolute lightness with a specific cosmic destination (the lunar sphere), and relative lightness with a direction toward the circumference, not random motion.

Checkpoint
What surface does absolute lightness align the body with?
A) The center of the world
B) The concave surface of the lunar sphere
C) The convex surface of the lunar sphere
D) The circumference

Key Takeaway
Absolute lightness drives a body to the lunar sphere; relative lightness only moves toward the circumference without reaching it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you strive for something, do you settle for 'most of the distance' like relative lightness, or do you push to the absolute endpoint? What holds you back from the absolute?
?
Fire reaches its ultimate place without hesitation. What is the 'fire' in your soul that you know should be burning all the way to its goal, but you let it cool into mere 'air'?
?
If absolute lightness is defined by its destination (the lunar sphere), what is the ultimate destination of your own actions? Are you content with partial motion, or will you realign your efforts to reach the true end?

Rejection of False Theories on Heaviness and Lightness
Rejects two theories of heaviness/lightness via contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the kalam author rejects the reduction of heaviness and lightness to humidity/dryness or the number of atomic parts, and how a single counterexample disproves both theories.

Core Idea
Heaviness cannot be explained by humidity or atomic count — a single waterskin proves it.

The author directly confronts two common theories about the physical causes of heaviness and lightness. Using a simple experiment with a waterskin, water, and mercury, he exposes a logical flaw in both theories. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the explicit rejection of two views and the counterexample given.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two rejected theories
The text states that heaviness and lightness are not reducible to (a) humidity and dryness, or (b) the abundance or scarcity of parts. Pinpoint these in the Arabic.
These theories were common in earlier natural philosophy.
3
Analyze the waterskin counterexample
The author argues: a single waterskin holds more weight of mercury than water, but water is more humid. If heaviness came from more parts, mercury would have more parts, implying empty spaces in water. Understand the logical contradiction.
The argument assumes equal volume and equal number of parts (atoms) if atoms exist.
4
Consider the conditional implication
The phrase 'otherwise there would be empty spaces' is a conditional consequence, not an assertion that empty space exists. Reflect on the difference.
This is a common point of confusion.
5
Connect to broader kalam debates
This argument relates to discussions on atoms, void, and essential properties. Consider why the author is making this point in a creedal work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال: وليستا راجعتين إلى الرطوبة واليبوسة، أو إلى كثرة أجزاء الجسم وقلتها على ما قيل لأن الزق يسع من الزئبق أضعاف ما يسع من الماء، مع زيادته في الرطوبة، وتساويهما في الأجزاء، وإلا لكان في الماء فرج خلاء، نسبتها إلى الأجزاء نسبة وزن الزئبق إلى وزن الماء) .
He said: And they (heaviness and lightness) are not reducible to humidity and dryness, nor to the abundance or scarcity of the body's parts as has been claimed, because a single waterskin (ziq) holds several times as much mercury (zi'baq) as it does water, despite the water's greater humidity, and the two being equal in parts; otherwise there would be empty spaces in the water, proportional to the weight of mercury to water.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This is a quotation from the original text (matn) that rejects two views: that heaviness/lightness are caused by humidity/dryness, or by the number of parts (as per atomic theory). The author gives a counterexample: a single container holds more weight of mercury than water, yet water is more humid. If heaviness were due to more parts, then mercury would have more parts, implying interpenetration or empty spaces in the water. The argument assumes equal volume and equal number of atoms (if atoms exist) but different weights, disproving the causal relation.

Key Terms
الرطوبة
اليبوسة
الزق
الزئبق
الجزء
فرج الخلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves the existence of empty space (void).
Correction: The argument only shows that if the two theories were true, then empty spaces would have to exist. It does not affirm the existence of void; it is a conditional proof.

Checkpoint
What does the example of the waterskin refute?
A) That all bodies have atoms
B) That heaviness is caused by humidity/dryness or number of parts
C) That fire is light
D) That water is heavy

Key Takeaway
Heaviness and lightness are irreducible essential qualities, not products of humidity/dryness or atomic count — a single counterexample suffices to reject both theories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The kalam argument uses a single concrete counterexample to dismantle entire theories. Think of a belief or habit you hold that feels well-established but has never been tested by a real counterexample from your life. Are you willing to honestly examine that belief by bringing in one concrete test from your experience this week? What might that test reveal about your own intellectual humility?
?
The authors were willing to accept that heaviness and lightness are irreducible qualities—they stopped trying to explain them away. Is there an attribute of Allah or a truth of the unseen that you have been trying to 'fit into' a human framework, making it comfortable for your mind? Will you ask Allah tonight to help you submit to His truths even when they resist your explanations?
