The Impossibility of Two Essential Movements
Sanusi states a principle and immediately rebuts it with a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that two essential movements cannot coincide is not a complete cause, as shown by the rope example and the analogy of a stationary object.

Core Idea
A universal claim can be disproven by a single counterexample, even in kalam.

Sanusi begins by stating a common kalam principle: two movements cannot essentially coincide in two directions. But he immediately presents a rebuttal that challenges this principle using a physical example—a rope pulled from both sides. This lesson unpacks that debate and shows how the principle is not a complete cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle and its rebuttal
First, read the claim that two movements cannot come together essentially. Then notice how the author counters it: it is not a complete cause, as seen in the rope example.
Pay attention to the logical structure: claim, counterexample, and further response.
2
Analyze the rope example
Consider how the rope is pulled from both sides but does not move—it remains stationary. This demonstrates that two opposing movements can coexist without causing motion.
The rope is not moving, so the two movements are not actualized in motion but are potential or tension.
3
Understand the final analogy
The passage ends with an analogy: the rope is like a stationary object that refrains from moving. This reinforces that the co-presence of opposing tendencies does not necessarily result in motion.
The key insight is that the principle of impossibility is not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اجتماع الحركتين بالذات إلى جهتين، وهو محال . ورد بأنه ليس من تمام العلة، كيف وقد اجتمعا في الحبل المتجاذب إلى الجانبين، وقد يقال لا مدافعة، وإنما هو كالساكن الذي يمتنع عن التحرك. قال : ومنع القاضي . اختلف أصحابنا القائلون بالاعتماد. فذهب القاضي إلى أن الاعتماد في كل جسم أمر واحد ربما يتعدد أسماؤه بحسب الاعتبار، حتى يسمى بالنسبة إلى العلو خفة، وإلى السفل ثقلاً. وليس له بالنسبة إلى الجهات الأخر اسم خاص، فما نقل عنه من جواز اجتماع الاعتمادات فمعناه جواز أن يفرض لذلك الأمر الاعتبارات المختلفة والإضافة إلى الجهات الست.
The coming together of two movements essentially to two directions is impossible. It was rebutted by [the claim] that it is not part of the complete cause; how has it [i.e., two movements] occurred together in the rope pulled from both sides? And it is said: there is no repulsion; rather it is like the stationary [object] that refrains from moving. He said: And the Qadi objected. Our companions who affirm i'timad differed. The Qadi held that i'timad in every body is one thing, perhaps its names multiply according to consideration, such that it is called lightness relative to upward and heaviness relative to downward. It has no specific name relative to the other directions. Thus what is transmitted from him about the permissibility of the coming together of i'timads means the permissibility of supposing for that thing different considerations and attribution to the six directions.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage begins by stating a physical principle: two movements in opposite directions cannot coincide in one body essentially. Then it presents a rebuttal: this principle is not a complete cause of impossibility because we observe such coexistence in a rope pulled from both sides—it does not move. Some even say it is not a repulsion but like an object that refuses to be moved. The author then introduces the Qadi's position: among those who affirm i'timad, the Qadi holds that i'timad in each body is a single reality; its different names (lightness, heaviness) arise only from different relations to directions. Thus when he allows the coexistence of i'timads, he means the same attribute can be related to multiple directions conceptually.

Key Terms
اجتماع الحركتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle that two movements cannot coincide essentially is universally true without exception.
Correction: The passage shows that this principle is not a complete cause, as evidenced by the rope example where two movements coexist in a stationary state.

Checkpoint
What example is used to rebut the claim that two movements cannot coincide?
A) A stone falling down
B) A rope pulled from both sides
C) A bird flying
D) A tree swaying

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two essential movements is not a complete cause; examples like a pulled rope show that opposing forces can coexist without resulting in motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been certain a principle was absolute, only to later find an exception in your own life? How does the rope example challenge you to hold your convictions with more humility?
?
What 'impossible' situation are you facing right now that might actually be an opportunity for equilibrium, like the stationary rope? What would it look like to stop assuming movement is necessary?
?
Which of your beliefs might be missing a 'complete cause'—something you haven't considered that could change everything? What would it take for you to test that belief today?

The Qadi's View on I'timad as a Unitary Attribute
Sanusi presents the Qadi's unique view on i'timad as a single attribute with relational names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadi's doctrine that i'timad in every body is a single attribute whose names (lightness, heaviness) arise from different directional considerations.

Core Idea
I'timad is one in essence but named differently by relation.

Having discussed movement, the author turns to the concept of i'timad (inclination/leaning) and introduces the Qadi's position. Among those who affirm i'timad, the Qadi holds a unique view: it is a single attribute in each body, but its names—such as lightness and heaviness—depend on the direction considered. This lesson unpacks that nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qadi's position on i'timad
Read the passage carefully to identify the Qadi's core claim: i'timad is one thing per body, and its names multiply by consideration.
Notice the contrast with other scholars who might treat i'timad as multiple.
2
Understand the naming by direction
The Qadi gives examples: the same attribute is called 'lightness' when related to upward and 'heaviness' when related to downward. Other directions have no special name.
This shows that the attribute itself does not change, only its relational description.
3
Interpret the allowance of 'coexistence of i'timads'
When the Qadi allows the coexistence of i'timads, he means that the one attribute can be attributed to all six directions conceptually. It is not a multiplicity in essence.
This resolves potential confusion: the Qadi does not posit multiple i'timads in a body.

Source Text

?

Disagreement on I'timads: Contradiction and Multiplicity
Presents three views on i'timads and the correct distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on whether multiple i'timads can coexist in one body and the correct distinction between natural and unnatural i'timads.

Core Idea
Only natural i'timads (heaviness/lightness) are truly contradictory under one aspect.

The nature of i'timad (inclination) in bodies was debated among kalam scholars. Some argued that multiple i'timads toward different directions cannot coexist, while others gave examples from physical experience to show they can. This passage presents those views and settles the matter with a precise distinction between natural and unnatural i'timads.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the three positions
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Identify the three distinct views presented: contradictory, non-contradictory, and the correct view with examples.
Pay attention to the examples used for the second position.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on the terms 'i'timad' (inclination), 'mutadadd' (contradictory), 'ghayr mutadadd' (non-contradictory), and 'tabi'i' (natural) vs 'ghayr tabi'i' (unnatural).
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Analyze the example of the heavy stone
Work through the example: a stone pulled upward has a natural downward i'timad, but when pulled downward, it has an unnatural upward i'timad. See how this supports the non-contradictory view?
4
Grasp the correct view
The author's truth: only natural i'timads (heaviness and lightness) are contradictory under the same aspect; natural and unnatural i'timads can coexist. Understand why this resolves the debate.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب بعضهم إلى أنها متعددة متضادة لا يقوم بجسم اعتقادان بالنسبة إلى جهتين، وبعضهم إلى أنها متعددة غير متضادة، لأن من جذب حجراً ثقيلاً إلى جهة العلو فإنه يحس منه اعتماد إلى جهة السفل، ولو جذبه غيره إلى جهة السفل يحس منه اعتماد إلى جهة العلو، ولأن كلاً من المتجاذبين جبلا على التقاوم، والتساوي في القوة يحس من الحبل اعتماداً إلى خلاف جهته. والحق أن الاعتمادين الطبيعيين أعني الثقل والخفة متضادان، لا اجتماعهما في شيء واحد، باعتبار واحد وأنه لا تضاد بين الاعتماد الطبيعي وغير الطبيعي، كما في الحجر الذي يرفع إلى فوق، فإن الرفع يحس منه اعتماداً إلى السفل والمدافعة صاعدة.
And some of them went to [the view] that they [i'timads] are multiple and contradictory: two i'timads relative to two directions cannot subsist in one body. And some [went] to [the view] that they are multiple but not contradictory: because whoever pulls a heavy stone upward senses from it an i'timad downward, and if another pulls it downward he senses from it an i'timad upward; and because each of the two pullers is disposed to resistance, and equality in force [causes] one to sense from the rope an i'timad opposite to his direction. The truth is that the two natural i'timads—namely heaviness and lightness—are contradictory; they do not come together in one thing under one consideration, and that there is no contradiction between natural and unnatural i'timad, as in the stone lifted upward, for the lifting senses from it an i'timad downward while the repulsion is upward.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents the different positions among scholars on the nature of i'timad. Some say i'timads are multiple and mutually contradictory, so two i'timads toward two directions cannot coexist in one body. Others say they are multiple but not contradictory, giving the example of a heavy stone pulled upward: the puller senses an i'timad downward from the stone, and if pulled downward, an i'timad upward. Also, when two forces pull a rope equally, each senses an i'timad opposite to his pull. The author then states the correct view: the two natural i'timads (heaviness and lightness) are truly contradictory and cannot coexist in one thing under the same aspect. However, there is no contradiction between natural i'timad and unnatural i'timad, such as lifting a stone upward: the unnatural upward push coexists with the natural downward i'timad of heaviness.

Key Terms
متضاد
غير متضاد
الاعتماد الطبيعي
غير الطبيعي
ثقل
خفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any two i'timads in a body are mutually contradictory.
Correction: Only natural i'timads (heaviness and lightness) under the same consideration are contradictory; natural and unnatural i'timads can coexist, as shown in the stone lifted upward.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which i'timads are contradictory?
A) Natural and unnatural
B) Two natural i'timads (heaviness and lightness)
C) Two unnatural i'timads
D) All i'timads

Key Takeaway
The correct view distinguishes natural from unnatural i'timads: only natural ones (heaviness/lightness) are contradictory under one aspect, while natural and unnatural i'timads can coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience a natural inclination towards laziness and an unnatural effort to pray Fajr on time. Do you regard these as contradictory, leading you to despair of overcoming laziness? The lesson shows that natural and unnatural i'timads can coexist without contradiction. How does this change your strategy in your daily struggle against your lower self?
?
Consider a time you felt torn between a natural desire and a religious duty. Did you assume they cannot coexist, so you must eliminate the natural desire entirely? The author's distinction teaches that natural and unnatural can be present together. What would it look like for you to allow both to exist while prioritizing the unnatural for the sake of Allah? What concrete step will you take today to act on this insight?
?
The second view in the passage mistakenly treats all i'timads as comparable. In your own life, have you ever equated different types of inclinations—like a dislike for a certain worship with a general distaste for obedience—and concluded that they are all equally contradictory to faith? How can the correct distinction between natural and unnatural help you re-evaluate a specific spiritual challenge you currently face?

Objection and Answer on the Definition of I'timad
Taftazani records an objection by al-Amidi and gives a two-part answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's objection to the definition of i'timad as a complete cause and the answer distinguishing between proximate principle as complete cause versus a more general principle.

Core Idea
I'timad does not need to be the full cause — absence of a preventer is enough.

In this passage, Taftazani records a sharp objection by al-Amidi against a previous argument about i'timad. The objection claims that the analogy used is invalid because effects can differ without the agents being opposites. The answer clarifies the meaning of 'proximate principle' and offers a counterexample from a rope under tension. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection by al-Amidi
Carefully read the objection which claims that the earlier argument is an analogy without a common factor. Notice how al-Amidi uses the example of nature causing both movement and rest with different conditions.
2
Understand the first part of the answer
The answer distinguishes two senses of 'proximate principle'. If it means the complete cause, then i'timad is not a complete cause; it requires the absence of a preventer. If it means a broader principle, the existence of the effect is not guaranteed.
This is the key logical move.
3
Analyze the second part: the rope counterexample
The answer then provides a counterexample: a rope pulled by two equal forces towards opposite directions. Each puller feels a repulsion towards the opposite direction, showing that two i'timads can coexist as opposites.
Note the force of the analogy.
4
Reflect on the final objection
The passage ends with a further objection that the rope might be like a stationary object resisting movement, with no actual repulsion. Consider how this might challenge the earlier reasoning.
This final point is left unresolved in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتمادان الموجبان لهما، وحينئذ لا يرد ما قال الأمدي : إن هذا تمثيل بلا جامع، كيف والحركة أثر الاعتماد وتضاد الآثار لا يوجب اختلاف المؤثر فضلاً عن تضاده كالطبيعة توجب الحركة، وبشرط الخروج عن الحيز الطبيعي، والسكون بشرط الحصول فيه على أن الفرق قائم. فإن اجتماع الحركتين إلى جهتين إنما امتنع لاستلزامه حصول الجسم في حالة واحدة في حيزين، ولا كذلك الاعتمادان . الجواب : أنه إن أريد بالمبدأ القريب تمام العلة، فلا نسلم أن الاعتماد كذلك، بل لا بد من انتفاء المانع . وإن أريد الأعم فلا نسلم أنه يوجب وجود الأثر على أنه لو تم هذا الدليل تضاد الطبيعي وغير الطبيعي لجريانه فيه سلمنا لكنه معارض بأنهما لو كانا متضادين لما جاز ،اجتماعهما واللازم باطل لأن الحبل المتجاذب بقوتين متساويتين إلى جهتين متقابلتين يجد فيه كل من الجاذبين مدافعة إلى خلاف جهته. وقد يقال : لا ، بل هو كالساكن الذي يمتنع عن التحريك لا مدافعة فيه أصلا.
The two i'timads (اعتمادان) that necessitate them; and then what al-Amidi said does not apply: 'This is an analogy without a common factor. How so? Movement is an effect of i'timad, and the opposition of effects does not necessitate difference in the agent, let alone opposition, just as nature necessitates movement with the condition of exiting the natural place, and rest with the condition of being in it, such that the difference stands. For the coexistence of two movements towards two directions is only impossible because it necessitates the body occupying two places at one state, but not so for the two i'timads.' The answer: if by 'proximate principle' is meant the complete cause, then we do not grant that i'timad is like that; rather the absence of a preventer is necessary. And if by it is meant the more general, then we do not grant that it necessitates the existence of the effect. Moreover, if this proof were sound regarding the opposition of the natural and non-natural, it would apply to it; but we grant it, yet it is countered by [the fact that] if they were opposed, their combination would not be permissible, and the consequent is false, because a rope pulled by two equal forces towards opposite directions has each puller finding a repulsion towards the opposite of his direction. And it may be said: no, rather it is like the stationary [object] that resists being moved; there is no repulsion at all.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage records an objection-and-answer pair. The objection, attributed to al-Amidi, attacks an earlier argument (likely about two i'timads being opposites) by claiming it is an analogy without a common factor: movement (an effect of i'timad) can be opposed, but that does not imply that i'timads themselves are opposed, since opposed movements cannot coexist in one body because that would place the body in two places, but two i'timads can coexist without such absurdity. The answer distinguishes two senses of 'proximate principle' (المبدأ القريب). If it means the complete cause (تمام العلة), an i'timad is not a complete cause; it requires the absence of a preventer. If it means a broader principle, it does not guarantee the effect. The answer then presents a counterexample: a rope pulled by equal forces shows that two opposing i'timads can coexist, producing a state of mutual repulsion rather than annihilation. A further objection—that the rope is like a stationary object resisting movement—is noted but not resolved, ending the passage.

المبدأ القريب
تمام العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: I'timad must be a complete cause to produce its effect.
Correction: The passage clarifies that i'timad is not a complete cause; it requires the absence of a preventer (المانع) to produce its effect.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given, what is required for i'timad to produce its effect if by 'proximate principle' is meant the complete cause?
A) The presence of a facilitator
B) The absence of a preventer
C) The agreement of the two i'timads
D) A natural disposition

Key Takeaway
The definition of i'timad does not require it to be a complete cause; absence of a preventer suffices for its effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that the same principle can produce opposite effects under different conditions. Will you commit to pausing before assuming opposition in others' motives?
?
The answer distinguishes between a complete cause and a partial cause where only the absence of a preventer is needed. Where in your spiritual life are you waiting for perfect conditions to act, when actually the only thing missing is the removal of an internal preventer like laziness, pride, or fear? What is one preventer you can remove today?

The counterexample of the rope pulled by equal forces shows that opposition can coexist without absurdity. Think of a relationship where you and another person seem to be pulling in opposite directions. Could it be that both are actually applying force toward good, but from different perspectives? Classification of I'timad and the Debate on Air's Nature
Commentary on classification and first dispute: air's i'timad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite classification of i'timad into necessary (lazim) and non-necessary (mukhtalif) and the specific debate between al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim regarding whether air has an inherent ascending i'timad.

Core Idea
Does air rise by nature or by force? The Mu'tazila debated its intrinsic tendency.

This passage from the commentary on al-Maqasid introduces the Mu'tazilite classification of physical tendencies (i'timad) into natural (lazim) and non-natural (mukhtalif). It then presents the first substantive dispute: whether air possesses an inherent upward i'timad, as al-Jubba'i argued, or not, as Abu Hashim contended. Understanding this debate clarifies how early kalam thinkers approached causality and natural motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compression of the commentary style.
2
Identify the classification and the two positions
Find the classification of i'timad into lazim and mukhtalif. Then locate the arguments of al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim regarding air's i'timad.
Al-Jubba'i uses the wineskin experiment; Abu Hashim uses the wood example.
3
Engage with the objections and replies
Trace the objection-response pairs. See how each scholar anticipates and counters the other's point. Note the final preferred view.
The conclusion: air's entrapment among wood parts prevents separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والمعتزلة يسمون الطبيعي من الاعتماد لازماً، كاعتماد الثقيل إلى السفل، والخفيف إلى العلو، وغير الطبيعي مختلفاً كاعتماد الثقيل إلى العلو، والخفيف إلى السفل ،قس أو كالاعتماد إلى باقي الجهات ولهم اختلافات في باب الاعتماد منها : ما قال الجبائى : إن في الهواء اعتماداً صاعداً لازماً لما يشاهد في الزق المفتوح المقسور تحت الماء، أنه إذا شق خرج الهواء صاعداً ، وشق الماء بل لو زال القاصر الهواء بالزق، وما اتعلق به من الثقل . لا يقال : يجوز أن لا يكون ذلك لصعوده اللازم، بل لضغط الماء إياه، وإخراجه من حيزه بثقل وطأته، لأنا نقول : لولم يكن في طبعه الصعود والطفو على الماء لما زاد ثقل وطأة الماء إلا استقراراً وثباتاً كسائر الأثقال، سيما إذا بقي الزق مشدوداً . قال أبو هاشم : ليس للهواء اعتماد لازم ، ولو كان في طبعه صعوداً لانفصل عن أجزاء الخشبة التي في الماء، وصعد دون الخشبة ، إذ لا سبب عند الجبائي لصعودها وطفوها سوى تخلخل أجزائها وتشبت الهواء بها، لا يقال: يجوز أن يفيده التركيب حالة موجبة للتلازم وعدم الانفصال، سيما وهو هواء لم يبق على كيفيته المقتضية للانفصال لانكسار سورتها بالامتزاج، لأنـا نقول : الكلام في الأجزاء الهوائية المجاورة للأجزاء الخشبية لا التي صارت جزء الممتزج كما في سائر المركبات على ما يراه الفلاسفة، فالأقرب أن يقال : إن احتباسها فيما بين الأجزاء الخشبية منعها الانفصال، وأوجب الاستتباع
He said: The Mu'tazila call the natural [type] of i'timad 'lazim', like the i'timad of a heavy thing downward and a light thing upward; and the non-natural they call 'mukhtalif', like the i'timad of a heavy thing upward and a light thing downward, or analogously like i'timad towards the remaining directions. And they have disagreements in the chapter of i'timad, among them: what al-Jubba'i said: that in air there is an ascending lazim i'timad, because of what is observed in an open wineskin forced under water: that when it is split, the air comes out ascending, and splits the water; indeed, if the restraint were removed, the air would [ascend] with the wineskin and the weight attached to it. It cannot be said: perhaps that is not due to its necessary ascent, but due to the water's pressure on it and its expulsion from its place by the weight of the water's pressure; for we say: if ascent and floating on water were not in its nature, the weight of the water's pressure would only cause it to settle and become stable like other heavy objects, especially if the wineskin remained tied. Abu Hashim said: Air does not have a lazim i'timad; if it were in its nature to ascend, it would separate from the parts of wood that are in water and ascend without the wood, since according to al-Jubba'i there is no cause for their ascent and floating other than the rarefaction of its parts and the adherence of air to them. It cannot be said: perhaps composition imparts a state necessitating adhesion and non-separation, especially since it is air that has not remained in its quality necessitating separation because its force is broken by mixing; for we say: the discussion concerns the airy parts adjacent to the wooden parts, not those that become part of the mixture, as in other composites according to the philosophers. So the closest [view] is to say: their being trapped among the wooden parts prevents their separation and necessitates their being carried along.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is the commentary (sharh) that opens by giving examples of the classification: natural i'timad (lazim) like heavy things falling and light things rising; non-natural i'timad (mukhtalif) like heavy things rising and light things falling. Then it proceeds to the first disagreement: whether air has a natural upward i'timad. Al-Jubba'i argues that it does, based on the observed behavior of air trapped in a wineskin under water: when the skin is split, the air bursts upward, splitting the water. He anticipates the objection that this could be due to water pressure rather than inherent lightness, and refutes it by claiming that if air had no natural upward tendency, water pressure would only compress it like a heavy object. Abu Hashim counters that air has no such inherent i'timad; if it did, it would separate from submerged wood and rise alone, since according to al-Jubba'i the wood floats only because of air trapped in its pores. Abu Hashim then addresses a potential objection that composition might bind the air to the wood, but distinguishes between air that has mixed (becoming part of the composite) and air that merely surrounds the wood; the latter, he argues, is simply trapped mechanically. The passage ends with a preferred view: the air's entrapment among the wood parts prevents separation.

Key Terms
لازم
مختلف
اعتماد صاعد
هواء
زق
تخلخل
امتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That air's upward motion is purely due to external pressure, without any intrinsic tendency.
Correction: The debate shows that the Mu'tazila considered both intrinsic nature (i'timad) and external forces. Al-Jubba'i argues for intrinsic ascent, while Abu Hashim denies it; neither attributes motion solely to pressure without considering inherent qualities.

Checkpoint
What evidence does al-Jubba'i use to argue that air has a natural ascending i'timad?

B) The behavior of an open wineskin forced under water
C) The floating of wood
D) The movement of clouds

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite debate on air's i'timad illustrates how early kalam used empirical observations and logical objections to analyze physical causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What evidence from your life points to one or the other, and how does that affect your sense of responsibility for change?
?
This debate hinges on whether what we observe (air rising) is due to an intrinsic quality or an external force. In your spiritual life, do you attribute your inclinations toward good or evil to your own created nature or to circumstances? How might this distinction affect how seriously you take your own actions?
?
Abu Hashim argued that if air had an intrinsic upward tendency, it would separate from wood in water. Are there any 'mixtures' in your life—relationships, habits, or environments—where you assume something is part of you when it might actually be merely trapped? What would it take to test that assumption?

The Persistence of I'timad: Al-Jubba'i vs. Abu Hashim
Second dispute: whether lazim i'timad endures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second disputed question regarding i'timad: whether the lazim (natural) type endures, and the arguments from both sides.

Core Idea
Persistence of i'timad hinges on whether its essential description is shared.

This lesson explores a key debate within the Mu'tazili school: does the natural type of i'timad (lazim) endure over time? Al-Jubba'i denies it entirely, while Abu Hashim affirms it based on observation. Their exchange reveals how kalam scholars use analogies and essential definitions to resolve such disputes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the flow of the debate.
Notice the contrasting positions presented impersonally.
2
Identify the Two Positions
Find the statements of al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim. Note what each claims about the endurance of lazim and mukhtalif i'timad.
Al-Jubba'i says both are non-enduring; Abu Hashim says lazim endures.
3
Analyze the Arguments and Rebuttals
Examine al-Jubba'i's two arguments and the rebuttals. Ask: Why does sharing 'descending i'timad' matter? Why is the analogy to sounds rejected?
This step develops your understanding of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما قال الجبائي : إن الاعتماد غير باق لازماً كان أو مختلفاً، وقال أبو هاشم بل اللازم باق بحكم المشاهدة، كما في الألوان والطعوم، تمسك الجبائي بأن الإنسان إذا تحامل على حجر هابط فاعتماد المختلف غير باق فكذا ،اللازم لاشتراكهما في أخص أوصاف النفس، أعني كونه اعتماداً هابطاً، وبأن ما لا يبقى من الأعراض كالأصوات وغيرها، لا فرق فيها بين المقدور وغير المقدور ، فكذا في الاعتمادات التي مختلفها مقدوراً، ولازمها غير مقدور. ورد الأول بمنع كون أخص الأوصاف الاعتماد الهابط، بل الاعتماد اللازم، والثاني بأنه تمثيل بلا ،جامع
And among them is what al-Jubba'i said: that i'timad is not enduring, whether lazim or mukhtalif. Abu Hashim said: rather the lazim is enduring by the judgment of observation, as in colors and tastes. Al-Jubba'i argued that when a man leans on a descending stone, the mukhtalif i'timad is not enduring; so too the lazim, because they share the most specific description of the essence, namely being a descending i'timad; and because what does not endure among accidents, like sounds and others, there is no distinction between the volitional and non-volitional; so too in the case of i'timads, whose mukhtalif is volitional and whose lazim is non-volitional. The first [argument] was rebutted by denying that the most specific description is the descending i'timad; rather it is the lazim i'timad. And the second [argument] was rebutted by [saying] it is an analogy without a common factor.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the second disagreement: whether the lazim (natural) i'timad endures (باق) over time. Al-Jubba'i maintains that no i'timad endures, whether lazim or mukhtalif; i'timad is a transient accident. Abu Hashim counters that the lazim type does endure, analogously to colors and tastes which persist by observation. Al-Jubba'i offers two proofs for his position: (1) The most specific description (أخص الأوصاف) common to both lazim and mukhtalif is 'descending i'timad' (اعتماد هابط); since the mukhtalif is observed not to endure (e.g., when one leans on a stone and then stops, the i'timad ceases), the lazim must also not endure, because they share that essential description. (2) Accidents that do not endure, like sounds, make no distinction between those caused by choice (مقدور) and those not; since lazim i'timad is non-volitional and mukhtalif is volitional, if the latter does not endure, the former should not either. The rebuttals (from Abu Hashim's side) are then given: to the first, it is denied that the most specific description is 'descending i'timad'; rather it is 'lazim i'timad' (so the argument fails). To the second, it is said to be an analogy without a common factor (i.e., the comparison to sounds is not valid). The passage thus records both positions and the initial exchange of arguments and counterarguments.

Key Terms
باق
لازم
مختلف
اعتماد
أخص الأوصاف
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'enduring' (باق) in this context means infinite duration or everlastingness.
Correction: In kalam, 'baqa'' refers to persistence over time, not necessarily infinite duration. The debate is about whether i'timad continues to exist after its initial moment, like colors, or vanishes immediately.

Checkpoint
On what basis does Abu Hashim claim that lazim i'timad endures?
A) Logical deduction
B) Observation, like colors and tastes
C) Scriptural evidence
D) Consensus of the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
The debate shows how kalam uses analogical reasoning and essential definitions to settle disagreements about the nature of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you lean on a stone, do you feel that pressure as something you control or as something that persists independently? How does your answer affect how you view your dependence on Allah's constant creation of accidents?
?
Al-Jubba'i argues that sharing an essential description means sharing the property of non-endurance. Where in your life have you assumed that sharing a trait with someone means you share the same fate or outcome? How does this overgeneralization strain your relationships or your understanding of God's justice?
?
The rebuttal rejects the analogy to sounds as lacking a common factor. Think of a time you drew a false analogy between two situations in your religious practice. What was the missing 'common factor' that led you to a mistaken conclusion?

The Two Extremes of Color and the Reality of the Type
Defines the two extremes of color and argues that the type is the specific instances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam classification of colors, including the opposition of black and white, the nature of intermediate colors, and the claim that the type (النوع) is realized in its particular instances.

Core Idea
The type is not a universal; it is found in its specific instances.

In this passage from Sharh al-Maqasid, the author addresses two foundational points about colors: the opposition of extremes and the nature of the type. Before we can discuss theological implications of sensible qualities, we need to grasp the kalam framework for classifying colors. This lesson unpacks what it means for black and white to be opposites, whether intermediate colors are also opposites, and how the type (النوع) exists in concrete instances like snow white and ivory white.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic and translation without analyzing. Notice the technical terms: طرف, تضاد, نوع, وسط. This will give you a sense of the author's argumentative flow.
The text breaks off at the end; ignore that for now.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes two distinct but connected claims: (1) about the extremes of color and intermediates, and (2) about the reality of the type. See how the author transitions from one to the other using 'قال'.
3
Understand the condition for opposition
Notice the phrase 'إن لم تشترط غاية الخلاف' and later 'إن اشترط'. The author conditions the opposition of intermediates on whether utmost difference is required. Reflect on why this matters.
This condition is central to the kalam theory of opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : للون طرفان هما البياض والسواد المتضادان وبينهما وسائط، وهي أنواع متباينة بل متضادة، إن لم تشترط غاية الخلاف. حقائق الألوان، بل جميع المحسوسات ظاهرة غنية عن البيان ولا خفاء في تضاد السواد والبياض لما بينهما من غاية الخلاف لكونهما طرفي الألوان، وأما ما بينهما من الحمرة والصفرة وغير ذلك فعند المحققين أنواع متباينة، يختص كل منها بآثار مختلفة وليست بمتضادة، إن اشترط بين المتضادين غاية الخلاف والاقتضادة. قال: والتحقيق أن النوع ليس هو البياض مثلاً، بل البياضات التي تحته مثل بياض الثلج وبياض العاج، ونحو ذلك، وكذا سائر الألوان، بل
The first topic: Color has two extremes, which are whiteness and blackness, the opposites, and between them are intermediates, and they are diverse types, rather opposites, if the utmost difference is not required. The realities of colors, indeed all sensible things, are apparent and need no explanation, and there is no concealment in the opposition of blackness and whiteness due to the utmost difference between them because they are the extremes of colors. As for what is between them, such as redness, yellowness, and others, according to the investigators they are diverse types, each characterized by different effects, and they are not opposites if between opposites utmost difference and opposition is required. He said: The investigation is that the type is not whiteness, for example, but rather the whitenesses that fall under it, like the whiteness of snow and the whiteness of ivory, and so on. Similarly for all other colors, and
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid addresses the nature of colors within a kalam framework. It begins by stating the first topic: color has two extremes, namely whiteness and blackness, which are opposites, and between them are intermediate colors. These intermediates are diverse types, and could even be considered opposites if one does not require strict maximal opposition. The author then asserts that the realities of colors, and indeed all sensible things, are self-evident and need no proof. The opposition between black and white is clear because they are the extremes of the color spectrum. As for the intermediate colors like red and yellow, according to the investigators (المحققون) they are distinct types, each with different effects, and they are not considered opposites if one insists on utmost difference and opposition as a condition for true antagonism. The second part introduces a further investigation: the type (النوع) is not the universal concept of whiteness itself, but rather the specific instances of whiteness that fall under it, such as the whiteness of snow and the whiteness of ivory. The same applies to all other colors. The text breaks off, but the argument is that the type exists as particularized instances, not as a Platonic universal.

D) The type is the intermediate colors.

Key Takeaway
The passage teaches that in kalam, the type (النوع) is realized in its specific instances, and the opposition of colors is conditioned by the extent of difference.

The Extremes of Color and the Nature of Intermediate Colors
Al-Taftazani defines the extremes of color and explains the classification of intermediate colors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why black and white are considered opposites in classical kalam, and how intermediate colors are classified as distinct types rather than mere gradations.

Core Idea
Color extremes are opposites; intermediate colors are distinct types, not degrees.

This passage from al-Taftazani examines the nature of colors through the lens of kalam. It establishes the two extremes of the color spectrum—white and black—as true opposites, and then clarifies the status of intermediate colors like red and yellow. Al-Taftazani aligns with the investigators who consider these intermediate colors as separate types, each with its own effects, rather than as simple shades between black and white.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own phrasing guide your understanding.
2
Identify the two extremes and opposition
Note that white and black are called the two extremes and are opposites due to extreme difference (ghayat al-khilaf). This is the key reason.
Opposition here means complete difference, not mere contrast.
3
Understand the condition for opposition
The author explains that if we require 'extreme difference' to define opposites, then intermediate colors are not opposites of each other or of the extremes.
This condition is crucial for the discussion.
4
Learn the investigators' position
The investigators (al-muhaqqiqun) hold that intermediate colors are distinct types (anwa' mutabayina), each with its own specific effects, not mere shades.
This refutes the common misunderstanding that they are degrees.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this classification impacts discussions on universals and the nature of sensible qualities in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : للون طرفان هما البياض والسواد المتضادان وبينهما وسائط، وهي أنواع متباينة بل متضادة، إن لم تشترط غاية الخلاف). حقائق الألوان، بل جميع المحسوسات ظاهرة غنية عن البيان ولا خفاء في تضاد السواد والبياض لما بينهما من غاية الخلاف لكونهما طرفي الألوان، وأما ما بينهما من الحمرة والصفرة وغير ذلك فعند المحققين أنواع متباينة، يختص كل منها بآثار مختلفة وليست بمتضادة، إن اشترط بين المتضادين غاية الخلاف والاقتضادة .
First topic: Color has two extremes: white and black, which are opposites, and between them are intermediaries, which are distinct types—rather, opposites—if the extreme of difference is not stipulated. The realities of colors, indeed all sensible things, are obvious and need no explanation, and there is no obscurity in the opposition of black and white due to the extreme difference between them, as they are the two extremes of colors. As for what is between them—like red, yellow, and others—according to the investigators, they are distinct types, each of which has different effects, and they are not opposites if one stipulates extreme difference and opposition between opposites.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the extremes of color and clarifies the status of intermediate colors. The author states that color has two extremes: white and black, which are opposites. Between them lie intermediaries (such as red, yellow, etc.). Some might think these intermediaries are also opposites, but the author clarifies that if we require extreme difference ('ghayat al-khilaf') to define opposition, then the intermediaries are not opposites; rather, they are distinct types. The author then explains that the realities of colors and all sensible qualities are self-evident. The opposition of black and white is clear because they are the extremes. As for intermediate colors, the investigators hold them to be distinct types, each with its own specific effects, and they are not opposites if we define opposition by extreme difference. This sets up the discussion on whether categories like color are universal or specific.

The True Type is the Specific Instance
Al-Taftazani defines the true type as specific instances and extends it to colors, tangibles, and visibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's argument that the true types of sensible qualities are specific, uniform instances rather than universal concepts.

Core Idea
Types are not general concepts but the specific, uniform individuals.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents his investigation (tahqiq) into the nature of types in sensory qualities. He argues that when we say 'whiteness' or 'heat,' the true type is not the abstract universal but the concrete, specific instances that share a uniform essence. This clarification is essential for understanding how gradation (tashkik) works in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins with a strong claim about whiteness.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that the type is not whiteness in general but specific whitenesses. Understand why this distinction matters.
The author uses examples like snow and ivory.
3
Extend to other categories
Notice that the same logic applies to all colors, tangible qualities (heat), and visible qualities (light). Each type is defined by its specific, uniform instances.
For heat, the type is not absolute heat but heat of pure fire.
4
Compare with the apparent view
Read the later part where the author contrasts the apparent view of scholars (types as general categories) with the reality (types as specific instances). Reflect on why the reality differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والتحقيق أن النوع ليس هو البياض مثلاً، بل البياضات التي تحته مثل بياض الثلج وبياض العاج، ونحو ذلك، وكذا سائر الألوان، بل جميع المقولات بالتشكيك حتى إن النوع من الملموسات هي الحرارة المخصوصة، ومن المبصرات الضوء المخصوص لا مطلق الحرارة والضوء نعم قد يكون لجملة جملة من الأنواع عارض خاص له اسم خاص كما في الألوان، وقد لا يكون كما في الأضواء، وأجيب بأنه وإن لم يدخل فيه فقد دخل ماهية المعروض للأشد، ومبنى ذلك على امتناع التفاوت في الماهية وذاتياتها ما به التفاوت إن لم يدخل فيها فذاك وإلا فلا اشتراك، ونوقض بالعارض، وأجيب بأنه وإن لم يدخل فيه فقد دخل في ماهية المعروض للأشد، وفيه نظراً وبالجملة فعدم دخول ما به التفاوت فيما فيه التفاوت، إما أن يمنع التفاوت فيتم النقض، أو لا فلا يتم الدليل، ومن ها هنا ذهب بعضهم إلى نفي التشكيك والاشتداد وبعضهم إلى إثباته في الماهية وذاتياتها، حتى جعل الماهية الخطبة في الخط الأطول أطول، وفي الأقصر أنقص . الظاهر من كلام القوم أن أنواع اللون هي السواد والبياض والحمرة والصفرة وغير ذلك، وأنواع الكيفيات الملموسة هي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة، ونحو ذلك، إلا أن التحقيق هو أن أنواع اللون هي البياضات المخصوصة التي لا تتفاوت أفرادها كبياض الثلج مثلا، وكذا في السواد وغيره، بل في كل ما يقال بالتشكيك حتى إن النوع من الملموسات ليس مطلق الحرارة بل الحرارة المخصوصة التي تكون في أفرادها على السوبة كحرارة النار الصرفة مثلاً، وانوع من المبصرات ليس مطلق الضوء، بل الضوء المخصوص .
He said: The reality is that the type is not whiteness per se, but rather the whitenesses that fall under it, such as the whiteness of snow and the whiteness of ivory, and so on. Likewise for all other colors. Rather, all categories are by gradation (tashkik), such that the type of tangible qualities is specific heat, and of visible qualities is specific light, not absolute heat and light. Yes, it may be that for a whole group of types there is a specific accident that has a specific name, as in colors, or it may not, as in lights. It was answered that even if it does not enter into it, it has entered into the essence of the substrate of the more intense. The basis of this is the impossibility of variation in the essence and its essentials. If what makes variation does not enter into them, then that is fine; otherwise, there is no commonality. It was objected by the accident, and answered that even if it does not enter into it, it has entered into the essence of the substrate of the more intense. There is consideration in this. In summary, the non-entry of what makes variation into that in which there is variation either prevents variation, then the objection stands, or it does not, then the proof is incomplete. Hence, some went to negate gradation and intensity, and some to affirm it in the essence and its essentials, such that they made the essence of a longer line longer and of a shorter line shorter. The apparent from the words of the scholars is that the types of color are black, white, red, yellow, etc., and the types of tangible qualities are heat, cold, moisture, dryness, etc. However, the reality is that the types of color are the specific whitenesses whose individuals do not differ, like the whiteness of snow, etc., and likewise for blackness and others, rather in everything predicated by gradation, such that the type of tangible things is not absolute heat but specific heat that is uniform in its individuals, like the heat of pure fire, etc., and the type of visible things is not absolute light but specific light.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the author's investigation (tahqiq) regarding the nature of types in colors and other sensible qualities. It begins by stating that the true type is not the general concept (e.g., whiteness as a genus) but rather the specific instances (e.g., the whiteness of snow, the whiteness of ivory). This applies to all categories that are predicated by gradation (tashkik), such as tangible qualities: the type is not heat in general but a specific heat (e.g., the heat of fire). Similarly, for visible qualities: the type is not light in general but a specific light. The author then discusses an objection and answer regarding whether the differentiating factor ('ma bihi al-tafawut') enters into the essence. The objection points to the case of an accident ('arid), and the answer is that even if it does not enter, it enters into the essence of the substrate of the more intense. The author notes that there is consideration (nazar) in this. He summarizes that the non-entry of the differentiating factor either prevents variation (making the objection valid) or does not prevent it (making the proof incomplete). This leads to a disagreement: some deny gradation entirely, while others affirm it in the essence itself (thus making the essence of a longer line 'longer' and of a shorter line 'shorter'). Finally, the author notes that the apparent view of the scholars is that types are general categories, but the reality is that they are specific instances uniform in their individuals. This is a sophisticated discussion on the ontology of natural kinds and the semantics of universal terms in the context of gradational predicates.

Key Terms
النوع
التشكيك
البياض
الحرارة
الضوء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that types like whiteness are universal concepts that apply identically to all instances.
Correction: Al-Taftazani insists that true types are the specific, uniform instances; the universal is merely a label for a group of related types.

Checkpoint
According to the tahqiq, what is the type of color?
A) Absolute whiteness.
B) Specific whitenesses like whiteness of snow.
C) Blackness only.
D) All colors collectively.

Key Takeaway
The true type is the specific, uniform instance, not the absolute universal concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of concepts like 'goodness' or 'truth', do you treat them as real things that exist on their own, or do you see them only in their specific instances? How might this lesson's teaching that the true type is the specific instance change how you relate to abstract ideas in your daily life? Commit to noticing one abstract concept today and asking yourself: 'Where do I see this in a specific, concrete form?'
?
Consider the people you categorize—'the religious', 'the ignorant', 'the successful'. Are you reducing them to a universal label instead of seeing each as a unique type? This lesson insists that the real type is the specific individual. Who is one person you have been treating as a general type? What would it mean to see them as their own 'specific instance'? Will you do that today?

The Objection on Differentiating Factor and Disagreement on Gradation
Al-Taftazani presents objection, answer, counter-objection, summary, and the resulting disagreement on gradation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if the differentiating factor (ma bihi al-tafawut) does not enter the essence, then gradation is impossible, and the scholarly disagreement that arises from this debate.

Core Idea
Does gradation require a differentiating factor within the essence? Scholars disagree.

Al-Taftazani presents a sophisticated objection to his own view: if the factor that makes one instance more intense (e.g., whiter) does not enter the essence of whiteness, then how can there be gradation at all? He then provides answers and counters, leading to a split: some deny gradation entirely, while others affirm it even within the essence itself. This dialectic is crucial for understanding the metaphysics of qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Identify the objection that if the differentiating factor does not enter the essence, then there is no commonality, and note the answer involving the substrate of the more intense.
2
Follow the counter-objection by accident
The objection is raised again with the case of an accident ('arid). Understand why this challenges the answer.
3
Grasp the summary of the argument
Read the summary: non-entry of the differentiating factor either prevents variation (making the objection valid) or does not (making the proof incomplete).
4
See the resulting disagreement
Note that this leads some to deny gradation and intensity, and others to affirm it within the essence, even to the point of making the essence itself varying.
The example of a line: the essence of a longer line is longer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, scholars disagreed fundamentally about whether gradation exists in the essence itself. Think of an aspect of your deen—iman, taqwa, love of Allah—where you intuitively assume it 'varies by degree.' But the passage asks: does the variation touch the essence? Examine your own heart: do you treat your faith as a fixed essence that can only increase accidentally, or as something that itself becomes 'longer' or 'shorter'? What would change if you took the latter view?
?
The objection from the accident forced the answer that the differentiating factor enters the substrate. Consider your own identities—Muslim, father, son, student. When you see variation in your practice or traits, do you locate the deficiency in the essence of that role or in your substrate (your soul, your body)? How does that attribution affect your repentance and growth? Honestly, which way of seeing things leads you to change—and which leads you to excuse yourself?
?
Al-Taftazani leaves the response with 'there is consideration' (فيه نظر)—it is not fully settled. Where in your life have you accepted a comfortable answer to a deep question about your deen without sitting with the 'nazar' (critical reflection)? This lesson shows that even great scholars left questions open. Will you allow yourself to sit in the discomfort of unresolved questions—about qadr, about the nature of the Qur'an, about your own soul—or do you rush to a conclusion to avoid the tension? What would it cost you to stay with the question a little longer?

The Impossibility of Gradation in Essence
Taftazani argues that gradation cannot be in the essence and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why gradation cannot occur in the essence itself and how Taftazani responds to objections using the factor of difference.

Core Idea
Gradation belongs to accidents, not to the essence itself.

This passage tackles a central issue in ontology: can the same essence be present in individuals with varying degrees? Taftazani argues that essence cannot admit gradation, and he carefully addresses an objection based on accidental properties. The discussion illustrates a key principle in Islamic logic and metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's own argument flow through before interpreting.
Note the technical terms: tashkik, mahiyya, dhatiyyat, `arad.
2
Identify the claim and examples
Taftazani states that all categories are said by gradation, giving examples of specific heat and light. Notice that he distinguishes between types with and without a common accident.
The examples are from tangible and visual categories.
3
Follow the objection and answer
The core argument is that difference cannot be in the essence. An objection is raised using accident (`arad). The answer involves the substrate (ma`rud) of the more intense. Understand the logical structure: if the factor of difference is outside the essence, then no difference in essence; if inside, no sharing.
The passage also notes a criticism (nazar) of this answer.
4
Understand the summary and positions
Taftazani concludes that the non-inclusion of the factor either prevents difference (objection stands) or not (proof fails). This leads to two scholarly positions: negating gradation in essence or affirming it (e.g., line example).
Reflect on how this argument applies to other cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميع المقولات بالتشكيك حتى إن النوع من الملموسات هي الحرارة المخصوصة، ومن المبصرات الضوء المخصوص لا مطلق الحرارة والضوء نعم قد يكون لجملة جملة من الأنواع عارض خاص له اسم خاص كما في الألوان، وقد لا يكون كما في الأضواء، وأجيب بأنه وإن لم يدخل فيه فقد دخل ماهية المعروض للأشد، ومبنى ذلك على امتناع التفاوت في الماهية وذاتياتها ما به التفاوت إن لم يدخل فيها فذاك وإلا فلا اشتراك، ونوقض بالعارض، وأجيب بأنه وإن لم يدخل فيه فقد دخل في ماهية المعروض للأشد، وفيه نظراً وبالجملة فعدم دخول ما به التفاوت فيما فيه التفاوت، إما أن يمنع التفاوت فيتم النقض، أو لا فلا يتم الدليل، ومن ها هنا ذهب بعضهم إلى نفي التشكيك والاشتداد وبعضهم إلى إثباته في الماهية وذاتياتها، حتى جعل الماهية الخطبة في الخط الأطول أطول، وفي الأقصر أنقص.
All categories are [said] by gradation (tashkik), such that the type of tangible things is the specific heat, and of visual things is the specific light, not absolute heat and light. Yes, it may be that groups of types have a specific accident and a specific name, as in colors, and may not, as in lights. And it was answered that even if it is not included in it, it has entered the essence of the substrate for the more intense. And the basis of that is the impossibility of difference in the essence (mahiyya) and its essentials (dhatiyyat): the thing by which difference occurs, if it is not included in it, then that [is the case]; otherwise, there is no sharing. And it was objected by the accident (`arad). And it was answered that even if it is not included in it, it has entered the essence of the substrate for the more intense; but there is consideration in it. And in summary: the non-inclusion of the factor of difference in the thing having difference either prevents difference, thus the objection is complete; or not, thus the proof is not established. And from here some went to negate gradation (tashkik) and intensification (ishtidad), and some to affirm it in the essence and its essentials, such that they made the essence of a line longer in the longer line and shorter in the shorter line.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument about the impossibility of gradation in the essence. It begins by stating that all categories are said by gradation (tashkik), meaning they apply to individuals with varying intensity, and gives examples: the type of tangible things is specific heat, not absolute heat; the type of visual things is specific light, not absolute light. Then it notes that groups of types may share a common accident and name, like colors, or not, like lights. The text then presents an objection and answer regarding the factor of difference (ma bihi al-tafawut). The basis is that difference cannot occur in the essence and its essentials: if the factor of difference is outside the essence, then there is no difference in the essence itself; if it is inside, then the weaker individual cannot share the essence. An objection is raised using the accident (`arad): perhaps the factor is an accident. The answer is that even if it is not included in the essence, it enters the essence of the substrate (ma`rud) of the more intense. However, a criticism (nazar) is noted. The summary says that the non-inclusion of the factor either prevents difference (so the objection stands) or does not (so the proof fails). Consequently, some scholars negate gradation in the essence, while others affirm it, using the example of a line: the essence of a line is longer in a longer line and shorter in a shorter line.

Key Terms
تشكيك (tashkik)
ماهية (mahiyya)
ذاتيات (dhatiyyat)
عارض (`arad)
اشتداد (ishtidad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that gradation can occur in the essence itself, such that the same essence varies in intensity.
Correction: The passage argues that difference cannot be in the essence or its essentials; if it were, there would be no shared essence. Any gradation is accidental and belongs to the substrate, not the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if the factor of difference is not included in the essence, what follows?
A) Difference is realized in the essence
B) There is no difference in the essence
C) The objection is automatically invalid
D) The weaker individual shares the essence

Key Takeaway
Gradation cannot be in the essence itself; it is only in accidents, and the factor of difference must lie outside the essence or in the substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that gradation cannot be in the essence itself—difference is only in accidents. When you evaluate your own iman, do you treat it as a graded essence or as a fixed essence with graded manifestations? If your iman's essence is constant, how does that change your anxiety about your faith's fluctuations? This week, when you feel your iman is 'low', will you focus on reviving its accidental expressions or question its essence?
?
Consider a time you judged another person as 'less' or 'more' intelligent or virtuous. According to this argument, does that difference lie in their essence or in accidents? How does your answer affect how you treat them? Before tomorrow, catch yourself in a judgment and consciously attribute the difference to accidents rather than essence.
?
The line example shows that some affirm gradation even in essence—making the longer line essentially different from the shorter. When you see diversity among people (race, gender, ability), do you see different essences or the same essence with different accidents? What is your soul's immediate reaction? Challenge yourself today to see every human as sharing one essence with accidental variations, and let that shape your interactions.

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
Apparent vs. Verified View on Species of Qualities
Taftazani contrasts apparent and verified views on species of color and tangible qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the apparent and verified views on what constitutes a true species (naw`) of qualities like colors and tangible properties.

Core Idea
True species are not broad categories but specific, non-differing individuals.

Taftazani begins by presenting the common view that species of color are general categories like black, white, red. But then he reveals the verified view: the true species are the specific individuals that do not differ among themselves, like the whiteness of snow. This lesson clarifies that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall comparison between apparent and verified views.
2
Identify the two views
Pinpoint the apparent view: that species are general categories. Then find the verified view: species are specific, equal individuals.
The verified view applies to everything said by gradation (tashkik).
3
Reflect on the examples
Notice the examples given: whites like snow, heat like pure fire, light like sunlight. Why are these considered the true species?
Each example is a specific instance where individuals do not differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظاهر من كلام القوم أن أنواع اللون هي السواد والبياض والحمرة والصفرة وغير ذلك، وأنواع الكيفيات الملموسة هي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة، ونحو ذلك، إلا أن التحقيق هو أن أنواع اللون هي البياضات المخصوصة التي لا تتفاوت أفرادها كبياض الثلج مثلا، وكذا في السواد وغيره، بل في كل ما يقال بالتشكيك حتى إن النوع من الملموسات ليس مطلق الحرارة بل الحرارة المخصوصة التي تكون في أفرادها على السوية كحرارة النار الصرفة مثلاً، والنوع من المبصرات ليس مطلق الضوء، بل الضوء الخاص الذي لا يتفاوت فيه أفراده كضوء الشمس مثلاً، وإنما يقع الاشتباه من جهة أن الأنواع قد يكون لجملة جملة منها عارض خاص، واسم خاص كالبياضات المشتركة في تفريق البصر وفي اسم البياض والسوادات المشتركة في قبض البصر، وفي اسم السواد وكالحرارات والبرودات، ونحو ذلك فيتوهم أن تلك الجملة نوع واحد بخلاف الأضواء، فإنه لا ينفرد جملة جملة منها بعارض واسم فلا يتوهم ذلك فيه، بل ربما يتوهم كون المجموع نوعاً واحداً فاللون والضوء قد وقعا في مرتبة واحدة من المبصرات، إلا أن اللون جنس الألوان بخلاف الضوء لما فيه من التفاوت، والضوء توهم نوعية لتقارب أنواعه بخلاف اللون، وإنما توهم ذلك في جملة من أنواعه كالبياض مثلاً لتقارب أنواع البياض، وكالسواد لتقارب أنواع السواد وعلى هذا القياس فصار الضوء بمنزلة البياض مثلاً في أنه ليس نوعاً لما تحته ولا جنساً بل عارضاً، ومبنى ذلك على ما تقرر عندهم من أن القول بالتشكيك لا يكون إلا عارضاً لامتناع التفاوت في الماهية وذاتياتها، لأن الأمر الذي به يتحقق التفاوت حيث يوجد في الأشد دون الأضعف إن لم يكن داخلا في الماهية لم يتحقق التفاوت فيها بل كانت في الكل على السواء، وإن كان داخلا فيها لم يتحقق اشتراك الأضعف فيها لانتفاء بعض الأجزاء مثلاً الخصوصية التي توجد في نور الشمس دون القمر، إن كانت من ذاتيات الضوء لم يكن ما في القمر ضوء، وإلا لم يكن تفاوت النورين في نفس الماهية.
The apparent from the speech of the scholars is that the types of color are black, white, red, yellow, and others, and the types of tangible qualities are heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and so forth. However, the verified view is that the types of color are the specific whites whose individuals do not differ, like the whiteness of snow for example, and similarly in blackness and others, rather in everything that is said by gradation (tashkik) such that the type of tangible qualities is not absolute heat but the specific heat whose individuals are equal, like the heat of pure fire for example, and the type of visual things is not absolute light but the specific light whose individuals do not differ, like the light of the sun for example. And confusion only arises from the fact that some groups of types may have a specific accident and a specific name, like the whites sharing in dispersing the sight and in the name 'whiteness', and the blacks sharing in contracting the sight and in the name 'blackness', and likewise heats and colds, and so on, so one imagines that that group is one type, unlike lights, for no group of them has a distinct accident and name, so that is not imagined of them, rather one might imagine that the whole collection is one type. So color and light are in the same rank among visual things, except that color is a genus of colors unlike light because of the difference in it, and light [people] imagined to be a type due to the closeness of its types unlike color, and that was only imagined of a group of its types like whiteness for example due to the closeness of types of whiteness, and like blackness due to the closeness of types of blackness, and analogously, light became like whiteness for example in that it is neither a type for what is under it nor a genus but rather an accident. And the basis of this is what is established with them: that the claim of gradation (tashkik) is only accidental because of the impossibility of difference in the essence (mahiyya) and its essentials (dhatiyyat). For the thing by which difference is realized, when it exists in the more intense (ashadd) without the weaker (ad`af), if it is not included in the essence, difference is not realized in it, rather it is the same in all; and if it is included in it, the weaker does not share in it due to the absence of some parts. For example, the specificity that exists in the light of the sun but not the moon: if it were among the essentials of light, what is in the moon would not be light; otherwise, the difference between the two lights would not be in the essence itself.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a clarification and argument about the true nature of species (anwa') of qualities like colors and tactile properties. It begins by stating the apparent view of scholars: that the types of color are general categories like black, white, red, yellow, etc., and the types of tangible qualities are heat, cold, moisture, dryness. Then it presents the verified view (tahqiq): the true types are the specific individuals that do not differ among themselves, for example, specific whites like the whiteness of snow, and similarly for blackness and others. This applies to everything said by gradation (tashkik). So the type of tangible qualities is not absolute heat but specific heat whose individuals are equal, like the heat of pure fire; the type of visual things is not absolute light but specific light whose individuals are equal, like sunlight. The passage then explains the cause of confusion (ishbah): some groups of species share a common accident and name, e.g., whites share the accident of dispersing vision and the name 'whiteness', blacks share contracting vision and the name 'blackness', heats share the accident of heat, etc. This leads one to mistakenly think that group is one single species. In contrast, lights do not have such a common accident and name per group, so that confusion does not arise; instead, one might imagine all lights are one type. Then it compares color and light: both are in the same rank of visual things, but color is a genus of colors, while light is not a genus due to difference within it; light is mistakenly thought to be a type because its types are close, but that mistake only occurs for groups like whiteness due to closeness. Accordingly, light is like whiteness: neither a type for what is under it nor a genus, but an accident. The basis (mabna) of all this is the established principle: gradation (tashkik) is only accidental because difference cannot occur in the essence (mahiyya) and its essentials (dhatiyyat). The reasoning: if the factor of difference exists in the more intense but not the weaker, and it is not part of the essence, then there is no difference in the essence itself—it is the same in all; if it is part of the essence, then the weaker does not share in that essence, because part of the essence is missing. For example, the specificity that makes sunlight different from moonlight: if that specificity were essential to light, then moonlight would not be light; if not, then the difference between the two lights is not in the essence of light itself. Hence, gradation is only accidental.

Key Terms
تشكيك (tashkik)
نوع (naw`)
جنس (jins)
عارض (`arad)
ماهية (mahiyya)
اشتباه (ishbah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General categories like 'whiteness' or 'heat' are true species.
Correction: The verified view holds that true species are the specific equal individuals, not the general category.

Checkpoint
According to the verified view, what is the true species of visual things?
A) Absolute light
B) The specific light whose individuals do not differ, like sunlight
C) All lights collectively
D) The accident of light

Key Takeaway
The verified view holds that true species are specific individuals without internal difference, not broad categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often settle for a general understanding of Islamic concepts—like the apparent view of species. Where in your own faith have you been satisfied with a vague idea, avoiding the precise, verified reality that Taftazani demands? Will you commit to digging into one such concept today?
?
This lesson shows that true species are uniform individuals, not graded categories. Think of an area in your life where you've blurred distinctions—like treating all forms of patience as the same. How can you now seek the specific, uniform core that defines true virtue in that area, and what will you do to act on it?
?
Taftazani's verified view requires recognizing gradation (tashkik) yet still identifying the uniform essence. When have you confused something that comes in degrees (like your iman) with something that should be constant? What specific step can you take today to anchor your belief in the ungraded, certain truth rather than fluctuating feelings?
